A Liberal Discount to the Trade.
Paperknife: * The fashionable Melh. Dante Society is
getting a rather large concern, but somewhat too muoh
like the Yorick Club, where the Yoriekism has become
small by degrees and beautifully less, since the days, or
rather nights (suppers of the gods), when Charley Bright
waved the silver ladle, and punch was punoh, not goose-
gog. Now lam a Dantite. Genuine. A series of years
ago I was sent to report a lecture on Dante, at the
Wyselaskie Hall, Ormond College, by Professor Harper.
“ What mullock 1 ” was my reflection, not on Harper, but
on Dante, as I came out in the middle. The Inferno
seemed to me tommy-rotty Christmas pantomime. But
gradually Dante clawed round me. I have broken the
back of him in the Italian. The Paradiso raptures me;
tlie Purgatorio genially delights ; the Infernoanvazes, and
still somewhat amuses. I can sympathise with the im-
mature minds' at the Melb. Dante Sooieta. The other
night they guyed a series of dissolving views, fromDor£’a
Inferno.*
“ALL WORK AND NO PLAY
. . . MAKES JACK A PULL BOy.”
It wouldn’t matter so much if you would limit your business to fixed business-hours ; but you are
always at it—day and night, Sundays and holidays, you are worrying over your business, and the
inevitable consequence is—break down. Be advised in time health is the greatest wealth ; eat
more ; drink less ; when business is over, think of lighter things ; and, above all,
Srpoke Cigarettes.
THE RED PAGE .
From a correspondent)—
... I think the typical Australian is degenerating
trom the physical standard of his British forbears. Our
two chief functions, as a genus, are nutrition and repro-
duction ; and he nourishes less well, reproduces less well
(yes, I say this despite the high Australian birth-rate—
whioh may be accounted for by peculiar environment—
which is in great part not the birth-rate of the typioal
Australian, but of his alien parents—and which is being
.owered as the typical Australian becomes proportion-
ately more numerous).
Look at the typioal British * new-chum ’ —especially the
steerage passenger. How is he known, even to the
Street-boys ? By his florid color, which means plenty of
rich blood ; often by his big frame, which means strength
and energy ; often by his fat head— i.e., by his big skull
and big jaw—associates of‘common sense’ and ‘push.’
We, on the oontrary, are a sallow, small-jawed, brittle-
boned race—and our children ?
Of oourse, I am speaking generally, giving you the
result of many years' travel and observation in Australia.
Take my own case: I consider myself a typical Austra-
iian, perhaps a little better endowed in brains and body
than the average. Well, lam a cleverer man than my
father: I beat him regularly at chess and am a
muoh better whist-player. I speak better, write better,
have more knowledge of books and ideas, and
more intelligence to weigh and oriricise them. But my
father is a stronger man than I; he can endure a greater
physical strain ; I am sure he will live longer, bar acci-
dents. (Compare on this point an interesting 1 Bulletin '
paragraph of not long ago relating how the swimming
family of the Cavills had • fined down,’ so that the English
father was the best man of the lot, and the sons had
(mailer chests and slighter limbs in ratio to their age when
reaching Australia.) . . .
Thus I find the typical Australian distinctly weaker in
Vitality, in manhood and womanhood, than his typical
British progenitor. At the same time he is smarter ; his
intellect is keener; but it is a nervous, excitable, spas-
modical smartness, lacking persistency and thoroughness,
flashing brightly rather than illuminating steadily. . . #
* * * •
And so on, through a number of interesting
imj sessions, whioh our correspondent avows
cannot, or cannot yet, be anchored to statistical
fact. But, transferring his conclusions from
life to literature, they do not go unsupported.
It is the writer’s experience that young con-
tributors to our literary journals who may
be taken as typical Australians prefer to
express themselves in verse rather than in prose
—and verse is the weak feminine of prose.
The body of Australian literature in verse is
probably superior both in mass and quality, con-
sidered as verse, to the body of Australian litera-
ture in prose, considered as prose. This affirmation,
indeed, is not capable ol satisfactory proof : it
would be safer to say that our verse-literature, so
far, tends to be superior to our prose-literature.
The familiar cavils regarding birth-place and re-
presentative character come to complicate com-
parison. But some result can l>e reached by
eliminating doubtful factors. Suppose we pair
Bruuton Stephens with Charies Kingsley, as
possible exotics; and Gordon with Browne;—
taking the best-known names. Clarke may balance
Kendall, for, though Kendall was born in Aus-
tralia, Clarke’s work is at least as intimately
Australian. Then vve come to the younger ones—
less well equipiped mentally, maybe, Imt some of
them with full as inuoh ol the bardic fervor.
Lawson scores against himself: some of his prose
seems better, as prose, than any of his yerse, as
verse; but one might hold that his total verse-
performance exceeds iu value his total prose-per-
formance. But Paterson and Boake are. almost
wholly known by their verse ; Daley is essentially
a poet—with a splendid achievement, yet less
splendid because he has hail to write prose
for a livelihood; .1. s . O liara and Jennings
Carmichael can occasionally charm in verse,
but their prose has not lieen thought worth
collecting. On the side of the prosers Becke
and Montgomery may lie set. But Mont-
gomery is alien ; and Becke cannot outweigh the
swarm of young versi-rs whose names recur in a
throng tovi numerous to mention. Of the women
writers it may be said, genenfily, that their verse
has more charm than tlveir prose has power. ...
And, the conclusion, after this haphazard survey,
is still doubtful. Apparently one may keep the
impression that young Australia runs to verse,
but there is no very distinct evidenoe to warrant
the impression.
• » • •
Macaulay argues, in an interesting passage, that
poetry is essentially the work of a youthful
nation, so that the bards of elder races will
never reach the imaginative height of Homer.
But he has forgotten that man is a microcosm ;
that though the race may wax old, the individual
is re-born continually—in an altered environment
indeed, but with radically the same impulses and
emotions as his predecessors ; and that, if it be not
possible for a bard of to-day to feel the same kind
of stimulus as the bard of 2000 years ago, he may
at least be stimulated to the same degree . and his
poetry may have equal potency. In arguing to
the race instead of to the individual Macaulay
argued to the wrong standard. There is no doubt
that poetry is essentially the work of a youthful
individual—simply because age dulls the senses
and sensations, and the kernel of lyric force is
keen feeling. But (and the gist of the matter
lies here) it is essentially the work of a nervous,
excitable individual . . . and this is where the
typical Australian constitution comes in, if the
typical Australian constitution really be (or be
becoming) excitable, nervous, comparatively weak
physically, very precocious intellectually (by a
natural correlation). Are we really ‘ good for a
spurt, but bad for a strain ’ in literature and life ?
• • • »
Here are three books of Australian verse need-
ing notice; and it_ may be remarked (as bearing
on the preliminaries) how frequently good local
verse comes for notice in these and other critical
columns, and how rarely good local prose. Occa-
sionally there come stories and sketches, short
or longbut where are our histories, our
biographies, our science, our philosophy ? Cer-
tainly Gibbons and Spencers are not born every
century—and this is a young country—and the
typical Australian has hardly arrived ; but is the
outlook promising ?
* * * *
G. W. L. Marshall-Hall has now three verse-
booklets to his credit—the last * A Book of Can-
ticles.’ He is sometimes hysterical, sometimes
fantastic, sometimes interesting, rarely or never
forcible. His feminine preponderance is well-
marked, in fact—which accounts for his recently-
rhymed admiration of Streeton ; for Streeton is a
big, dominating fellow of an artist, with plenty
of force to attract feeble-pulsed followers. ‘ A
Book of Canticles ’ shows the fluent woman’s
faculty for weaving phrases, and the fluent
woman’s fibreless flatness. Occasionally there
comes an attractive line or image;—and, for tast-
ing, this is the best of the most attractive * Can-
ticle ’ —
ON MICHELAGNOLO'S MASK OF MARY.
O lovely face—thou dream of love’s desire—
Just instinct with warm life’s voluptuous spring 1 —
Droops* thou those perfect lips to hide the fire
Within thy languorous eyes slow-ripening?
What half-born fanoy pouts upon those lips ?
Lips, O ! than sweetest fruit more sweet to crush !
How tenderly the chin’s full roundness dips
Into the round slim neck I—She moves!—O hush! . .
» * « *
Arthur A. D. Bayldon’s verses, some of them
reprinted from ‘ The Bulletin,’ have a curious in-
terest of craftsmanship. He is no poet, for he
has no lyrical faculty—if he has emotion he can-
not communicate it ; lie is no thinker—his ideas
are all commonplace or worse ; but he has a keen
eye for a good picture and a good phrase (little
ear, or instinct), and by dint of accumulating
purely objective impressions he occasionally pro-
duces an intellectual effect which almost raises
thought to the pitch of feeling. Naturally, there-
fore, he succeeds best in sonnets, which are a kind
of elderly, philosophical form of verse, where
phrase and thought may take the place of passion—
usually better served in a freer medium. (Shakes-
peare’s sonnets were probably composed during his
30 40 decade, when his emotions had lost their
first poignancy, and his intellect had gained
full ascendancy). Bayldon, as affirmed, has no
original thought; but he has impressions (his own
or others’), and with his phrases he paints admir-
able sonnet-pictures.* This, of Marlowe, does not
now look so flawless as it looked in a four-years-
ago ‘ Bulletin ’ to a younger critic ; but it is very
fine.
The last line reminds one of somebody’s * Saul
at Gil boa ’ —
... fled
A hireling dabbled from the dreadful deed—
a fine picture-hne. Occasionally Bayldon is
eehoish—of Poe, Emerson, who not: are the im-
pressions of the sonnet on ‘ Maurice de Guerin ’
won at first-hand, or borrowed from Arnold’s
essay ?
Here the well-sustained images—a series of arbi-
trary, external pictures—have the force of thought,
because applied to a man whose known charac-
teristics enforce them ; and the result is a fine
sonnet. Take away the title, and there is left
only an empty catalogue. It is an interesting ex-
ample of the writer’s method; and here is an-
other. -v
Now, that is pure Bayldon. You see, there is
no thought in it: he has nothing to say ; there is
no value in the mere affirmation that Satan made
a journey. But reading Bayldon’s verses is like
studying a chess-problem—you find the key-move
and straightway all’s clear. Now the key-move
in ‘ Satan Alert ’ is the very fine image italicised.
Perhaps it came to Bayldon through Milton, with
the reflection that a devil covering ‘many a rood ’
of Heaven ought to make a perceptible draught
when he went through space at celestial speed ;
perhaps he saw the stars quivering one night
through differently-refracting layers of atmo-
sphere, and, easting about for a cause, hit upon
the transit of Satan; —the Lord and Bayldon
know exactly how it was. Anyhow, there was
the image, and one would wager that was first
set down on paper and the preliminary lines were
written up to it. A fragment on ‘Sunset’ has
two splendid images, but Bayldon has not bothered
padding them to sonnet length because it is un-
necessary to ‘show cause ’ —they are self-explana-
tory.
SUNSET.
The weary wind is slumbering on the wing.
Leaping from out weak twilight’s purpling blue
Burns the proud star of eve as though it knew
It was the big king jewel quivering
On the black turban of advancing night.
In the dim west the soldiers of the sun
Strike all their royal colors one by one,
Reluctantly surrender every height.
* • * •
One does not remember anywhere to have met
such an exclusively intellectual relish for a fine
eye-filling phrase. Yet mostly, as shown, each is
a magnificent cul-de-sac, leading poetically no-
where. (The ease of an artist in ecstasy over
formless splashes of color is analogous.) It is a
pity ; for Bayldon’s book has many good lines,
and when he can lean on the reader for a pre-
liminary idea they make uncommonly good verses.
* * * *
Chris: Brennan is a verser in yet another
category. Bayldon wants a dash of the woman—
to give him laughter and tears, and flushes of
tenderness, and floods of passion ; and he wants
more vigor —his brain seems tired, anaemic, work-
ing slowly and laboriously. And Marshall-Hall,
as before stated, has plenty of woman and a good
deal of vigor, so Arthur W. L. Bayldon-Hal-1, if
we could only mix him, would probably marry the
Muse in immortal earnest. Brennan can woo
alone, but he woos very tardily his ‘XXI
Poems ’ (Angus and Robertson) are represented
as four years’ output, which means an average
of less than half-a-poem a month. And the
afflatus-years slip away so quickly ! Of course, it
is in verse, if anywhere, that mere quality is
better than mere quantity, but quantity of quality
is the final desideratum.
• • • •
And it is only at the end of his four years’
course that Brennan is beginning to do memor-
able work. At the outset he is lost in misty ideas
and whirling verbiage, through whioh by degrees
he gropes his way to lucidity and force—one can
see both his mind and his style strengthening as
he goes. (Even yet Venus has not quite risen
from the foam of words.) And at last he reaches
a fine poetical performance with a rare poetical
promise, as in ‘Romance’ —
Of old, on her terrace at evening
. . not here . . in some long-gone kingdom
O, folded close to her breast! . .
—our gaze dwelt wide on the blackness
(was it trees ? or a shadowy passion
the pain of an old-world longing
that it sobb'd, that it swell’d, that it shrank T)
—the gloom of the forest
blnrr’d soft on the skirt of the night-skies
that shut in our lonely world.
. . not here . . in some long-gone world . .
close-lock’d in that passionate arm-clasp
no word did we utter, we stirr’d not:
the silence of Death, or of hove . .
only, round and over us
that tearless infinite yearning
and the Night with her spread wings rustling
folding us with the stars.
. . not here . , in some long-gone kingdom
of old on her terrace at evening
O, folded close to her heart! . .
* * » •
This has what is called in critics’ jargon ‘ the
lyrical cry’—a bird not common in any latitude,
but most often met where names like Brennan, or
Quinn, or O’Reilly, cluster round the parallels.
And there are two or three other poetical piece*
in Brennan’s booklet which have the same feeling,
though it is not tuned to quite the same pitch.
The most powerful piece is ‘ Cities ’ : whioh waB-
- in ‘The Bulletin
Doubtless this is already enshrined in the scrap
books of the illuminati. There are not many
things in life better than, being a writer, to happen
across some one to whom your lone-forgotten
emotion has been translated through the magic of
print; some one who has had that emotion by sim
for years, and has lived with it and mingled with
it, and tenderly regarded your version of it at
times of birth, or marriage, or burial —at the
crises of joy or grief, or when se felt good or wanted
to feel good, or when the spring-change was thril-
ling ‘ this machine.’ Someone sent in the other day
an old scrap-book compiled mainly from Parkes’s
‘ Empire ’ in its lusty days, and treasured through
the years since. And upon certain pages where
verses nestle over the name of A. J. Evelyn,
there are stains probably of time, yet not impos-
sibly of tears —for the forgotten Evelyn was worth
a tear. A poet wants no better immortality for
his verses than tear-stains in an old scrap-book.
•The difference between thought and impression, as
here understood, is that thought is dynamic and impres-
sion merely static. Thinking is like grinding corn into
flour—whioh means motion, labour, foice used and con-
verted ; but you can receive and return impressions
almost as passively as a mirror receives and returns
images. Thoughts deal with things in themselves; and
impressions with the look of things—their outsides,
externals.
MARLOWE.
Vith Eastern banners flaunting in the breeze
doyal processions, sounding fife and gong
And showering jewels o’er the jostling throng
March to the tramp of Marlowe's harmonies.
He drained life’s brimming goblet to the lees.
He recked not that a compeer calm and strong
Would tune great notes to his impassioned song
And top his cannonading lines with ease.
To the wild clash of cymbals we behold
The tragic ending of his youthful life:
The revelry of kisses bought with gold ;
The jealous rival and the perilous strife ;
A harlot weeping o’er a corpse scarce cold ;
A scullion fleeing with a bloody knife.
MAURICE DE GUERIN.
The embodied soul of lightnings, winds, mid seas ;
A peal of bells archangels loved to ring ;
A garrulous Greek centaur that did sing
In modern woods and valleys at his ease ;
A bower of fancies murmurous as bees;
An aviary of all sounds of spring ;
A magic furnace wherein everything
Did kindle into golden melodies ;
A wave of frenzy fastened and confined :
A sparkling bubble on life’s turbid tide;
A dream of spring fresh from the Eternal Mind ;
A mosque where soirg was orowned and deified ;
A tube blown through by an Olympian wind ;
An angel bird that warbled, loved, and died.
SATAN ALERT.
Wild rumors of divinities o’erthrown
By dwarfs that nothing knew and yet were wise,
Their home ap earth-speck somewhere in the skies,
Roused into life the arch-fiend on his throne
Where wrapped in baffling darkness, proud, alone
With fiery thoughts all manshalled in his eyes,
He pondered o’er a mighty enterprise
To wrestle back those glories once his own.
He spreads his vasty wings and enters space,
And steers his way through systems without end
That travelling flash and thunder, singe his face
And ponderous bulk and shoulders that extend
O’er vaulted leagues—a whirlwind as he passed
Tossed all the stars like leaves tossed in a blast.
The yellow gas is flred from street to street
past rows of heartless homes and hearths unlit
dead churches, and the unending pavement beat
by crowds—say rather haggard shades that flit
round nightly haunts of their delusive dream
where’er our paradisal instinct starves—
till on the utmost post, its sinuous gleam
orawls in the oily water of the wharves,
where Homer’s sea loses his keen breath, hemm’d
what place rebellious piles were driven down—
the priestlike waters to this task condemn’d
to wash the roots of the inhuman town !
where fat and strange eyed fish that never saw
the outer deep, broad halls of sapphire light,
glut in the city’s draught each nameless maw
—and there, wide-eyed unto the soulless night
methinks a drown’d maid’s face might fitly show
what we have slain, a life that had been free,
clean, large, nor thus tormented—even so
as are the skies, the great winds and the sea.
Ay, we had saved our days and kept them whole
to whom no part in our old joy remains,
had felt those bright winds sweeping thro’ our soul
and all the keen sea tumbling in our veins,
had thrill’d to harps of sunrise, when the height
whitens and dawn dissolves in virgin tears,
or caught across the hush’d ambrosial night
the choral music of the swinging spheres
or drunk the silence if nought else— But not
and from each rolling soul distil in dreams
a poison o’er the old earth creeping slow
that kills the flowers and curdles the live streams,
that taints the fresh breath of re-risen day
and reeks across the pale bewilder’d moon
—shall we be cleans’d and how ? I only pray
red flame or deluge, may that end be soon !

[For The Bulletin.]
THE OLD MUNDOORAN ROAD.
(After, and with apologies to, Patterdylaw and
Son.)
There are few to take the places of the many gone
below,
Since Lucretia kept the “ grog-shop on the old
Mundooran road ;
Many partings—few reunions—since the days of
long ago: _ ,
Times are changed. Lucretia’s well, she s
changed—her name and her abode.
From the four points of the compass, spots that
scarcely bear a name,
Little Hells or Heavens, damned or dear, but
far beyond our reach,
Storm-blown derelicts of Fortune on Life’s back-
water we came,
To be anchored at Lucretia’s, or careened upon
the beach.
Any tint but black or yellow; there we drew the
color-line. -
But in other things we none of us were squeamish
to a shade.
Most of us were old scow-bankers, and our motto
“ Mine is thine, ”
Or it might be “ Thine is mine.” What odds,
so long as “ she ” was paid ?
’Twas a harbor snug and landlocked, was that
“ pub.” Lucretia kept,
Where you dropped your bower anchor (if you
had one) in the sand ; _
Light in ballast, pooped and leaky, with a jury-
jigger stepped,
After knocking round the Horn, two hundred
days from land to land.
No one asked you where you hailed from ; no one,
cared where you were bound.
No one felt like one too many ; share alike for
great and small;
There was always plenty liquor and tobacco going
round ’
For a busted privateersman ; and Lucretia
bossed us all!
All ! the fuming “ free-and-eas;es ” on a keg of
“ Queensland tea ! ”
And the half-forgot ten “chanties,” trolled to
many a clinking can
Till the dawn peeped thro’ the knot-holes and the
magpies joined the glee
Mixing “Mother’s with the Angels” with the
classic “ Hog-eyed Man ! ”
But they’ve mostly knuckled under to the “ great
Australian thirst,”
Walker, Hennessy and Usher; and they 11 lie
no more to load
At the old familiar moorings, for the best are as
the worst, ■' •
And Lucretia’s left the shanty on the Old Mun-
dooran-road !
NIL.
THE CHARCOAL BURNER.
When the bush is cleared and the conquering
town
A march to the West has made,
And the forest is pierced by the roads laid down
Out under the gum-trees’ shade ;
Then the narrow mounds where the scrub grows
rank
And the twisted vines strike root,
Point the place where a forest giant sank
With the Burner’s axe at his foot.
For the sods were piled on his prostrate bole
And the biting flame applied,
And for days he burned while the hot sweat stole
And steamed through his rugged hide.
Then his bones were sold and the mound was
cleft,
And glowed with a dull red glow,
And the giant’s cremated flesh they left
A bed where the brambles grow.
'Twas a gay, free life when the land was young
And the gums were many and stout;
And despite all that’s been said or sung,
To the Burner the best life out;
For he went as the clock-bird chimed for day,
And the east showed the dawn’s red prints,
Through the bush where the dewstarred cobwebs
lay
In a thousand rainbow tints.
When came the December sun’s white heat
In a wave o’er the forest trees,
Then the shaded earth was a cool retreat
Where the Burner smoked at his ease,
Or lay in a long luxurious sleep—
For smoothly ran his life,
Like rivers that slowly through forests cieep
Unstirr’d by the storm’s rude strife.
When the red-gold flush left the western hill,
And the purple skies turned grey—
At the hour when the bush the shadows .All-
Then the Burner went his way
To the slab-built home where he slept, and
dreamed
Of gums that were miles in girth,
Of a giant axe that flashed and gleamed
And rang through the whole wide earth. .
But deep in the earth was a charcoal bed
That Nature had hidden there—
The remains of myriad forests dead—
And the miner laid it bare ;
And when from the earth’s exhaustless store
The thousands of tons were cast,
The axe of the burner rang no more—
His trade is a thing of the past.
ULOOLA.
A SURMISE.
When some sharp shock of fear or grief,
A spasm of sickness or of pain
O’ertakes us, Nature sends the boon
Of blest nepenthe and relief ;
She lays soft hand on heart and brain,
Our senses swim, we faint, we swoon ;
We swoon, and in a blank complete
We find surcease of pain and care.
What pleasing comfort here wo see 1
May not an age-long swoon be sweet?
If Death’s ambassador be fair,
Shall not the King all-radiant be ?
J. LIDDELL KELLY.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 28, 1897.
The Laucknow Strike.
Ben Tillett, the English Labor agitator,
lately arrived in New South Wales to preach,
among other things, the old trades-unionism
to a benighted Cornstalk population. And
almost at the moment of his coming the
Lucknow gold-miners’ strike arose to put the
old unionism to the test for the many dozenth
time, and probably to demonsti’ate once more
that it is not altogether the thing it professes
to be. The old unionism has tried con-
clusions with Capital many times in Aus-
tralia, and on every important occasion
during recent years it has gone under. For
a very long time past it has not placed one
victory of any importance to its credit. The
great Broken Hill strike ended in lizzie and
disaster, and grievously reduced wages, and
hard slavery, and the gaoling of the strike
leaders, and every kind of failure. The
Newcastle coal strike ended in the most
abject collapse. The great Queensland
shearers’ strike appeared, at one time, as if
it was going to end in revolution. It looked
as if all the elements for an insurrection
of the good old Peruvian or Columbian type
were there ; but there was no warlike filibus-
ter to lead them, and the men were not of
the kind who make a day of barricades ; the
struggle was fought out on strict unionist
lines, and it ended in lower wages
and long terms of gaol for the chiefs
of the movement. The famous maritime
strike—about the biggest of its kind in this
country—went the way of all the rest. And
the main reason why unionism is at a com-
paratively low ebb in Australia just now is
that all these and many other failures ex-
hausted the funds and the confidence of the
unionists, and half-convinced them that the
old weapons are practically obsolete, like
the bow and arrow, and the club and other
things of the past.
* * * *
The Lucknow miners have every sem-
blance of justice on their side. The mining
companies are doing well, and no necessity
drives them to a reduction of wages. The
rate of pay is already low, and the proposal
to make it lower—to bring it down below
the minimum figure hitherto paid on the
cheapest goldfield on this continent—is mere
insolent greed and rapacity. But the fact
that the miners have a good cause doesn’t
improve their prospects in the least. The
strike opens with the usual enthusiastic
assurances that the men must win if they
only have patience—the same assurances
which have preceded every failure up to
date. All the evidences are that the men
must lose. The funds are low to begin with,
and the subscriptions front other districts are
a precarious source of revenue, because the
subscribers are poor and none too numerous.
Also, through past experience, they are
none too hopeful, and can hardly but feel
that they are subscribing to keep up a
battle that is already lost. While the miners
lose their source of income by waiting, the
shareholders lose comparatively nothing.
The gold in the ground does not deteriorate
by lying there a few mouths longer; the
price of it does not depreciate ; there is no
risk of losing a market—gold is the one
thing for which there is always a market ;
and, if there is a delay of three months now,
it only means that the mine will last three
months longer in the end. Therefore, if it
was impossible to get other miners, and the
matter was really a trial of patience between
the companies and their employees, the com-
panies have all the advantage. So long as
enough men can be obtained—as they have
been in this instance—to keep things in
repair (no labor conditions apparently enter
into the case at Lucknow), a rich gold-
mining co. can afford to wait more patiently
than almost any other joint-stock enterprise
on earth.
* * * *
Then there is a further consideration—the
places of the miners undoubtedly will be
filled. Even in the biggest strike that ever
took place in Australia enough men, of some
sort or other, were found to fill the places of
the strikers after a fashion. Therefore it is
hopeless to suppose that enough men
can't be found to replace the mere
handful who are out on strike at Luck-
now. There are abundance of unemployed
miners in these provinces to fill their places
several times over. The strikers can offer
these men no inducement not to accept the
work and wages that are waiting for them,
except the inward glow that comes of a con-
sciousness of well-doing, and Australia is so
poor and hungry and wretched nowadays,
and work is so scarce, and workers so many,
that mere inward glow is at a discount.
Even if the strike pay does hold out inde-
finitely, which is more than doubtful, it is
only one remove from starvation at the best.
The law is already covertly taking sides with
Capital, as it always does, by increasing the
punishment for petty assaults about sixfold,
it being the regular practice of the law to
regard solitary acts of “ intimidation ” by a
unionist on a non-unionist as hideous crimes,
while constant acts of intimidation by a lar-
rikin push upon a whole neighborhood are
hardly regarded as any offence at all. By-and-
bye, in all probability, the law will take sides
with Capital more openly. Amid all these
and many other disadvantages the cause of
the Lucknow strikers is practically hopeless—
if the companies consider the miserable little
grab, which is the sole cause of the quarrel,
to be worth fighting for. There is no pride
of prophecy about this utterance ; the situa-
tion is so obvious that everybody, even the
strikers themselves, must be aware of it.
They have justice with them and the big
battalions against them, and in such cases
the exact value of justice is about one straw
—a little more or less.
♦ * * *
Trades-unionism pure and simple has no
weapon but the strike. It is a good weapon
of a kind ; Capital almost always wins, but
in many cases the victory costs more than it
is worth, therefore trades-unionism pre-
vents a multitude of small tyrannies. But
where the robbery proposed is large
enough to cover the expense of robbing,
Labor nearly always is robbed ; therefore
trades-unionism is a miserably defective
weapon at the best. Probably the best thing
about it is that it is a means towards the
acquisition of political power. The chief
lesson of the Lucknow strike, likewise of the
Broken Hill strike and many others, is that
a statutory minimum wage is badly wanted
in New South Wales, as well as many other
items of labor legislation. They can’t be
secured by strikes, or by trades-unionism
alone, or by any means outside Parliament.
And it begins to be an open question if they
can be secured inside Parliament by the
Labor-party as at present constituted. It
is very rarely indeed that a mere third party
secures anything that is much worth having.
* * ♦ •
What New South Wales badly needs is to
drop its Labor-party and the uncouth desig-
nation thereof into oblivion and start a
Democratic-party in its stead.' The mere
Labor vote that is to say, the horny-handed,
jiick-and-shovel vote —is apparently not
-strong enough to command an absolute
majority in the Legislature, yet the name of
the party is specially calculated to warn off
everything but the pick and-shovcl vote. A
Democratic party, which invited into its
ranks everybody who would subscribe to a
Liberal platform, might command enough
support in addition to the pick-and-shovel
vote to raise a majority in the Assembly and
form a Government of its own. Also, it
might command enough ability, which the
present Labor-party, in some respects at
least, does not. The party, so far as appears
at present, has no financier whom the coun-
try is likely to trust with command of the
Treasury, and no legal member who is fit to
fill the Attorney-Generalship, and while it
remains so distinctly a pick-and-shovel party
it is not likely to have either. This is a
second reason why it has hardly any prospect
of getting the Government into its own
hands—the want of numbers being a first.
A third reason is the want of a complete
policy. It has no distinct fiscal belief, no
clear or definite financial policy, and though
it has a very definite and respectable pro-
gramme of social reforms the country will
never put it into power until it knows more
accurately what will happen to trade and
industry when it gets there The present
Labor-party, in fact, seems only to aim at
being a third party, and therein lies its weak-
ness. As a third party it can only urge Reid
and Brunker and M l Mill an, or else Lyne
and Copeland, and Nicholas Willis to do
things for it, and neither Reid, nor Brunker,
nor M‘Millan, nor Lyne, nor Copeland,
nor Nicholas Willis has any real sympathy
with it, or will do anything more for it than
can be helped, and what is done will be done
as badly and reluctantly as possible. The
only real hope for the Labor-party is to get
a better name and a wider programme, and
gather in more adherents, and get into a
position to do things for itself.
* * * *
This prospect may seem a long way off, but
the hope of achieving prosperity and indus-
trial freedom by the old device of trades-
unions and strikes alone is even more remote.
Yet The Bulletin is an enthusiastic advocate
of trades-unionism. Without it, wages and
labor-conditions would take the shortest-
possible cut to Tophet. With it, if the
workers rely on it alone, they will probably
get there also in the end, but the progress is
vastly slower. Trades-unionism in the recent
bad years has proved itself powerless to make
the condition of the worker any better, but
it has done an immense amount, merely by
making victory for the capitalist a costly affair,
to prevent his condition becoming anything
like so much worse as it w'ould otherwise
have been. Yet the fact remains that, in
every important battle for years past. Labor
has either gained nothing or has lost some-
thing. The fact also remains that its party
in Parliament is losing rather than gaining
in strength and repute ; that it is hardly
likely to do anything else while it remains a
mere third party ; and that while it- professes
to be only a Labor institution with a Labor
policy, instead of a National body with a
policy for all classes, it is almost certain to
remain a third party and nothing more.
These, however, are mere generalities, and
as there are already too many generalities
and too much mere poetic whoop and too
few business-like details in politics, The
Bulletin will endeavor to explain at an
early date exactly how it thinks the reorgan-
isation of the New South Wales Labor-party
might be attempted.
More Tote
The “ tote ” question, which has agitated the
pious people and the aggregate Ikey Mo of
New youth Wales for years, is again raised by
the Bill of which Sleath, M.L.A., is parent.
So far as things have gone, the chances of
Sleath’s measure seem to be infinitesimal. It
is a crude and unworkable affair at the best,
and partly on this ground, and partly with a
view of having it peacefully talked to death on
general principles, the Assembly passed it over
to a select committee. And the select commit-
tee will probably lose it in a corridor, or down
a sink, or let it accidentally blow away. In
many respects Sleath’s well-meaning little
Bill deserves no better fate. It is half-hearted
and apologetic, and its general tenor is to pro-
vide that the totalisator may be used under
close restrictions, on racecourses only, by a cer-
tain number of clubs, provided these clubs don’t
use it too often, and provided they give a cer-
tain amount in stakes, and on various other
conditions. The machine is to retain 10 per
cent, of the stakes, and of this the Government
is to get 3 per cent, (it certainly should be five),
the racing clubs receiving the balance. The
Government’s 3 per cent, is not to go into the
consolidated revenue, but the unclean thing is
to be put away surreptitiously into a separate
fund, and disinfected, and used for purposes of
charity. These are the main features of the
measure, and what they principally suggest is
that it sadly lacks the courage of its opinions.
* * * •
The proviso that the Government’s share of
the ill-gotten loot is to be set aside for the pur-
poses of charity, appears in almost every scheme
for legalising the hated instrument, and in al-
most every argument advanced in its favour.
It is obviously intended as an apology—an
acknowledgement that the money is unclean
cash from an unclean source, and requires to be
sanctified by the pious but misused name of
benevolence ; it is an offering up of incense to
snuffle, and a bowing of the knee in the house
of Podsnap. Yet the incense and the abase-
ment in the fane of Podsnap are quite uncalled
for. There is no need of apology, and no
reason why the three per cent. ' should be
specially set aside for purposes of charity. It is
quite as honest and quite as reputable as any
other source of income, and the Consolidated
Revenue may take it without any fear of con
tamination. The chief means by which that
revenue is raised are taxes on drink and
drunkenness ; the proceeds of the sale of lands
stolen from the aboriginal ; taxes on ditto;
taxes on incomes, including the incomes of the
bookmaker, gambler, drink-vendor and persons
of equally disreputable pursuits; lines levied
on account of drunkenness, perjury, brutal
assault, obscene language, theft, and the like—
the State actually makes a revenue out of these
things ; also opium and morphia duties,
and things equally horrible. There is hardly
die source of revenue which is any cleaner, and
very few that are so clean, as the totalisalot
percentage would be, therefore the attempt to
apologise for the instrument by devoting the
Stale’s share of the proceeds to charity is quite
unnecessary. The Treasurer who takes 6d. in
the £, income-tax oil the gambling revenue of
Isaac Mo, bookmaker, and has lio qualms of
conscience, is a very dull-witted hypocrite if he
expects the World to believe that his conscience
won’t allow him to take in the £ on the
gambling income of the machine Which is only
Isaac Mo in an inanimate form without the
jewels and the whisky-breath and the blas-
phemy. A Treasurer, in fact, considering
where his money mostly comes from, has no
right to possess a conscience. It is even an
open question if he should have a soul.
* * * *
In short, it is a sufficiently good reason for
making revenue out of the totalisator that the
Treasury is desperatel}’ hard-up, that the
deficit has become chronic, and that-New South
Wales wants at least another £1,500,000 per
annum of revenue to make ends meet honestly.
And, as a side-issue, there is the probability that
the Government is wholly damned already-
through living on drink and opium revenue,
and the price of stolen lands, and on the
income-tax percentage upon the proceeds of
drink, gambling, fra,ud, and miscellaneous
crime, and on the price (collected in the law-
courts) of outrage, wife-beating, cruelty to
animals, and all manner of horrors. And, as it
can’t well be damned any worse, it, might as
well take the tote revenue and make the best
of it.
* <: * *
Another weak point is the proviso, which ap-
pears in almost all schemes for the legislation
of the tote, that it shall be used only so many
times a year by so many clubs, on racecourses
only, and only on the courses of clubs which
gave so many hundred pounds a day in prize-
money. These are more needless apologies for
the instrument. Moreover, they are wholly
illogical, according to the practice of the State
in other matters. The law' doesn’t enact that
drink—which is always quoted as the curse
that is twin to gambling—shall be imported
only upon so many days a year, by so many
people ; that il shall be drunk only in certain
places and on certain specific occasions ; or that
no one shall drink it unless he gives £4OO a day
in stakes, or anything equally ridiculous. It
simply collects with all possible energy its
share of the proceeds in-customs and excise
duty, and license fees, and in fines levied on
the common drunk. If it considers that
further restriction is needed, its usual course
is to take a larger share of the proceeds.
It furnishes every facility for letting the public
have as much drink as it wants, merely devoting
itself to keeping the traffic as respectable as
possible. In like manner it is justified in sup-
plying the people with facilities for all the
gambling they want by letting the totalisator
do business (provided it is worked by reputable
men and the State’s per-centage, is duly forth-
coming) at every race-meeting, and also in
business centres, apart from racecourses, where
there is sufficient demand. If there is reason
to believe that gambling is cariied to excess,
or is likely to be so, the obvious proceeding is
to increase the tax and incidentally get more
money for the Treasury. If that is not suffi-
cient the next best idea is to increase the tax
again.
* * * *
One argument most enthusiastically urged
against the honest, reliable totalisator which
will yield a revenue to the State is that “ there
is far too much racing in Australia.” The fol-
lowing extract from a speech by Crick, M.L.A.,
is a fair sample of this kind of logic :
Well, the needs of 4,.'500,000 people in the matter of horse-
racing were satisfied by 80 meetings in the year in Lon-
don, as against 60 in four months in and about Sydney.
During the year 1895 there were only a few more race-
meetings in the whole of Great Britain than there were
within the racing district of Sydney. That would be
including the Hawkesbury and taking in Warwick Farm
and the old Liverpool course. Within that limited area
there were nearly as many race-meetings in 1895 asj there
were in the whole of England. Those are some figures
which, I think, would justify this House in saiyng that it
ought to have full information on this matter before deal-
ing with it in the slipshod manner proposed. Hon. mem-
bers know only too well that more gambling is,earried on
in the city of Sydney than in any other city of its size in
the world. Walk down King-street, not on Saturday, a
half-holiday and legitimate racing day, but on any other
day of the week, and you will see a stream of people
passing in and out of these betting-shops. There is .now
and then a spasmodic effort on the part of the police to
close them ; but notwithstanding all that has been done
within the last few weeks, betting and gambling are as
rampant in the city of Sydney to-day as they have been
at any time during the last three years.
The facts of the above statement are right
enough ; the inference drawn from them is
utterly foolish and illogical. The theory of
the opponents of the tote is that, because this
dreadful state of things has arisen under exist-
ing conditions—said conditions consisting of
bookmaker Ikey Mo and his satellites—there-
fore we should stick to the existing conditions.
The idea of quoting the horrors of things as
they are as a reason why these things should
remain as they are, is wildly original, yet that
is exactly what Crick and the other opponents
of the legalised and State-supervised totali-
sator are constantly doing. The weak-kneed
supporters of the machine are not much more
logical. Their theory is that, under the Ikey
Mo regime, there are far too many race-meet-
ings and far too many illicit gambling-shops.
So they propose to introduce an honest machine
which is calculated to supersede Ikey Mo—hut
to introduce it under such restrictions that it
will apply to about one race-meeting out of
every four, or less. And, as for the other three,
they propose to leave them under the same
bad conditions as at present so as to make
things better. In other words, they also agree,
though with certain limitations, that because
the Ikey Mo regime has produced the worst
kind of results, therefore Ikey Mo should be
left alone, to go on producing the worst kind of
results,
it incic ii*u tuo many race-meetings, as
Crick and others have stated, the short and
simple way to make them less is for
the Government to license race - courses
and racing-clubs under strict conditions, to
limit the number of clubs and the number of
meetings, to require that a certain amount be
given in prize-money on each day of every


meeting, and to shut down remorselessly on
pverv club which isn’t up to sample. As,
however, the opponents of the totalisator, who
mlain so bitterly about race-meetings being
so numerous, never suggest that anything
. 1,1 K e done to reduce their number, 1 hi.
Sdllbtis takes the liberty to assume that they
Jon’t want them reduced. The excessive num-
ber of race meetings is never heard of except as
nnrmiraent aganst the totalisator. And then
Hie' only remedy ever suggested is that the
bh ® hods which led to this excess should
T strictly left alone. The Bplt.etin’s
theory is the totalisator should be
remitted at every race-meeting If there
£ re too many of them, the fact that the
ones are held under the aegis of
Sy Mo instead of under that of the machine
WO n’t make them any less ; and if some of them
Tre so disreputable that the machine can t he
trusted there, then they are also so disreputable
that the live-meat bookmaker and the public
shouldn’t be there either. In fact, the argument
fl at the totalisator shouldn’t be legalised be-
cause there are too many race-meetings hasn t
go" a leg-not even the gouty leg of bookmaker
Mo—to stand upon.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Very Peculiar Minister Gould.
When writing last week on the curious pro-
ceedings in the case of Thomas Wield, Ihe
Bulletin was unable to say what had been
their final issue. It could only record that
Suffield had been approached, at the instance
of the verv peculiar Minister Gould, and had
been told that, if he petitioned for release at
the end of the year, his petition would receive
favorable consideration.
That, thereupon, Suffield declined to peti-
tion, on the ground that he was an innocent
man and did not want a mitigation of his
sentence, but an enquiry into its justice or m-
That Suffield was then written to a second
time and informed that, if he liked to change
his mind, his petition would still be favorably
considered.
That Suffield did not reply _
That Mr. Griffith, M. L. A., then interviewed
Suffield and urged him to accept the Depart-
ment’s offer. ... , •
And that Suffield asked time to consider.
That was as far as The Bulletin and the
very peculiar Minister Gould had got last week.
This week The Bulletin announces that, after
consideration, Suffield for the third time re-
futed to petition for release; and thereupon the
very peculiar Minister Gould let the matter
drop—because he had to.
Everybody naturally asks, “ What is there
behind these extraordinary proceedings of an
alleged Department of Justice, when dealing
with an alleged brutal prisoner, who is alleged
to have committed an abominable outrage on a
woman, and who only escaped hanging because
the jury did not believe or did not understand
the evidence, and yet did not wish to offend
the Judge, who believed and did not under-
stand the evidence ? The Bulletin replies that
behind these proceedings is the Botany Bay
maxim— Burlce enquiry ! cover things up! hush
things up! for God’s sake, don’t let the
'public have a chance of learning whether
or not Justice has blundered grievously,
and an innocent man has been sent to eat his
heart out in penal servitude.
That is what lies behind the strong efforts of
the Department of Alleged Justice to get Suf-
field to petition for release, and thereby to
inferentially acknowledge his official status as a
prisoner, to inferentially adm.t that his sen-
tence was more or less just, since he would
abandon his appeal for justice in order to sue
for mercy.
In the Legislative Assembly last week the
very peculiar Minister Gould asserted that lie
still believed in Suffi eld’s guilt—and this in
face of the strenuous efforts of his Department to
get this alleged guilty man to sign a paper which
would give a decent excuse for letting him out
of gaol, and for burking enquiry into liis case !
Minister Gould may still believe in Suffield s
guilt: but that his Department does—c redat
Judaeus!
Let Parliament now say if, in view of these
things, Suffield’s case is to remain where it is.
Let it insist, as a first step, that the papers in
the case be printed at once. I hey are not veiv
numerous ; the printing would not be costly ;
and the evidence in the case would then speak
for itself. So would the persistent malice of
certain police-officers ; so woinl the crass
obstinacy of the Department of alleged Justice ;
so would the appeals of Suffield himself,
wearing his life out behind the bars. And
with these papers, let there be printed
the four sworn affidavits of James W ar-
ren, Lydia Warren, Annie Warren, and the
young mail who was a co-boarder with hut-
field in the Warrens’ house—these affidavits
containing much new evidence ; compiled >y
Andrew Kelly, then M.L.A., four years ago ;
forwarded to the Crown Law Offices, as Ihe
Bulletin is credibly assured, through \\ . 1.
Dent, Mayor of Lambton ; duly acknowledged,
as The Bulletin is also assured ; and never
heard of afterwards —never collated with the
other papers ; never laid on the Legislative
Assembly table ; never referred to by the
Minister. Why??'?

Bricks and Straw.
Dear Bulletin,— When the blood-torrent of
the Indian Mutiny had washed effectually out
of the oriental Briton his contemptuous in
difference to what the mere “nigger might
think or feel, there set in a reaction, wine 1
having worked fatally far in the reverse direc-
tion—confronts the British Raj to-day, with
what is something more than a possibility o a
similar catastrophe. The pendulum has swung
too far over; extremes have met; and, it
England is to keep India, she will have to >e-
take herself again to the strong hand with
which she seized it. The “ conciliation gunn
is played out, and, for all the cosset and
coddle to which the Hindoo has been treated,
he has but come to despise his white
master more—not to hate him less. In de-
ference to the clamours of a blatant evan-
gelism and a windy philanthropy that know
nothing of the true Oriental inwardness,
the Hindoo has been laboriously veneered with
Europeanism. He has been “ improved out
of the more distinctive of his hereditary cus-
toms —“ fitted up wit h all the latest improve-
ments” in Occidental education—given a voice
in the making of the laws and a status in the
administration thereof And the grimmest
satire on the utter futility of the whole business
is found in the fact that the better class Ben-
gali, the man who has fallen most gracetu ly
into line with this bogus Britannieism and
whose acute intellect conforms itself most
readily, to all appearance, to Western ways
of thinking—is the man who is most assidu-
ously and effectually blood-poisoning British
supremacy in India. Cowardly and effeminate
himself—sly, subtle and secret—the Bengali
babu turns to dangerous account his acquaint-
ance with both the European and the native
habits of thought, by hoodwinking in the one
direction and inflaming in the other. In a word
—while apparently more completely on the
conciliation “string” than any of his com-
patriots—the Bengali is an indefatigable sedi-
tion-sower amongst those manlier races oi tne
Peninsula who, because they are less abject in
'their subservience to the European, are less
treacherously virulent in their hatred of him.
The “ educated ” Bengali may be—he mostly
is —a gentleman of the most charming manners
and dulciloquent address ; lie may be, and very
often is, a journalist, a Government official, a
councillor, a judge even; but he remains to
the tips of his fingers a typical Asiatic, who
would gladly see cut, to-morrow, the throat of
every white man and woman in India ! the
super-Anglicised babu, it principally is, who,
as editor of a native print, increases gradually
for his readers the dose of disaffectionate sug-
gestions, until a faint-hearted and farcical pro-
secution warns him that it is time to star
afresh somewhere lower down the scale ; anc
he it is who, if England is not sooner or later to
face another slaughter-carnival, can no longer
be left practically unhindered in his propa-
ganda of sedition or patriotism. It Eng-
land shall ever again be forced to a death-
grapple with those of the Indian races who are
foemen worthy of any steel, right back at the
root of the matter will be found this antagon-
istically contemptible product of the hopeless
attempt to get Occidentalism in any shape
much deeper than the Oriental skin, and to
maintain, by elaborate machinery, an ascend-
ancy which was obtained by naked physica
violence. That is the one brutal, unescapable
fact which renders ridiculous the attempt to fit
on to the situation the fable of the peasant and
the snake. The Bengali maybe an admirable
analogue of the snake, but the parallel tails m
the fact that the hand which has now doctored
him up almost to biting point, is also the hand
that originally scotched him. And the Bengali
balm himself, keenly reminiscent of the fact—is
taking good care that none of his countrymen
shall forget it.—Mokanna.
The Resumed Areas.
A league of selectors has been formed in the
Western division of N.S.W., and branches are
being organised at various centres, with the
excellent object of securing to members assist-
ance in all matters connected with the land
laws and their administration. The weak
feature of the organisation is that the complete
policy was drafted by the promoters, and
apparently without a very full discussion of the
whole matter. It may therefore be doubted
whether, upon more ample consideration, the
league would hold to its plank of out-and-out
opposition to the attachment of resumed areas
to pastoral leases which is the backblooks
question in land legislation at present. 1 he
last Land Act authorised the Minister to make
these attachments where it is shown that the
resumed areas (which are those parts of squat-
that are open to selection) are not, and are
not “likely to be, required for the purpose of
close settlement before the expiration ot the
pastoral leasehold area.” But it also pro-
vibes that the areas so attached may be
withdrawn again on six months notice
being given to the lessee, and that with-
out compensation. The selector, however,
insists that the attachment locks up the land
against settlement, and that, anyway, even
assuming the present administrators of the
law, from the Minister downward, to be fairly
honest, they cannot tell what land is likely to
be required for settlement during the next lo
or 20 years. Also, assuming that a demand
will arise for some of the land which has been
attached, the selectors are not prepared to
admit that every Minister who will be in office
during the next 15 or 20 years will at once
withdraw it. The only safe plan, they argue,
is not to attach land at all—to let it remain
open. But that isn’t safe either ; for while
half of every Western squattage is a resumed
area held only until a selector wants it, so long
will these areas remain unimproved, especially
as though the selector has to pay for improve •
ments, he only pays what they are worth to
him. Further, while resumed areas are unim-
proved they yield far less wealth than they
could and should do ; and they are neglected
until they become the breeding-grounds of
vermin, which are comparatively a greater
curse to the selector than to the squatter ; and
the rental value of all surrounding country is
reduced, while from the resumed areas them-
selves the Slate draws a mere bagatel e
on account of the insecurity of the title
tenure If future Ministers could be absolutely
trusted to withdraw lands as they are wanted
for settlement, the present provision would be
the best one possible. The selector would be
fairly well satisfied ; he could easily apply six
months before he actually required the land.
The squatter would be fairly well satisfied also.
It would give him only a six months assured
tenure, it is true ; but his greatest trouble now
is the shark who comes along, drops his sheep
down on the best patch of feed or the best
waterhole, holds land and improvements against
the station by virtue of a lease application, and
then, when the feed and water are all done,
changes his mind and doesn’t select there a
thing which, of course, he never really intended
to do. A six months’ assured tenure would
stop that game. And the bush-worker would
be fairly well satisfied, too; at any rate,
the nlan would result in the further im-
proving of the country and in heavier stock-
ing, which are the things that ought to
benefit the bush - worker. The weak point
in the law as it stands is the entire de-
pendence upon future Lands Ministers. ul '
were removed, and if the withdrawal o lam
from lease took effect six months after an appli
cation was lodged, without any special refer-
ence to the Minister at all, the difficulty would
be about met The dangers resulting from at-
tachment would be provided aga nst without
altogether abolishing a system which, win e
admitting of abuses as the law stands, is calcu-
lated to do a great deal of good to a country
that very badly needs it.
Old-Age Pensions
The Victorian Old-Age Pension Commission is
rapidly degenerating into burlesque—if it hasn t
already arrived there. In fact, it was pretty
much of a burlesque from the beginning,
like Commissions in general. The members
of it knew' very little about the details of
an Old-Age Pension system to begin with ;
there never has been any such thing m Aus-
tralia, and the only thing resembling what is
wanted has to he looked lor in Europe. ie
witnesses knew just about as little, or less, or
exactly the same reasons. What the conn ry
wanted to know was (1) how many people there
were in the province of the proposed pension
age, which information could have been supplied
bv the State Statistician in a very tew minutes ;
(2) how' much it would cost to give these people
so much a week each, which could be woiket
out in five minutes with a pencil and a piece of
paper; (3) how to pay them the money m the
best and cheapest w ay ; and (4) how to raise the
money. The latter idea is the one on which
the witnesses mostly spread themselves, and as
they were mainly amateur financiers without a
workable idea among them the Commission has
got practically nothing but drivel to show tor
its time and'labor and expense. rhe most
vigorous witness of the crowd wanted to give
10s, {per week to every inhabitant aged over 00,
which would cost about £1,800,000 a year, and
proposed raising the required amount by a
tax on land. Unluckily, the amount that
can be raised even by the largest kind
of‘land-tax is not wholly unlimited ; it the
Government taxed all the land in V ictoria
to its full unimproved value—in other words,
if it seized the whole rental, which is the same
thing as confiscating the land bodily without
compensation—it wouldn’t gel, at present,much
over £4,000,000 a year. Therefore, the pro-
posal in question would mean raising the
total taxes on land to at least 10s. m
the £ of rental. The chances are that the
country isn’t taking any. Another individual
proposed an extra tax of fid. in the £ on all
incomes. This would yield barely enough on
paper, even if it could all be collected without
any expense, but a lot of the very smal
amounts owing by Bill and Jim would never
be collected, and a lot more wouldn t pay tor
stationery and expenses. Another idea was
the deduction of a percentage from sums re-
covered in the courts, which, considering that
the average verdict, even if it is collected,
pays very little more than law expenses, is
hardly promising. Several dozen persons came
forward to propose a tax of 24 per cent, on
theatrical receipts and the gate-money at open-
air sports, with a perfect faith that it would cover
everything handsomely, whereas the solemn
probability is that it wouldn’t pan out for more
than £50,000 of the required million or two.
Apparently the best device of all is to shut up
the Commission with every possible speed, and
let Parliament tackle the matter itself. Parlia-
ment may not be a very intelligent institution,
bub compared witli the oldest inhabitant who
maunders before the Commission it is wisdom
incarnate. The Pension Commission invited
everybody who had a great or valuable oi
original idea to come forward and help it m
dealing with an urgent but very difficult re-
form, and so far it appears that there isn t a
great or valuable idea in the community. 4he
nearest approach to one was the land-tax idea,
but even the biggest land-tax that the country
would be likely to stand wouldn t nearly fall
the gap.
Ed. Bulletin, — Re your recent articles on the
Suffield case, it may be of interest to youi
readers to know that even so early a writer on
English Law as Matthew Hale in his Pleas
of the Crown ” has pointed out the necessity of
taking extraordinary precautions in a trial for
rape. “ It is,” he writes, “ an accusatjpn
easily to be made and hardly to be proved, and
harder to be defended by the party accused
though never so innocent.” And Hale, o
course, wrote at a time when the legal definition
of “carnal knowledge” was by no means so
wide as now. After setting forth a number of
cases where, though the charges were entirely
groundless, the parties accused either suffered or
escaped with great difficulty, and no one knows
with what mental agony, he goes on to say, I
only mention these instances that we may be
the more cautious upon trials of offences of this
nature, wherein the Court and jury may, with
so much ease, be imposed upon, without great
care and vigilance, the heinousness of the offence
many times transporting the Judge and jury
with so much indignation that they are over-
hastily carried to the conviction of the persons
accused thereof by the confident testimony some-
times of malicious and false witnesses .” Sydney
people need not be reminded of the Buttner
case as a striking illustration of the truth of the
above remarks.—Yvon.
The scandalous case of murderer Lyfield
cropped up in the Vic. Assembly a few nights
ago, when somebody questioned Attorney-
General Isaacs as follows : “ Had his attention
been drawn to the fact that a solicitor had
charged Henry Lyfield the sum of £l2OO for
defending him on a charge of murder ? (2) As
Lyfield was afterwards adjudged insane, so
far as murder was concerned, was it not pro-
bable he was insane on financial matters also
when he agreed (as alleged by the solicit! r) to
pay him £l2OO for the case ? (3) Would the
Acting-Premier order an enquiry, with a view
to saving some of the amount from the solicitor
in order to provide for the relatives of Lyfield .
To which Mr. Isaacs replied that the law had
already made an attempt to tax the aforesaid
bill of costs, but the Chief Justice had ruled
that when a client contracts with his solicitor
for a certain defence at a certain price the
solicitor’s bill is absolved from taxation. And
in partial excuse for this grabbing of the client s
estate Mr. Isaacs said there was. no allega-
tion that Lyfield was insane at the time he made
the agreement, but only at the time the mm dev
was committed .” Mr. Isaacs further stated that
the Crown was anxious to do all it could to
obtain some of Lyfield’s money for his family,
but he carefully avoided the question of an en-
quiry as to the circumstances of themurderers
defence. An enquiry would probably reveal a
lot more sanity in Lyfield than was suggested
by the behaviour of that incestuous beast
during the hurried progress of his trial for
murder It would probably display Lyfif.ld
as a businesslike farmer authorising the solicitor
to engaee leading barristers regardless of ex-
pense. The general public has quite decided
that the hanging of anybody, whilst Lyfield
remains alive, will be an act of hideous in-
justice.
N.S.W. Justice Stephen, in the course of the
Norton contempt case, rightly condemns the
undiscriminating use of the phrase ‘ Contempt
of Court.” It is not contempt of court, the
Judge holds, that is involved in the general run
of these cases for attachment, but words or
acts calculated to prejudice a plaint;ff’s or de-
fendant’s chance of fair trial in fact, contempt
of justice, or contempt of the people. But there
are, of course, direct contempts of court as, for
instance, when the Brisbane police-clerk canied
a revolver into court as a menace to Judge or
counsel engaged. The shocked Judge, in the
fright of the moment, ordered the foolish youth
to imprisonment for life. Contempt of Parlia-
ment still figures among nominally awful
offences, but has been sadly burlesqued in
practice. George Dill, of Melb. Argus, was
summoned to the Bar of the House for con-
tempt, and committed to the custody of the
Sergeant-at-Arms, but was released on paying
fees. Hugh George, of the same paper,
was, for contempt, ordered to be im-
prisoned in Parliament till he paid fees.
But George wouldn’t pay, and after Parlia-
ment had made itself supremely ridiculous it
swallowed the contempt, let go the fees, and
ran the bold printer out again. N.SAV. Par-
liament is learning by bitter experience to take
whatever contempt is offered and say nothing
about dungeons or fees. In fact, any man who
can get himself summoned to the Bar of N.S. VV .
Parliament, and can be proved to have covered
the institution with contempt, is pretty sure of
being returned at the head of the poll at next
election.

The one thing which is more utterly stupid
than the drafting of the N.S.W. Land and In-
come Tax Act is the drafting of the Validating
Bill which is to amend it. The blank in-
capacity of Acting-Premier Bunkum and his
colleagues sticks out conspicuously all through
it. For instance, take the following clause
Where, before the day of the commencement of this
act, a notice of assessment of land-tax or income-tax, or
a notice purporting to be such, has been given by the
commissioners to a taxpayer, but the amount of the tax
has not been paid, the assessment of the said tax and the
said notice shall be deemed valid and to have been duly
made and given, and the said amount, shall be due and
payable within 30 days of the day aforesaid, notwith-
standing any omission to compb' with or contra\ ention ot
the principal Act.
Consider the beautiful comprehensiveness of
this. The trouble to be remedied was simply
that the original Act required the assessment
books to be completed before the tax was pay_
able. They couldn’t conveniently be completed
in time ; therefoi’e an amending Act was re-
quired stating that their completion before the
collection of the tax was not essential. The God-
fearing Government probably didn’t feel sute
that it could draft that amendment properly,
so it proposes to make dead certain by enacting
that in every case where the lax Commis-
sioners have demanded any money off anybody
as landaninkum-tax, that money shall be
legally due despite anything either here or here-
after. The clause covers everything. And
now the question arises whether it would be
safe to validate even the angel Gabriel in such
a wholesale fashion as this.
One of the anomalies of the Vic. Crown Law
Department is its subservience to the tinpot
dignitaries of the municipalities surrounding
Melbourne. A number of the latter have
manufactured the rule that the Mayor for
the time being shall invariably act as chairman
of the bench! and, consequently, whenever a
police-magistrate arrives, he finds his seat
occupied by an aitchless person with a red face.
Instead of calling a six-foot policeman to chuck
the local intruder out of the chair, the I -M- 8
have contented themselves with making a “ tew
remarks” about want of courtesy and have given
these courts a wide berth in the future. Ihe
result generally has been to make such tri-
bunals stink with jobbery and virtually fall into
the hands of a favorite solicitor who pulls the
strings while the bench works. Indeed, the
chairman has been known to act as a tout lot
its white-haired legal boy and advise litigants
in the court building to retain the services of
one Mr. Shark. The litigants usually
take the hint—and get the verdict. In cases
outside the scope of the lawyer aforesaid, the
most ludicrous injustice and legal ignorance
prevails, and one court is simply a gold-mine
for strange lawyers who know their business,
in that adverse decisions invariably result, in
successful “orders to review.” The wild-eyed
Lormer did excellent service by showing up
the glaring injustices of the Vic. Bench, but the
greater part of the injustices are perpetrated in
cases between common persons, and cases of not.
sufficient public interest to earn a report and
possible exposure. The occasional presence of
a P.M. would shake things up considerably,
and why the Crown Law Dept, chooses td back-
down before the mandates of the tuppenny-
ha’penny local councils is a thing no civilised
person can understand. “1 he honorarium
magistracy ” the man who invented tins
phrase deserves a statue.

Frank Lincoln, the American humorist, is
doing a week of hard glacial deposit at the Cen-
tenary Hall, Sydney. Why the genial cocktailist
should have resulted in so prompt and unanimous
a frost it is difficult to imagine, but the frost itself
is beyond all doubt. When Lincoln was here last
he was a great success ; the old Gaiety was as full
as it could hold ; the audience fell down and wor-
shipped the lady with the whistling front teeth,
and the demoralised passenger, and the maniac
musician ; and the visionary drink fell like dew
on a thirsty land. The memory of that spectral
refreshment remained green through nine long,
sad years; it survived the silver-boom and the
bank-burst, and the building society collapse, and
all manner of other woes, and half Australia told
the story, as its own particular joke, to the other
half. And now that Lincoln has returned there is
a thin smattering of audience in the Centenary
Hall —an audience that smattered very little the
first night, and less the second, till it
seems possible, at time of writing, that it will
presently not smatter at all—and the lost,
lorn joke echoes dismally in the great empti-
ness. Yet the humorist is as brilliant as ever ;
some of his stories are new, and if the cocktail and
the lady with the whistling tooth are not entirely
fresh, they are good enough to bear a lot of repe-
tition. Also, Bert Levy’s caricatures are a
powerful attraction, the sketches of Brough,
Thornton, ’Arry Rickards, the ancient King Billy
who hangs about Poverty Point, Premier Reid as
be will be when he gets back from the Jubilee,
Lincoln, Sayles —the eullud gentleman with the
mammoth mouth, Lonuen, and others being all
particularly excellent. Probably the explanation
is that Sydney is a more serious-minded place
than it was ten years ago ; or possibly it is a more
frivolous place, but it frivols with legs nowadays,
where it used to frivol with brains. One explana-
tion is as explanatory as the other. Anyhow, the
city has tendered Frank Lincoln a most cordial
invitation to go away.
** * *
Sydney Palace re-opened on Monday, and
if the success of the first night keeps up through-
out the season Rickards will probably make a
small occasional profit out of his venture. But
the show which the lavish ’Arry has put on at the
Palace can’t possibly leave much profit. It is a
highly expensive affair, and the Most Beautiful
Theatre on Earth is a small structure at the best
—so small that even when it keeps full the cash
margin can’t amount to much. Therefore the
public had better roll up vigorously. The Bio-
graph is the biggest feature of the new show. It
is our old friend the Cinematographe with im-
provements ; the mad and dizzy wobble, and the
flicker, and the streak-of-lightning aspect of the
original “ Gin.” have been considerably alleviated ;
the pictures are larger and better defined, and
they are all exceedingly beautiful. “ Niagara ”is
the best and most thrilling picture yet produced,
and the fire-engine race, “shooting the shoot”—a
giddy view of a boat going down a rapid at 00
miles an hour, and the sketch of a dude overboard,
are close up. The small, soapy nigger in the bath,
however, seems to take best with the audience,
and the encore which followed the infant African
shook the building to its foundations.
♦ ♦ * *
Rickards has imported a number of excel-
lent singers—even the boy phenomenon, despite
his silk knickerbockers, and his heavenly expres-
sion, and his fly’s eyebrow, and The Bulletin’s
objection to the boy phenomenon in general, must
be admitted to be above the average. Professor
Morritt’s illusions are mostly good enough to pass
muster, especially the one by which he produces
four nice girls out of nothing ; and Miss Went-
worth's monologue is really amusing, which
doesn’t often happen to the lady monologist.
That old-time favorite, Miss Fanny Liddiard, is
one of the new importations, and she looks as
pretty as she used to do when she and Nellie
Stewart reached for each other’s hair in “ Madame
Angot ” ; also, she sings, if possible, more sweetly
than ever. Nevertheless, on the opening night
she got about the coldest reception of the
evening. The audience didn’t rise up honestly
and state what grievance in had against the
charming Fanny, but it failed to give
her half the encouragement she deserved. Possi-
bly her songs, which were musical and compli-
cated things with much elaborate execution in
them, are not the kind that a Palace audience
craves after. Something with a breezy howl in it
might go better.

Maggie Moore filled in a night or two with
that bright Irish play, “Mrs. Quinn’s Twins.”
This was followed by a spell of “Johanna,” which
isn’t bright, but is lamentably confused, and is
disfigured by a dead pawnbroker, and a general
tendency on the part of everybody to turn up un-
accountably as everybody else’s long-lost wife or
daughter, who left home many years previously
for reasons that are as undiscoverable as the lost
grave of Moses, On Saturday the “ Trilby ” bur-
lesque, which proved such a decided success in
Miss Moore’s last season, will be revived, and
Svengali will again make music for ze pig-dog and
ze Scotch.

* * & *
James MacMahon, the Mighty Atom, goes
in strongly now for war-pictures at his Salon Cine-
matographe, the “Charge of Turkish Cavalry ”
being about the most soul-stirring item of the lot.
Anybody who feels like enlisting in Majah
Neild’s regiment of amateur warriors should have
a look at this picture as a preliminary. Then
when he has considered the mad onslaught of
the hairy furies of Bagdad and Byzantium,
and asks himself how he would like to leave
the day-book and the ledger, and go out
hurriedly to argue the point with a similar
human cataract, he can go and enlist in a
more serious spirit. The Bulletin doesn’t mean
that he should give up the idea of enlisting, but
it is always advisable for him to know in advance
what kind of people are waiting about in the dark
corners of the earth to jump on the fresh and
giddy book-keeping militaire as soon as oppor-
tunity presents itself.
* * * *
Melbourne theatres just now are being
generously supported by people who, for the most
part, have seen the same show several times
already and are taking another dose through force
of circumstances. The programmes remind one of
Chirgwin’s introduction to his song—“ I don’t
know whether you'll like it, but you’ve got to 'ave
it.” They’ve got to have “ Lights o’ London ” at
the Royal, for a fortnight, and because there was
nothing unusual happening elsewhere George Rig-
nold shook his tale of woe at a crowded audience
on Saturday. Familiarity doesn’t breed scorn for
G. R. S ims’s doleful melodrama, although the
higher etilehaw of this journal revolts against the
financial principles which impel an admirable
Shakespearian actor to jump into a tank. “ Lights
o' London” seems less wildly improbable than
a lot of other, and livelier, dramas as regards plot,
also it introduces many small items of human
interest, in a natural sort of way. Moreover, it is
commendably free from bloated capitalism, and
riotous living. George Rignold wanders listlessly
through a world where nobody has any money
to speak of, except a casual philosopher, who
stumbles across a £ 10-note, and starts a baked-
potato and ha’penny-ice business, with every pros-
pect of having to file his schedule, owing to gene-
ral depression. A world where policemen show a
tender feeling for the poor and homeless by
running them into a nice warm gaol, if they behave
themselves, and where wifely affection thrives on
ill-treatment, and professional philanthropy is an
animated finger-post directing weary pilgrims to
fo and die in the workhouse. The real world of a
iondon that plays no part in the Jubilee illustra-
tions of polite weekly papers. George Rignold
and his plunge into the tank are about the most
important-looking features of a long and mode-
rately strong performance. Stirling White, Diver,
J• W. Sweeney, Frank Hawthorn, and Miss
Georgie Smithson come to the fore at various
times. Miss Emily Hughes, whose elocution is
now almost as good as her voice, creates a pro-
found impression by urging G.R. to knock Diver
out in one round, and a handsome young novice
(Miss Lilian Wheeler) cuts a promising figure as
the—well, call her the girl who doesn’t get for-
mally married to the man she loves.
* * * *
Thirteen years have glided over Rev.
Robert Spalding’s cold in the head since his
goloshes first trod Melbourne boatds, and mean-
while he has struggled through many revivals of
the stale bath-bun, and has been hurled bodily
into a wooden box at four different theatres.
Nevertheless, his latest outbreak at the Bijou pro-
mises to earn another large profit. “ The Private
Secretary ” is very little more, or less, than the
subjection of a gentle shepherd to personal vio-
lence and all-round contumely. The main secret
of its success is suffering, patiently-endured by a
red-nosed curate who comes under the description
of “absolutely pure,” like the recent Sunday-
school teacher. Australia very properly revels in
the maltreatment of Rev. Spalding, on account of
his exceeding goodness, and although the new
Uncle Cattertnole can’t hope to obliterate the
public’s fond recollections of old friend Harwood,
he will be generally admired for his savage
attacks upon the cloth. Which reminds us that
Rev. Spalding would be a good subject for another
religious controversy. Is he a great moral lesson,
and are we indebted to Early Christianity for his
bottle, of milk ? Outside of Frank Thornton and
the new Uncle Cattertnole, there are one or two
members of the Bijou company (notably Miss
Carew) who make the best of their parts, and
several other members who don’t. But the short-
comings are scarcely worth talking about, as long
as Thornton is there to play the name-part.
