The Selbinis are leaving Melb. Opera
House this week, but the spectacle of Mrs. Turner
and Premier George expressing their jubilee feel-
ings from a Royal kerridge will remain a feature
of the bill for some time to come. Business was
immense at the Opera House last week. On the
Tuesday night people fought for standing-room to
hear Harry Rickards read a list of joys in store.
And when that remarkable man, adopting his
most fatherly manner, told the multitude that he
had made arrangements to build for them, one and
all, the very best theatre that money could buy,
and was about to take another fifty years’ lease of
their kind indulgence, he got such a greeting as
probably did make the youthful veteran glad to be
back among them once more, ladies and gentlemen.

“ The fortunate but deserving Hermans are rehearsing
to go on tour in ‘The Circus Girl’ with one of George
Edwards’provincial companies," writes Tub Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “The three play minor parts, in
addition to providing a variety turn in the circus-scene,
and are financially well-treated. Behind the ‘make-up’
an Australian reputation goes for as little as it does in any
other line of life, yet the 1 underneathers,’ if they do not
put on too much side and open their mouths too wide,
will always get on here if they are honest, earnest mum-
mers. Billy Elton is biking, Poulton matineeing, and
Florrie Ford opening at London Pavilion—the favorite
‘ hall’of her countrymen—on August Bank Holiday, and
she will have a slashing show to knock the gallery.”
Somebody dropped Burke a turnip. He came slowly to
the footlights and called in a sympathetic voice, whilst
dangling the vegetable : “The individual who threw his
head can have it back by applying at the stage-door.”
Facts. —AI a certain Melb. show, the other night, the
inclusion of a certain new (faked) item, caused an amusing
episode in the circle. Paterfamilias, visiting the theatre
with two daughters aged about 15 and 16 respectively,
when the item in question came on, gravely pulled out
two handkerchiefs, which he placed on the heads of the
young ladies, at the same time remarking, “You know
quite enough, ray dears.’’

The replacement at a minute’s notice of a certain actor
by his under-study has given rise to litigation. Said
change was made betwt en first and second acts of a

In the days when veteran Edmund Holloway (“ Rob-
bery Under Arms” Moran, and numerous other Dam-
pierian *’ villians”) used to run his own show he, though
no musician himself, had a great idea of the effect of a
little “ shivery grass” properly administered. His coach-
ing of a rather green accompanist on one occasion will,
perhaps, be remembered by a few-. “ Now, young man,”
said Edmund in his slow, ponderously-dramatic style,
addressing the embryo Paderewski, “ when the prima-
donna receives her cue, you will play pi-an-issimo. On
her appearing at the wings you will play pi-ano. As she
slow ly advances two steps upon the stage you will play
crescendo. With her continued progress towards the
front you will play forrr-te ; and when finally she reaches
the footlights 3 - ou will belt the blanky inside out of the
instrument and bring down the house.”

George Darrell has just concluded a season at the
Theatre Ro3'al, Perth (W.A.), during which he produced
“ Black-Eyed Susan,” “ the celebrated drama, written up-
to-date,” and a version of “La Tosca.” George, when
playing William, used to take an hour, as it were, to
choose a spot on which to die, till one night a god yelled,
“ Hurry up, George—we want to catch the last ’bus.”
As the hour was then 9.30, and the last ’bus left at 11.30,
the gag raised a laugh. The “ version of ‘La Tosca ’ ” was
as funny as a funeral.
The estate of the late David Lee, Melb. City Organist
Shows a deficiency of £258 13s. 7d. Almost anything
pays better in Melb. than pounding the keys—unless the
pounder happens to be a foreigner with a dishevelled
mane and a wild glare in his eye.
Pinero’s latest play, “The Princess and the Butterfly,”
was far from being a great popular success. All the
same, it yielded £16,000 to the St. James’s Theatre
treasury, from 96 performances-an average of nearly
£l6B per night. A partial failure is good enough when
the stalls are half-a-guinea apiece and the management
has a “ following.”
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
That eminently dull and ox-like person, H.S.W.
Acting-Premier Brunker, has rushed through the Assem-
bly an amending Income-tax Bill which looks a good deal
more confused than the original muddle. The way it is
now proposed to fix things is as follows : The citizen pays
tax in 1897 according to the amount of his income for
1896, and so on, it being assumed that his income for
1897 will be the same as that for 1896. If it is less, then
he has paid too much tax and is. entitled to a refund.
But instead of paying back the exact amount overpaid, it
is proposed, for no visible reason, to take an average of
the income for 1895-96-97, and if the income for 1897 proves
to be less than the average of these three .years, then he
gets his refund calculated on the difference —at least it
seems to be optional with the Government to refund
either on the basis of the previous year, or on that of the
three years’average, whichever way it can best swindle
the taxpayer. The tax is to be collected on one basis,
and the refunds are to be made on the other—or, at least,
the Government apparently reserves the right to make
them on the other. The clause reads as follows :
“ If any taxpayer proves to the satisfaction of the Commis-
sioners that his income chargeable, arising, or accruing
during the year of assessment fell short of the income
chargeable in respect of which the income-tax for that
3 T ear was paid, the Commissioners shall cause the assess-
ment to be amended, and shall give the certificate in
virtue of which a refund may be obtained as hereinbefore
mentioned : Provided that no such amendment of the
assessment shall be made or certificate given or refund
made unless the income chargeable, arising, or accruing
during the year of assessment is proved to be less than
the amount to be found by taking an average of the
income chargeable, arising, or accruing during the year
of the assessment and the two years immediately preced-
ing that year, nor shall any refund exceed the difference
between the tax on the income chargeable, arising, or
accruing during the year of assessment and the tax on
the amount found by taking the average as aforesaid.”
This arrangement — if it really means what it
says—pans out in a lot of wildly insane ways. For
instance, supposing a taxpayer’s income, apart from
exemptions, is as follows :
1395 £4OO I 1897 £lOOO
1896 1600 |
He would pay, for 1897, £4O because his 1896 income was
£l6OO. Then later on it is discovered that his 1897 income
is only £lOOO and that he should have paid only £25, and
is consequently entitled to £l5 refund. But apparently
he doesn’t get anything, and the Government keeps the
whole £4O, and swindles him out of his £l5, because,
though his income for 1897 is less than that for 1896, it
isn’t less than the average for 1895-6-7. On the other
hand, the three years’ income may be something like
this
1895 £lBOO I 1897 £7OO
1896 800 |
In that case the taxpayer will have paid for 1897 £2O (on
£BOO income), whereas he should only have paid t!7 10s.
(on £7uo income), and he should get a refund of £2 10s.
on the difference between £7OO and £BOO. But if the
average principle is applied he will get a refund of £lO,
calculated on the difference between his real income of
£7OO and his average income of £llOO for the three years.
Therefore the tangled and complicated swindle would be
against the Government. But apparently it won't apply
in that case ; the clause says the refund is not to be more
than the amount due on the difference between the 1897
income and the average income for 1895-6-7, but appa-
rently it may be a great deal less. So presumably the
average system will apply when it enables the Govern-
ment to swindle the taxpayer, and when it would enable
the taxpayer to swindle the Government the -refund will
be calculated on the other system.
Any Ministry with sufficient financial ability
and honesty to fill the billet of an office-boy in a pawn-
shop would solve the difficulty by abolishing refunds a’to-
gether. All that is wanted is to enact that the tax on the
1896 income shall be payable in ’97, and that on the ’97
income in ’9B, and so on ai infinitum. Then there would
be no refunds, no “arrears,” no averages, and•»»■» Insane
fool-complications whatever.

N.S.W. Post and Telegraph deficit for the year
ended 30th June, ’97, is reported as £133,406. This is
about the usual deficit. N.S. W. Postal Department has
been a woefully insolvent affair ever since the beginning.
• * • *
The Bulletin is getting very tired of the con-
stant reappearance of that unreliable item about the
N.S.W. railways having this year paid all expenses and
interest, and returned £33,479 profit. It all resolves itself
into an easy sum in subtraction. The deficit last year,
according to the Auditor-General, was £321,690. This
year the net earnings are £144,995 more than last, there-
fore, the probable deficit is £177,000 or thereabouts. In
the face of these melancholy figures Tiie Bulletin’s re-
marks about the above alleged profit are mostly “ dash.”
* * * *
Victorian Railway Department announces that
the receipts for the year ended 30th June, ’97, were
£386,000 short of expenditure. The 1896 deficit was
£583,000, so things are £197,000 better.

half-year ended 30th June is £9107. The doings of the
Standard for the last three years are as follows :
December, ’94 Loss £6837 I June, *96 Loss £7063
June, ’95 » 4908 December, ’96 ~ 7992
December, *95 ~ 1513 | June, ’97 ~ 9107
In the latter part of ’95 the Standard, by one of its
numerous recons ructions, reduced depositors' interest to
ou per cent., and secured other concessions, whereby the
bwik saved about 1 32,000 a year ; nevertheless it is a lot
worse after the saving than before. This half-year the
gross earnings are £1571, and working expenses absorb
£1256, so the net receipts are £315. Out of this £315 the
Standard Bank has to pay 1 9422 interest to depositors, so
its outgoings are just about 30 times the net income. It
has £551,707 paid-up capital, and there is nothing left to
call up ; but of this £414,602 is admittedly lost, and there
is an item of £529,232 “ doubtful accounts at book value.”
Out of £1,300,398 total nominal assets, therefore, £414,602
consists of losses and bad debts, £529,232 consists
of doubtful debts, and only £356,564 purports to
be good. As the Standard owes £748,691 to depositors
and the public generally this looks like about 9s. in the £.
But the gloom which hangs about even those assets which
profess to be good is conspicuously evidenced by the fact
that they only yield at present rather less than eighteen
shillings per cent, per annum. Judging by the internal
evidence of the awful balance-sheet, and assuming that
the gross profit represents only 4 per cent, on the good
assets, the shareholders’ capital is certainly gone, and,
after preference creditors are paid, the Standard Bank
looks good for about 2s. in the £ to its deferred depositors.
The present market-value of these deposits is from 2s. 3d. to
2s. 6d. in the £, and Thb Bulletin reckons that price
decidedly stiff.
Within the last six months the Standard’s bad
debts have increased by £33,696, the accumulated loss
has grown by £9107, and the gross income has gone down
one-half (from £3268 to £1571). The following is this
wonderful institution’s gross income for six half-years :
December, ’94 ... £26,744 I June, ’96 ... £10,962
June, ’95 ... 23,238 December, ’96 ... 3,268
December, ’95 ... 17,241 | June, ’97 ... 1,571
At this rate, the Standard, next half-year, will have no
income at all. If there is anywhere on earth a more hope-
less-looking institution than this The Bulletin would like
to have its name and address forthwith. Yet, so long as
deposit interest is paid regularly (out of the depositors’
own assets) there is, apparently, no law' to knock the
melancholy remains on the head and put them out of
their misery. A statute by which utterly insolvent
financial institutions could be peremptorily shut up and
liquidated and put out of sight would fill a very urgent
want.
• * * *
Melbourne Tramway Co., for the 12 months
ended June 30th, realised £38,510 net profit, as compared
with £26,581 in 1896, £28,090 in ’95, £19,682 in ’94, and
£22,619 in ’93. The present is therefore by far the best
return since ’92—the last year before the famous Deluge,
when the windows of Heaven were opened, and it rained
reconstructed deposits for 40 days and 40 nights. The
usual 5 per cent, dividend (£24,000) is paid, and £15,000
is written off freehold properties.
* * * *
Last week silver got down successfully to 2s.
Ser oz., a fall of 6d. per oz. since last December!
ow an agitated world is waiting to see if the 2s. limit
will stop the fall, or if the miserable white substance will
drop into the wilderness of odd halfpennies and farthings
that lie between Is. and 2s. Anyhow, one certain fact is
that the margin of profit at even Broken Hill is getting
pretty narrow.
* * * m
Waverley (Sydney) Council has discovered, by
a special audit, that a deceased official has left behind
him defalcations of considerably over £lOOO. The defal-
cations go back as far as the special audit extended ; if it
had been carried further back the same chronic shortage
might have turned up every year since the borough
began. The local auditors never dropped on any of these
things; in N.S.W. the municipal auditor is elected by the
ratepayers, and as they, in the main, know nothing
about accounts, they generally elect a man who knows
nothing either —with the above results. The Waverley
Council now decides that the popularly-elected butcher-
auditor is a failure, and want some kind of Government
audit established by law. The other Councils would do
well to support this idea. Considering that any decently
smart man can embezzle money wholesale right under the
eyes of the average amateur auditor, and considering how
very few men there are who wouldn’t embezzle when so
beautiful an opportunity presented itself, the need for
State inspection is probably about six times as great as
anybody is aware of.
• * • •
At a meeting of Melbourne’s “slop” manufac-
turers, last week, a Mr. J. Barnett put in an artless pro-
test against the schedule of prices proposed by the cloth-
ing trade board, under the new Factories Act. Said dis-
tressful Barnett: “It was the earnest desire of em-
ployers to see their hands benefited as far as practicable,
and they had gone the length of offering an advance of
100 per cent, in the rates, but they were proposed now to
be raised 150 per cent.” The earnest desire to benefit
workers was evidently born of the employers’ sad convic-
tion that they couldn’t help themselves. Previous to the
Government appointment of an enquiry board these
same philanthropic slop-makers paid their hands half the
rates they are now offering.
* * * *
A sewing-machine importer, who was being sued
for commission, admitted in evidence, the other day, that
a machine which he would sell on the time-payment
system—a small deposit down, and half-a-crown a week—
at £l3, was invoiced to him at £2 18s. 6d. ! Also, that
many time-payment purchasers, after paying a few
pounds, could not keep up the payments, whereupon the
sewing-machine was taken back, renovated, and resold as
new. When middlemen fall out, honest folk get at facts.
It seems as if a sewing-machine, with a run of luck among
“ bad marks,” might pay for itself a dozen times over, and
then get sold for £l3 at the dead finish.
• • * ♦
Latest English advices announce the collapse of
the bike-boom. Quite a number of the very best manu-
facturers are reducing prices 50 per cent., whilst lower-
grade Yanks and German brands are on sale at ’Ome at
“any price.” It is notorious that three, at least, biq im-
porters in Melbourne are in a state of collapse, and the
grog-dealers, drapers, boot-dealers, &e., who, 12 months
ago, took bike importation on as a soft-snap are “ closing
out ” to a man. “ A bike ” (of some sort) “given away
with every pound of tea ” is not yet within measurable
distance, however.
The Melbourne banks alone hold certs, of un-
redeemed cycles enough to mount a fair-sized army,
whilst the money-lenders who made a speciality of ad-
vances on bike-hire agreements, are (even with a margin
of 50 per cent, to work upon) very badly left. It dawneth
on Israel that under-aged youth, “ living with mother,”
owning a knickerbocker suit and a life-interest in a
second-hand bike only, is bad “ security ” for the £l5 or
£2O owing on his machine.
Little wonder that the N.Z. Accident Insurance
Co. is full-up of insuring bikes against burglary, or that
the hire-merchants are grieved that the opportunity the
Insurance Co. thus offered them of making a recovery on
hire agreement machines is gone for ever.
Ed. Bulletin, —Noonewith any knowledge of the
subject could have written such an absurd letter as that of
“ Bovis,” in your issue of 14th. An ordinary fat bullock,
if boiled down, does not give 3cwt., or anything like
3cwt. of tallow. He does not give 20s. worth of manure
and etceteras, besides the tallow and hide, or anything
like it. The figures are ludicrously inaccurate. There is
no mystery about the treatment of a condemned beast at
Ramornie; he is simply boiled down for tallow and
manure, and except tallow, manure, and hide, there is no
other product. Any owner could at any time satisfy him-
self on these points'by watching. The working cost of
boiling down is, of course, far above ss. per head, when
fuel, interest on plant, &c., are considered. The cask
alone, or the share of it to hold 3cwt. of tallow, would
nearly swamp the ss. We do not charge 20 per cent, dis-
count for cash. (We charge 2 per cent, discount for cash.)
I should have thought this error the printer’s, not
“Bovis's”; but it is not greater than others he makes.
—C. F. Tindert. Manager, Ramornie.

At Melb. Supreme Court, last week, Judge
A’Beckett ruled that the executors of One Fredk. Hutch-
ings had lost about £9OOO of the estate in more or less
inexcusably unsafe investments, and were entitled to
repair the damage out of their own pockets. The decision
will, probably, lead to sundry other refunds from other
executors possessed of means to make atonement, for
their boomtime dealings with legacy money, but it can’t
benefit the army of widows and fatherless whose im-
pecunious trustees lived for awhile by pouring the estate
funds into “ snide ” mortgages.

Descriptions accompanied by gold-yields—names
of winners and the quantities won—from the alluvial
camp on the Klondyke goldfield, British Columbia, now
published, will keep up the rush. Nothing will stop men
going there in tens of thousands. The Canadian Govt,
has sensibly abandoned the proposed royalty, and has
reduced claims to 100 ft. Everything foreshadows a rush
such as the world has not seen for over 40 years.
* * * *
If any reliance is to be placed on newspaper
reports, the track of bones along the road to Klondyke,
when the snow melts next summer, should be something
like the great streak of skeletons which the French left
behind on the famous retreat from Moscow. Spring,
summer, and autumn at Klondyke last, three months ; the
other nine months are desolation and howling winter.
Even the fast-flowing' Yukon River is blocked with ice
from September to May. It is 1900 miles up the Yukon
to Klondyke. Some recent advices set forth that there
has already been a burst of premature winter ; that many
of the mountain passes are blocked by snow, and that
5000 miners are stranded on the overland track with no
chance worth mentioning of getting forward and no very
brilliant show' of getting back. Also the usual warning
comes along that the richness of Klondyke has been
greatly exaggerated in the interests of traders and store-
keepers and of the agile Yankee who peddles the wooden
ham of commerce. The one thing which seems reason-
ably certain amid much uncertainty is that the wolf and
the local bear and the remarkable blue fox will have a
square meal of lost prospector this coming winter.
* * * •
More trouble for Sunday Brunker ! The Cobar
police “ lately endeavored to slop the cyanide works from
w’orking on Sunday, although the work was proved to be
necessary. The case was dismissed on a technicality of
faulty information; but it is supposed that it will
be brought on again. It is stated that the police are
awaiting instructions from Sydney. If work is stopped
on the Cobar Gold Mines, it is argued that the police will
also have to stop the water-jackets of the local copper-
mine, which would mean an immense loss both to the
syndicate and the town.” Here, indeed, is a pretty kettle
of fish. The N.S.W. Minister for Justice is one of the
four lucky Great Cobar tributors who so profitably run
the water-jackets aforesaid : so the police, if they finally
decide to stop Sunday-work by the Cobar Gold-Mines’
100 stampers, and the cyanide-plant, attached thereto,
will, as a matter of logic, also have to shut-down on the
God-fearing Minister Gould’s Sabbath-smelting operations.
Developments in this ticklish matter will be awaited with
interest. The Cobar police probably “didn’t know it was
loaded.”
* * * *
The leases of the Pinnacles are now the oldest
held continususly in the Broken Hill and Silverton dis-
trict ; they were issued on Aug. 6, ’B5. The Broken Hill
leases, which followed a week or two afterwards, are next
in order of seniority. Which means that the whole of the
Thaekaringa, Day Dream, and Purnamoota “shows” —
the claims which brought the Barrier into notice just as
the Coolgardie claims attracted attention to Westralia—
have in the interim been thrown up, though some have
since been repegged.
* * * *
The B.H. Prop., which, a few years ago, held
only half-a-dozen mineral leases, has 36 now in its name,
and thus establishes the province’s record, though the
Tarrawingee Flux Co., with 28, runs it pretty close. One
by one, as leases around Broken Hill have been forfeited,
the Prop, has taken them up, some merely for the water
that could be pumped out of the shafts, but others for
possible sulphide bodies in them. Thus the co. holds the
Victoria Cross, next the B.H. North, and likely to prove
quite as good a claim. Which certainly is not saying
much, though North’s prospects have improved lately.

Parkes (N.S.W.) : “ A suspension of labor-con-
ditions granted to the Dayspring mine, to permit of more
capital being raised.” Good—but we never are told of

Queensland has 40 million acres of gold and mineral fields,
while geologist Jack estimates that the province contains
minerals of commercial value throughout half its area
Since 1860, gold has been raised to the value of
±39,196,465, while all minerals raised amount to
±48,444,074.
* » * *
Gympie, with a population of 12,140 persons,
employs 1100 quartz-miners and five alluvial miners.
Other trades number 575 devotees; merchants and
traders include 1060 individuals, while women and child-
ren number 9500. Consequently, one out of every twelve
of the population is a miner. . . . Contrast the Etheridge
with its 10,000 sq. miles, and 805 miners, of whom 104 are
Chinese.
• * * *
Croydon produced over 4f>,ooooz. gold during
the last half-year. The cyanide returns are falling
rapidly, most of the heaps being through. But the local
paper—the Mijndic Miner—is not anxious that Croydon
should be regarded outside as a prosperous community.
“We are nothing of the sort. We are a debt-ridden,
poverty-stricken crowd without a golden dollar we can
legitimately call our own. Like beasts in the back creeks
in drought-time, we are poor, and on our poverty-stricken
carcases live the bailiff, and the gentlemanly assignee,
and the other carrion crows of our civilisation, who wax
fat while we die for the want of a little of that golden
dust that we, by our labor, produce.” Poor and honest.
* * * *
Q. Royal Commission on Mining, &c., has
brought forward the Hodgkinson with a prophetic paean.
Its ieport quotes Govt. Geologist Jack, who is certainly a
cautious man, and yet who says, evidently in amazement:
“ The agents of capitalists are running all over the world
looking for mines such as have been abandoned on the
Hodgkinson by the score.”
And what Jack says of Hodgkinson, I. B. J.
Skertchley, lately Q. Assistant Govt. Geologist, sa.\s of
the Ilerherton region —only more so : “A more highly
mineralised area it would be difficult to find anywhere on
the globe.”
* * * *
Mr. Jack says the Hodgkinson was discovered
inopportunely. If found in ’96, it would have been won-
derfully active in ’97. Though 30 to 40 percent, of the
gold is lost, vet the yield on the Hodgkinson is higher
than the yield on the Towers. On Gympie a 2ft. lode
with half an oz. will carry dividends. On the Hodgkin-
son, shafts with a 2ft. lode carrying an oz. have been
abandoned.
* * * •
Q. Mines Under-Secretary Sellheim told the
Mining Commission that in ’96 there were 9606 white
gold-miners (7182 quartz, 2424 alluvial), and 758 Chinese—
a total of 10,364. But only 7927 miners’ rights were
taken out, leaving 2437 men who were gold-mining with-
out rights. “We never compel them to take them out
now,” said Mr. Sellheim. Whereupon the following j
“ Have you never compelled miners to take out miners’
rights?” “Yes, a great many thousand Chinamen.” “Have
you ever compelled a white man to take out a miner’s
right?” “Only on one occasion.” “What were the circum-
stances?” “ It was on the Palmer. The man simply defied
me ; he wanted to make a complaint, and he was taking
up great deal of my time, and I asked him if he had a
miner’s right. He said, ‘Miner’s right be !’ I
ordered him under arrest. In the evening he wanted to
take out a right, but I first fined him ±2 ; it cost him ±2
10s. altogether. When he got about 25 yards away he
said, ‘ I suppose you think you are God Almighty.’ 1
said, ‘ No, I am not that, but I am his first mate in these
parts.’ He cleared out then, and I was not able to get
him. That was the only occasion in 23 years in which I
forced a white man to take out a miner’s right.”
* * » *
One of the warmest mining meetings yet held
in Adelaide happened the other day. It was held in a Sun-
day school. When thelanguage became sulphurous, excited
shareholders would grab prayer-books and assume atti-
tudes of defiance, and ever and anon a shareholder, who
had perhaps called another gentleman a liar, would duck
behind a copy of the Revised Version, and then read out
the Lord’s Prayer and ask his enemy if he had ever heard
that before. The chairman of directors, at the conclusion
of the meeting, expressed his belief in a personal devil,
mentioning a shareholder.
* * * *
Yet another attempt to float a Bendoc sluicing
concern. This time by reference to what Rev. W. B.
Clarke prophesied long years ago. The money lost and
sluicing projects that failed on the Bendoc and Delegate
rivers are either cheerfully forgotten or discreetly omitted.

Pilbarra goldfields return for first quarter of
this year was 1407 0 z., and since then the new Labor
regulations have deprived half the miners of employment.
General Gordon South (W.A.) applying for
exemption. “ The co. is not short of funds, having
several thousands still in hand.” Well, is that any excuse
for sinking it in the ground of what seems a proved
duffer? Why not put it back in the shareholders
pockets . This can also apply to several other local pups.
* * * *
The wonderful developments in the half-dozen
marvellous mines of Kalgoorlie seem to go on uninter-
ruptedly. The world has never seen such a transforma-
tion as this field has revealed by development. The
Boulder regularly keeps a 3oz. average producing over
SOOOoz. a fortnight, and has not yet tackled its tailings.
The Lake View is nearly ready to double its output,
which will mean 12,0000 z. a month, and to date has pro-
duced nearly all its gold from levels, the stopes being
almost intact. The Ivanhoe is lying low pending the
payment of the half-million, which it will probably get.
The Australia blocks have started production in earnest,
and the Kalgurli mine is about to open the “jewel cham-
ber” of tellurides. Brown Hill is putting up more plant,
and the next big producer will probably be the Golden
Horseshoe. Don’t call it Westralia —call it Kalgoorlie.
* * * *
The slander-action against the editoi of a vigor-
ous W. A. paper— Kanowna Democrat— has directed public
gaze on the position of the White Feather Reward. The
editor has been fined “a bob,” but the mine will suffer
by the simple truth being told about it. Three years ago
it was one of the very few good-looking things found in
the country. A long surface chute of 2oz. ore in a big
solid, permanent-looking reef. It held out for about
100 ft.—much deeper than the average W.A. white quartz
mines. Shares have been up to £3, and the battery is
now available for public crushing. Out of Hannan’s
“ too much white quartz.”
* * * *
Coolgardie goes on shrivelling; the business
drifts steadily towards the Boulder. MacDonald, several
times Coolgardie s Mayor, has at length given the older '
town best- has resigned the office and taken his business
with him. The history of Silverton and Broken Hill re-
peated.
* * • •
Meeting to wind up the Fortune of War mine,
W.A., lapsed in Melb. for want of the statutory number
of shareholders. Nearly £30,000 spent without any
return whatever—and the very newspapers that now pub-
lish this little fact blissfully forget the columns used up
by them during the last three years in allowing lies to be
told about this mine.
* * * *
A mine now described as “ the old London
derry,” believed to be somewhere in Coolgardie district,
is “ looking up.” The whole of the white quartz taken
from a winze at the 200 ft. level has crushed 15dwt. to the
ton. Also remarked that recently the management ex-
posed the reef along the surface in the forlorn hope of
picking up some masses of rich stuff like that of the
“ golden hole.” Some 30ft. south of that spot they came
across another hole sunk 20ft. and then carefully filled in.
Sunk right in the centre of the reef and barren of gold !
Which sets the writer ruminating. Was that duffer sunk
by some earlier prospectors who missed that mass of gold
“on which you could hang your hat” glittering in the
noon day’s sun 30ft. away? Not much! Then was it
sunk by the finders of the “ golden hole ” just to see how
she looked ahead, so as not to sell her too cheap? Of
course the hole was carefully filled in. Why leave a dan-
gerous hole lying around for people to fall into ? Perhaps
this consideration actuated them in not sinking any
more along the line. They' stuck to the old hole and it
stuck just long enough to them. “My face is my for-
tune, sir,” she said. No monkeying allowed, no working
option. Probably Fingall, Myring and others did not
want one. No going down. It was a case of the public
going down, and they went and no mistake. Mostly on
their gold the finders are keeping the wolf from the door.
Myring, ex-rabhit inspector, is keeping a yacht in Eng-
land ; Fingall’s Irish estate has largely received the
benefit of his profits; the co. has declined to take another
mine in consideration ; Col. North is dead ; and the mine
is said to be in a state of resurrection !

From Westralia : You may be interested to hear
that the Great Fingall Co.’s battery of 30 head, which was
completely erected, bar about one steam-pipe, some six
months ago, has been sold to go to Niagara (for the Cos-
mopolitan Leases, I believe). I do not know the price,
but it is probably pretty cheap. Most of this machinery
was carted from Southern Cross at a cost of £9O per ton.
About 1000 tons of stone on the dumps.

Rev Joseph Campbell gave in Melb., recently, a
full exposition of his Thermo-Hype'phoric Ore Treatment
—a further development of the Lockwood-Chappie pro-
cess—a sort of water-gas system. He has gone to Maori-
land to practically demonstrate it. Other men are leav-
ing the church for the mining expert business with gold-
saving processes, in which the ever-incredulous cognos-
centi have about as much faith as they would have in a
new patent soul-saver
* * * •
The claws of the true democrat will appear, try
to conceal them however he may, even through judicial
gloves. Witness this fine piece of democracy in a judg-
ment of Higinhotham’s (the accuracy of the law can’t be
guaranteed): “ The labor-covenants in a gold-mining
lease are the real consideration for the lease, and should
he strictly construed.” What do the cheerful British
boomsters say to this—particularly Charters Towers
Mills and Co., who approached Q. Attorney-General
Byrnes in London with a petition that leases should be
non-forfeitnble for breach of labor conditions?

Dear Bulletin, —Re the decision of the Eng.
Roval Commission on Agriculture, that imported (Aus-
tralian) mutton 'must be legibly labelled when offered for
sale The bread-eater who lacks the brain-power neces-
sary to distinguish the home-grown and foreign article is
to he protected against the colonials —this, too, after all
our grovel and silken-bond blather. In plainer words
England, a so-called Freetrade stronghold, admits an
article free at port of entry, but levies a Protective tax on
it before it can be offered to the British public—the Aus-
tralian exporter being the loser in all cases. As pauper-
food, the merino is beastly good, doncherknow; as an
article of diet for the cultured—well! Why not brand it
before shipping—“ Made in Australia? ” England’s Aliens
Restriction Bill! — Veldt.

society
The Australian public is getting very tired
of the cable syndicate responsible for the simul-
taneous publication in all the “great” dailies of
trivial items interesting to one province only.
Readers of Sydney papers, for instance, were
lately stoked with a lot of dreary slush about
M.L. and S. A. nobodies who had temporarily
distinguished themselves by winning two-guinea
prizes at Bislev. Also, it was noted that the Vic-
torian, Turner, was given cablegraphic preference
overall the other Premiers. Reason—the cable-
men are both Victorian pressmen, and they are
directly controlled from Melbourne —two things
that will bring about a burst-up of the amalga-
mation sooner or later.
* * * ♦
First impressions of the returned Premier
Turner were not very satisfactory. He came back
to Melbourne in the same old togs, it is true, but
nobody who saw him ride along Collins-street,
gravely acknowledging the inaudible plaudits of a
multitude which wasn’t there, will be disposed to
believe that Turner’s tweeds enclosed the same old
man who went ’Ome some months ago. Unkind
critics will probably say that Turner bore himself
in the barouche like one gone somewhat mad on
the subject of his self-importance. If a.casual
small boy contemplated him with a stony stare
from the kerbstone, Turner doffed his boxer to the
urchin in an imperial style. He was palpably
imagining a non-existent demonstration all along
the line of route, which was about the last mistake
in the world that the original George Turner
would have been likely to make. His friends, no
doubt, will blame the Ministry in general for this
farcical spectacle. Isaacs, Best and co. travelled
down to Bacchus Marsh railway-station to meet
their chief, and prepare him for a “ magnificent
greeting.” Judging from the scowling faces of
Isaacs, Best and co., they saw the absurdity of
their slow procession up the unexcited street, and
were sorry they hadn’t smuggled Turner to the
Town Hall in a hansom cab.
Be missions. The State (remarks a well-
informed Bulletin correspondent) ought to pro-
hibit the senseless sacrifice of hysterical females
on the altar of Baal, or the L.M.S., or any other
such institution. In Papua, in addition to the
fearful ravages of the climate on the female con-
stitution—especially on that of the unmarried
girl—the barbarous costumes and customs of the
natives, often indescribably disgusting even io the
unholy trader, supply another good reason for the
abolition of this traffic. But especially stupid
and luridly insane appears the .attempt to convert
Papuans to some brand of Christianity or other
by means of unmarried female teachers, as the
native simply, from the pinnacle of his particular
moral prejudices, utterly despises an unattached
girl. The value of their services may, therefore,
easily be gauged.

The unexpected again. Vic. W.C.T.U.
has “determined to recommend the spread of
carefully-prepared purity literature to scl col-
teachers, through the medium of W.C.T.U.
branches, and to recommend the teaching of prac-
tical physiology in a way suitable to boys and
girls.” If the members of the W.C.T.U. could be
suspected of conscious humor, The Bulletin
would look upon the foregoing as a successful
specimen of their waggery. Meanwhile, it waits
to see how they will Bowdlerise “ practical physi-
ology ” so as to make it “ suitable to boys and
girls.”

♦ * * *
Vic. Chief-Justice Madden is rapidly gain-
ing a reputation for epigram in divorce cases.
One, in which several co-respondents figured,
elicted the remark from him that the lady was
“ evidently unsuited to the exclusiveness of mar-
ried life.” On another occasion he observed that
“ The respondent had, no doubt, felt the ennui
which assailed all married people from time to
time.” Which last self-evident statement would
seem to indicate that his bewaxed Honor is pre-
pared to apply the principle of divorce on a very
wide scale indeed.
It is a beautiful and touching commentary
on allegedlv-Christian practice that many of the
biggest churches, mortgaged to the very‘apex of
their steeples, are sacking paid bell-ringers, organ-
iBts, &c. ; the übiquitous bun-struggle and bazaar
have to be run for all they are worth to rake in
money enough to keep the show going, and some
ministers, rather than submit to the ‘ special
effort ” humiliation, forego a slice of their stipends
every year. Verily the Raws is sick, and the
pietist waxes too mean to pay for his own sal-
vation.

The world will not be humorless while
nature provides boarders. Before a Melbourne
suburban court: —
Witness : Yes, I am married to the woman who was
my housekeeper, another man is supporting my children.
Mr. Kelly: Will you swear that?—Well, complainant
is living in a house with one boarder. It stands to reason.
The bench made an order for ss. per week for each
child, with £2 7s. costs ; defendant to find one surety in
£2O.

Recent Sydney advertisement :—“ Piano :
Young lady will receive or visit pupils 2 hours

A Sydney bookshop, which often
shows rare volumes in its window, lately
displayed a very old and massive Bible,
opened at random and, as it happened,
at a chapter in Revelations wherein some
very gaudy obscenity appears. A clergy-
man, in passing, saw and read the awful
passage, which was in large enough print
to stop a whole presbytery on its way to
meeting. Horrified, the good man rushed
inside and drew the proprietor’s atten-
tion to the fact. “ Oh, dear ! oh, dear ! ”
exclaimed the bookseller, “now I shall
have to re-dress the whole window ! ”

H. : “I suppose the Protestant
mission field is somewhat equivalent to
the nunnery, and even the monastery, for
broken hearts. Here is a case given to
me on undoubted oral authority, but I am
not aware if it has appeared in print.
A Miss went from England to India,
some years ago, and devoted herself to
the lepers. Last year she had a furlough,
and visited her mother in England. There
she developed leprosy, beginning only
with a whiteness under one finger -naii.
Immediately she returned to'lndia, and
has segregated herself with the lepers for
ever. Miss Bruce, of Melbourne, was in
grave danger in the heart of India, by
last advices. She is alone, as a white,
in a compound where the famine-stricken
are attacked by the plague. Conse-
quently, no other persons are allowed to
go there. Miss Brulb has managed to
send a brave letter to her Meec. friends.”
The “ social news ” published in some
Maoriland newspapers from their “own corres-
pondents” in “the bush” is often deliciously
naive and breathes an arcadian simplicity not
wotted of in the other provinces. Examples :
. . . Mrs. Long, being an expert musician, is an
acquisition to this district, and her valuable services will
be availed of. . . . Mr. Long is a butcher, and will
run a cart up the coast, when no doubt he will do a good
business.
. . . There were present about 14 couples who gaily
tripped the light fantastic to the musical strains of the
Komata Orchestra, consisting of two accordeons and a
mouth-organ.
It is estimated that the average cost of
getting divorced in any part of Australia is just
ten times the average cost of getting married. 'The
lawyers ahead again.
Miss Ethel R. Benjamin,
LL.B., who enjoys the distinc-
tion of being the first Maoriland
lady barrister, was born and ;
educated in Dunedin, aiid is now
22 years old. Miss Benjamin
took first place in tin provincial
E location Board Scholarship in
’B7, matriculated from the Girls’
High School in ’93, and gradua-
ted in law this year from Otago
University, where she was placed
first in the first-class div sion in
jurisprudence and in constitu-
tional history and law respec-
tively. She also headed the
Maoriland list in Roman Law,
Evidence, and Equity respec-
tively, and was bracketed first
with another in Criminal Law
and real and personal property
respectively. Miss Benjamin
was admitted as a barrister and
solicitor of the Supreme Court of
Maoriland last April, and has
commenced practice in her native
city.

An unusual instance of a matron’s fall
from grace lately came before an Australian
Divorce Court. About 20 years after her marriage
in Scotland the wife took to drink and complained
that her new life in Australia was too slow for
her. She “ wanted admiration,” and couldn’t get
enough of it at her quiet suburban residence. So
she left the old man, and went back to Scotland,
where he subsequently met the middle-aged truant
“got up in such very gaudy colors that he would
not have known her if she had not been pointed
out to him.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From flowers faded long ago
The printed page has caught a stain ;
And, dreaming in the fire-light’s glow,
Old scenes come hack to me again.
The land-breeze blew across the broom,
Just as the white moon-crescent set;
You wore—the fairest in the room —
This daffodil and violet!
Il’e left the whirling, joyous dance ;
Unheeded might the music play
When wanton Cupid sent the chance
For you and me to steal away.
Ah, Youth is seldom over-wise !
And principles are wondrous weak
When glad lights shine in great grey eyes
And warmer gloivs a rose-dusk cheek !
The sky was pale with starry light ;
With beads of dew the lawn was wet;
On your white breast you wore that night
'1 his daffodil and violet!
0, new buds bloom as old ones die —
Those tender griefs are gone, and yet
Your sweet, faint odours breathe a sigh,
Dear daffodil and violet !

Dear Bulletin, —.Re the recent “bles-
sing ”of the Sydney Catholic Press plant. Any
R.C. who bids for clerical patronage in this way
must be very optimistic as to the permanency of
his business. It is a church rule never to sell any-
thing which has been accorded a formal priestly
benediction, so that the Press proprietors will be
within easy distance of Excommunication should
their machinery at any time be made the basis of
a cash transaction. —T.D.
At a Melb. suburban Mayor’s ball the
other evening, one of the guests was shaking hands
and bidding good night to the secretary, when a
full bottle of whisky unfortunately slipped out of
his waistcoat to the floor. He is not feeling very
well at present; in fact, he is much too ill to
attend the return ball.

An association of honorarium magistrates,
with a club-room in the city, is mooted in Melb.
What a glorious institution this would have been
in the palmy days of Rapiport and Baxter ! Clients
could interview beaks, beaks could make terms
with clients, and all would go merry as a marriage
bell. Name of club—“ Verdict Exchange.”

The noisy announcements of the open-
handed generosity of the badged and aproned
confraternity received a homely illustration up
North, the other day. The local brotherhood
announced a ball in aid of the local hospital, and
in due course 350 persons participated in the.
benevolent enterprise. But the money handed
over, when all accounts were settled, came to <£B
19s. Gd., or just about 3d. a piece. “ The noble
charity of our Order ! ”

even ask for official permission to marry Even
then he may not get the desired leave. The
department’s plea is that married and familied
policemen constitute a heavy drag on the “ mobi-
lity ” of the “ foorce.”

Damages for taking too much
skin is the basis of an action just
now pending in an Australian city.
T 1 ic trouble took place in an hospi-
tal. A patient was brought in
suffering from a frightful burning
accident, and, while the doctor
was attending him, a group of
pious visitors came in and wanted
to give the writhing, blackened
human cinder a tract. The doctor
said, indignantly, that it was skin,
not tracts, that was wanted, and
the pious deputation was so far
moved out of its usual groove that
its members agreed to contribute
a bit of cuticle apiece to graft on
the, sufferer. The medical gentle-
man accepted the offer and took a
sample from each tract-distributor,
and, at latest advices, one young
lady, who claims that she was
flayed recklessly and on too large
a scale, proposed to claim heavy
damages for assault, also battery
with variations. As a side issue,
it is claimed that she is a minor,
and was more or less skinned with-
out the consent of her parents,
and, altogether, the situation is
complicated.
Broken Hill has a Labor-party of two in
its municipal council. The meetings are held at
2.30 in the afternoons. One of the party is a
miner ; and when he is on day-shift his colleague
at prodigious length discusses the minutes and
stonewalls on the correspondence until at 4 o’clock
the other member knocks off work, leaves his crib-
can at the council chamber door, and takes his
seat. Then the real business is suffered to begin.

and calmly replied: “No,

“ The Guildhall Ball, the jubilee event of
a jubilee Mayor, was a scene of magnificent patchi-
ness and chaotic brilliancy,” writes an Australian
abroad to The Bulletin. “His Grace and His
Grease were there in both sexes; the former
clumpy in the legs and rickety in the eye, the
latter exudingly unctuous in his obeisance to him.
Westocracy, city-ocracy and suburbooracy were
there in all their pleasure-paint and shoddy
stencilling, but snobocracy claimed in loud, tin-
kettling tones a large majority of the 4400 said to
be present. Six or seven rooms were used for the
toe-skipping performance, at which there wei*e
more shirkers than workers; three or four-rooms
supplied refreshments, and three single.-hour con-
cert programmes were inflicted on crowded audi-
ences in the central chamber one after another.
The catering, in Sydney or Melbourne, would
have sent the responsible Mayor back to his bqrreL
bung or sheep-slicing at the very next election.
Just a rough and ready stand-up struggle whero
he who tipped wisely and well tippled freely and
often, and the uncanny man got a sore throat
from the dry, nutmeg-grater sides rubbing together
in chafing irritation. Dishes of dismembered
poultry and shabby chips of ham were hurled
broadcast on the table, and all speared at them
with their forks in unison ; if you were 10 seconds
behind, you impaled the dish pattern and cursed
the successful. The champagne ?—well, I trust
I’m too much of a gentleman to refer to the mis-
fortunes of others. The poverty of that wine was
something to be obliterated by a ‘ two of gin.’”

Dear Bulletin, — Re the Lascar’s reli-
ability in emergency, I think most of his reputa-
tion for the lack thereof is due simply to cold.
A low-air temperature he appears to stand fairly 1
well, but the wash of a cold sea—rapidly Cxtrac- '
tive enough of a white man’s vitality— seems’ ’
simply to shrivel the Lascar up, as if from the ’
effect of intense heat. Instance ; Voyaging Once”
in a “country ” (locally-built) ship from Rangoon ”
to Gilolo, we fell in with one of the comparati vely
cold S.E. blows you sometimes get thereabouts
before the change of monsoon —carried away our •
foremast, and had a lively time, with seas break- 1
ing clean over us at intervals for about 36 hours,
at the end of which period two Lascars of the '
crew were dead, and other three all but,- though
the rest (Javanese and Chinkies) were little the
worse. An instance of what I have known the >
Lascar do when the soul wasn’t chilled out of him
I have embodied in a yarn which has been in your
possession for over two years, and which cannot
therefore have been vamped-up to meet the ease.— :
Alex. M. . >

Upon this page are shown portraits of
Drs. Ramsay Smith and Leith Napier, Resident
Surgeon and Resident Physician at Adelaide
Hospital, who have been recently outlawed by the
British Medical Association for their connection
with the hospital trouble. This famous quarrel
began three years ago in the simplest fashion
possible. Dr* Perks, then Medical Superinten-
dent, chose Miss Gordon, a sister of the then Chief
Secretary, as Superintendent of Night Nurses,
and when a charge of favoritism was preferred,
because a number of senior nurses had been passed
over, he asserted that, she was “ the only charge ■
nurse ” fit to take the position. This led to an
indignant protest by a number of the nurses senior
to Miss Gordon, and, their conduct being con-
sidered contumelious, they were called upon to
apologise. All did so except Miss Hawkins, a
charge nurse, and Miss Graham, a probationer,
and these two were dismissed by the Board.
Thereupon Parliament intervened, the result
being the appointment of a Royal Commis-
sion to enquire into the matter. The
Commission reported that Miss Gordon should
not have been appointed, but that, as she
was not in any way to blame, she should be con-
tinued in office. It ordered, however, the re-
instatement both of Miss Hawkins and Miss
Graham—provided, however, that both the ladies
should first apologise to the Board ; and this they
did in the form prescribed. Notwithstanding
this, the Board refused to reinstate them, so when
its term of office expired the Govt, did not re-
appoint any of the members —who, it may be said,
just before they vacated their seats had put back
Miss Hawkins under protest. The new Board,
taking office in March, 1895, at once reinstated
Miss Graham. Then began the troubles with the.
hon. medical and surgical staff. They demanded
(1) that l")r. Hynes, a non-member of the South
Australian branch of the British Medical
Association, who had been given a seat on
the Board, should be called on to resign ;
(2) that they should have representation on the
Board; and (.3) that Miss Graham should
be removed. l)r. Hynes, for the sake of peace,
resigned, and the desiderated representation
on the Board was offered; but the Govern-
ment definitely refused to send Miss Graham
avvay. So the members of the hon. staff for-
warded their resignations and gave the Govern-
ment to understand that on a certain date named
they would vacate their positions. Applications
for resident medical men were at once invited;
and Govt, medical officers, with volunteers from
among the city doctors who placed humanity
before “ professional etiquette,” went into the
Hospital to look after the patients when the
original staff left. Drs. Leith Napier and Ramsay
Smith, who were chosen in England, arrived
about 15 months ago. The jealous Adelaide
brethren moved the B.M.A. to erase their names
from its membership-list—which was recently
done. And Drs. Smith and Napier, incredible as
it may seem, still survive ; and the Adelaide Hos-
pital receives and heals patients precisely as for
merly—or possibly a little better than formerly.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _ „ Sydney, August 24, 1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
Miss Flora Shaw, who has been so much slated
over the famous S. African telegrams, was a
much-fussed-over celebrity when she visited Aus-
tralia, two or three years ago. It wasn’t solely
the great paper that she represented that in safety
lighted her round this green isle; she reduced
several public men to dust and ashes, and press-
men were as wax in her hand. A story is told
u°xr* Shavv conversing with a member of
the Victorian Cabinet during a train-journey; at
first, the great man answered her queries on his
own particular topic with smiling condescension,
but by the journey’s end he was limp and flabby,
and mopping his fevered brow. The lady had
tackled him on his own ground, and fairly beaten
him off the field. She has a large following still.
One correspondent wails in print about the rude-
ness of the picture-paper artists who drew her in
court as a “plain, ill-dressed woman,” and
described her as “elegance itself.” As a matter
of fact, you wouldn’t remark her clothes; she’s
neither a doll nor a dowd, but a plainly-dressed,
slender, dark-eyed woman, with much charm of
manner and illimitable “go”—in some respects
strongly recalling the universally-admired Mrs.
Harry M‘Quade, of Sydney. At Bendigo are
still tenderly preserved a lengthy pair of over-
shoes, pointed out as the shoes worn by Miss Shaw
when making her descent into a celebrated mine ;
the shelf on which the goloshes stand is wide, but
there isn’t much room for any other bric-k-brae
on it.
A novel scheme for mammoth self-advertising
has been hit upon by a wealthy and clever London
woman, by name Mrs. Phillips. Her idea is to
form a sort of “ combine ” of women’s clubs of
every variety of usefulness or uselessness, and
call it the “Women’s Institute.” The plan of
operations is bewilderingly vague and comprehen-
sive. It contemplates keeping in stock lady-lec-
turers on all sorts of topics ready to be turned on
at a moment's notice, and a special feature is a
question-answering bureau,” where anxious en-
quirers may get recipes for raspberry-jam or re-.
moving freckles ; also, a lobbyist is to be employed
to look after the great “ woman question ” should
it crop up in bills before the Commons. A ramb-
ling yarn in the S.M. Herald a few days ago gave
these facts embodied in much language. How
very flowery the new woman is ! In the verbiage
of the first pars., there are traces of the “ noble
aims crystallised to glorious fulfilment ” which
flittered among the gems of speech in the Austral
alon prospectus, written by Miss Hirst Brown
(Mrs. Alexander). She is now Hyde Parking it
among the London social-reformers, and was the
first to boom Mrs, Phillips’ plar the Australian
press.
What does a woman's club amount to, anyhow?
The enthusiasts gird at men’s clubs for being the
“grave of ideals, sepulchral monuments of selfish
seeking of comfort where any notions of serving
the progress of mankind would be withered in the
atmosphere.” Sounds like Mrs. Caudle, and the
very thing that nagged-at man wants to get away
from. But all the ideals promulgated by the
women’s clubs as yet are the drawing-room fetishes
of social agonies and light refreshment. The Vic-
toria Salon is crowded every Friday afternoon
with a well-behaved and befrilled crowd showing
the chastened joy of an all-women gathering;
after a burst of chatter over tea-and-scones, they
depart as from an afternoon call. It is a success
socially, but doesn’t take much stock in ideals.
The evening debating struggles to which men are
attracted are much more lively musterings.
One scheme that means business is worth dozens
of organisations with their heads in the clouds and
no practical foothold. The proposed registry-office
for qualified teachers and governesses reads like
something with sense in it. The means of bring-
ing employer and employed together in the
governessing business are vague and muddled.
There is still clinging to the occupation the “ gen-
teel” notion that a “young lady ” would only go
out and teach among friends. If the barnacles of
“kind recommendation and patronage” by “the
Nicest only ” are to be cleared away, a well-
organised employment bureau managed by edu-
cated and disinterested women is the thing to do
it. It would save many ill-spared, fruitless regis-
tration fees on the part of girls seeking employ-
ment ; might, in time, raise salaries and become a
Governesses’ Union. Such a body, properly run on
trade-union lines, would benefit both teacher and
taught and is only rendered impossible by false
t professional ” pride which refuses to sink to
“ trade ” devices for protection.
A smart Melb. presswoman seized on Mrs.
Turner, on her return, and wormed cut facts
about her English tour from the newly-arrived
Premieress. She declared that her five weeks
among the jubilators were one continuous hustle—
only one day for shopping in the month, but pro-
bably hubby Turner is thanking his stars for that.
And she marvels at the price of “ things ” at the
head centre. They have a sliding scale of prices
m the Big Smoke. To sail into a palatial store
(per Royal carriage) and give Hotel Cecil as an
address is to invite pillage. As an unlabelled per-
son she could have ravaged the July sales for half
the money. Also, she wipes the vaunted lilies
and roses off the cheeks of J. Bull’s wife and
daughters by saying that those imaginary poetic
vegetable hues do wipe off. The London com-
plexion she pronounces a “fake.” The lady’s
maid must be a rouge-and-powder artist or she
isn’t worth her salt.
Local complexion-menders call their art face-
massage. Nearly every Nice woman now under-
goes a course of treatment by having her face par-
boiled over a steam-bath, some vigorous pound-
ings by the artist’s fingers, and a succession of
washes squirted on her features from a vaporiser.
Some trained nurses add this profitable branch to
their biz. with joyful results. The final touch-up
with color to cheek and eyebrow is optional, but
even without it, the patient looks years younger
and cleaner after the operation.
The Mayoress had a tea-and-tattle struggle on
Friday afternoon. Guests arrived in large num-
bers and shook hands with Mrs. Ives in one of the
side-rooms of the Town Hall. In another, music
and singing went on undisturbed By this wise
arrangement the performers were heard, as the
cackle was minimised by being distributed through
three rooms. No eye-arresting gowns were pre-
sent. August is the lowest of ebb-tides in frock-
ing. Winter dresses have been trotted out at this
month's shivoo preparatory to their taking their
departure before the coining spring glories.
The slow poison which lurks in the deadly
buttered muffin was pointed out by Dr. Dagmar
Berne on Monday afternoon at her lecture on In-
digestion—one of the series “for ladies only,”
arranged by the Sanitary Association. The room
was crowded, and some interested listeners had
to mount back seats to get a glimpse of the lectur-
ess, who discoursed on victuals and their often fatal
results. Among other things she had a slap at
vegetarianism, and reprobated the idea of confin-
ing one’s diet to grass. The audience frequently
applauded the sentiments, and then the great
majority went home to preside over the same old
meal of hot buttered toast and cold corned beef,
washed down by scalding tea, and to wonder why
the old man seems to be getting more and more
grey and haggard with despepsia as the years
roll on.
On Tuesday Dr. Muskett delivered an appeal to
his fellow-countrymen not to eat so much meat,
but to fill up on salads. But somehow Austra-
lians despise all sorts of grass, just as they despise
wine when beer and whisky are available. The
vegetable John’s “welly ni’lettucee,” makes cus-
tomers sniff suspiciously ; they doubt the cleanli-
ness and abjure all salad stuff in consequence.
A dreamy cradle-song, labelled simply “ Ber-
ceuse,” has been sent me. The words, both
French and English, are prettily poetic, and are
set to music by Liza Lehmann, whose name stands
for charming melody always. Another new song,
“ Trust me for ever ” (words by Algernon Linds,
music by John D. Davis), is an unconvincing little
wail which seems to pursue a small tune and fail
signally to overtake it. W. J. Ellis, of New-
castle, has “ dedicated by kind permission ” to
Her Ex. a really tuneful waltz which possesses big
chances of becoming popular. It certainly de-
serves a more attractive and imaginative title
than the “Brand Waltz,” which it bears, and,
although there is nothing strikingly original about
the composition, every bar of it is distinctly dance-
able.
At the Hambourg concerts, which it is the cor-
rect thing to attend and afterwards prattle about,
it is amusing to note the reserved-seat fillers
drowse dreamily during the long fantasias and
thunderous polonaises crashed forth by Mark, and
then shake themselves together and clap vocifer-
ously. The smart way to clap is to beat the, hands
together, holding them well up at about nose-level
so that barbarians sitting around may see the
impulsive joy which the clapper feels when the
performer leaves off. Most of the real musical
enthusiasm comes from the back-seats, where the
audience are mostly students and ardent amateurs
who go for love of their hobby or to study their
art. Admirers of the two brothers Hambourg
have now placed Jakoff on a pedestal beside
Mark : they say he is as soulful. The fiddle is a
torturesome implement if not wisely treated, and
little “Jasher” is putting more and more soul
into his playing at every concert. Mark is all
head, and his brother all heart. On Saturday
evening, at the end of the show, Manager Lem-
mone brought forward the two Hambourgs and
presented them to the filing-out Govt. House
party. Gov. Hampden seized each by his hand,
dropped it hastily, and walked on ; then the pro-
cession, which had come to a momentary stand-
still, re-formed, and the youthful geniuses right-
about-faced for the ante-room. The curtailed
little ceremony fell very flat.
A Maoriland paper publishes, at the tail of much
flourishing description, a wilted little interview
with its new Gov. Ranfurley, obtained, ostensibly,
at Sydney by a special reporter sent in the Govt,
steamer Tutenaki. Following the example of the
Argus, which scored by despatching the highest-
toned young man on its staff to meet the arriving
Sunbeam at Albany, the N. 2. Times envoy
journeyed hither. But his plans of worming state
secrets out of the vice-regal breast evidently didn’t
work. The printed interview contains a column
and a half of original foaming billows and seasick-
ness and one stick of alleged personal talk which
had all been remarked in other papers davs before.
The Maoriland hireling can’t have possessed the
savoir faire of the Melb. pressman, who was done
by his subject on velvet, had a special man told
off to wait on him, dined with the family and was
asked to Lady B. ’s own den for afternoon tea.
. The spring exhibition of the Society of Artists
is to include, beside the exhibits in the legitimate,
various side-shows; one will be a display of
posters, the very latest fashionable artistic craze.
Many examples of the street pictures—chiefly
from Paris—which beautify city hoardings will be
on view. Also, there will be exhibits of wood,
leather and china, hand-painted and otherwise
adorned by art. The two rival annual shows will
this year be almost next door to each other. No. 70
Pitt-street is the home of the old original Art
Society, and its front door will be officially
opened to the public by His Ex., on 11th Sept.
The Society of Artists has hung up its “God-
bless-our-home ” at 7fi Pitt-street, and sports its oak
until 2nd Oct. Among other attractions there was
to have been played an original operetta intro-
ducing local artistic celebrities ; but complications
have arisen which make intending performers’
plans without form and void..
Mrs. Eddy, widow of the late Commissioner of
Railways, is having more than her share of trouble.
Last week she received news that her 16-year-old
son, Edward, visiting a station near Nevertire,
had met with a serious accident. Young Eddy
was thrown from his horse and rendered uncon-
scious. but he is fortunately now recovering.
A Sydney girl has just distinguished herself at
the Royal Academy Art School in London, by
being the first Australian student'to gain a scholar-
ship there, thus winning free tuition for three
years from the best painters and sculptors. Miss
Allison Vivian was prepared in Sydney for her
exam, at the Moore-Jones studio.
Sydney girls were badly beaten at golf at the
Bondi links by a Victorian team of lady putters
and drivers. Down South the ambulatory sport
has been the vogue for years. Enthusiastic
women have lugged out to Caulfield heavy checks
ever since the ilopetouns made it fashionable to
be Scotch, but it’s quite a new start for the local
Nicest girls. They have not had as much prac-
tice as the Southerners, and, besides, they seem to
become tired sooner, and want to sit down and
admire the view between their drives at the
minute ball, which has an exasperating way of
never answering its helm. The new club-house is
to be opened on Saturday afternoon by Gov.
Hampden, who is ex-officio devoted to the game.
The necessary weapons are so numerous and so
expensive that it is the hall-mark of swelldom to
play “goff.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ ’Tis rumored that the crisp, go-ahead daughter
of a southern Governor keeps # a tight hand on the
domestic reins of the Big House during the tem-
porary retirement of its lawful mistress. The
same pretty young woman, with totally different
coloring, facially reproduces her canny father’s
small, firm mouth and decided nose, and looks a
long time at every shilling (considering her youth).
Had the lady not been bom with a golden spoon
in her mouth, she would, probably, have proved
herself possessed of the family money-making, as
she certainly is of its money-saving, knack.
“ The musicians who perform from time to time
at Lady Brassey’s private ‘At Homes’ are not
paid a fee. Things are worked on much more
economical lines. Lord Neville writes a suave
letter to an artist intimating that her ladyship is
giving an At Home on such a date, and would she
(or he) kindly help in the entertainment by con-
tributing a few items ? When the artist signifies a
willing hand, she (or he) receives a formal invita-
tion to her musical At Home. The artist gives up
the afternoon to trill or jingle in the vice-regal
drawing-room. Lord Shaftesbury offers up a
ballad, the Freeman-Thomases take small parts in
the harmonious struggle and —everybody is de-
lighted.
“ By the way, I am told that many of the pro-
fessional musicians who charge three and five
guineas for a performance either in public or
private earn be persuaded to charge half that sum
on condition that they are put down or announced
as giving their services gratuitously. Verily,
* Work is Disgraceful.’
“At present, Austral Salonites are suffering a
reaction after the hopes and fears, jealousies and
baffled ambitions inseparable from the annual vice-
regal visitation which happened on Thursday
night (August 19). The Governor and Mrs.
Freeman Thomas came as joint substitutes for
Lady Brassey, who is trying to hurry up her con-
valescence in Sydney sunshine. His Ex., who
was down on tire programme for a reading, but
absconded from the platform after a short speech,
was wheedled on again to render a few lines of
Stevenson’s amidst a storm of feminine applause.
“ Mdlle. Dreyfus gave her first conference at
Guvmentouse this season, under the auspices of the
Brasseys, on Tuesday nigtit, which was miserably
cold and wet. The attendance was weak, the
audience sympathetic, the lecture—just as lectures
go. Miss Buckley (the amateur, not the clever
professional) played a pianoforte jingle, to enliven
matters ; and some of the intellectual suggest that
a little singing be introduced anon. Perhaps Mr.
Rickards will be solicited by Lady Brassey to
kindly lend the Selbinis, or somebody else from
the Opera House firmament, to vary the bill.
“ On Friday (20th), at St. Kilda Town Hall,
Premier Turner and family underwent the inspec-
tion of loyal constituents, assembled at the bidding
of the local Mayor. Onlookers agreed unani-
mously that ‘ the old man himself,’ as he is called
in his own suburb, was the least altered of the
party. He still clings to tweeds and bob-tail
jackets, his eyes are as round as ever, and, when
not pump-handling supporters, his clasped hands
hang straight down in front in the old, meek way.
Nevertheless, in spite of these lovable landmarks,
the wanderer has embellished his social demeanor
by a few subtle airs and graces. At the Town
Hall that afternoon, though palpably worn, weary,
and fit for bed, he persevered with his nods and
becks and wreathed smiles until untravelled
brother politicians, looking on open-mouthed, felt
themselves wooden oafs iti comparison.
( “ Hi* womankind, of course, go one better than
Pa,’ and, at the reception, bowed from the waist
and shook hands from the shoulder, and smiled so
constantly and expansively that spectators ached
in every muscle for sympathy. Other women, en-
viously discussing their almost Parisian vivacity
of address, surmised that deportment, like every-
thing else, can be easily acquired in London, and
that some grande dame, less reposeful than any of
the dear Princesses, must have served the apt
Australiennes for a model.
With Mrs. Turner’s silver - flowered navy
blue gown, she wore a cute little zouave, slashed
with white lace and touched with turquoise blue
over a bodice of cream moire and lace. Her pale-
grey straw toque was smothered in white trim-
mings like a young widow’s wedding bonnet. Her
daughter had pink poplin and pink tartan silk
Both gowns stood out in stiff, well-built folds at
the back, and had the trigr French waists and
assertive curves elsewhere, too seldom seen out-
side fashion-books. Meantime, while the guests
of the day sparkled and gushed, their hosts
languished on their dais hard by, and by contrast
looked as immobile as the figures in successful
tableaux vivants.
“The festive hats and gowns brought back to
our shores by the wives and daughters of jubilating
Australian politicians look over gay and floral
in our present wintry weather. No doubt they
showed to better advantage in London on that
roasting 22nd June, when the ambulances were
full of “ sunstroke ” cases, Last Saturday, two of
the returned wanderers were on view, flitting in
and out of the bargain-shops at the unfashionable
end of Bourke-street. They were resplendent aa
to their heads, but their frocks, in their first youth,
could never have attained even Melbourne high-
water mark.
“ The Mayor of one of Melbourne’s many
1 cities ’ is a thoroughly practical admirer of the
New Woman. Lately a lady medico hung out her
red lamp in the neighborhood, whereupon the
civic potentate went off and purchased a ticket for
the complimentary return ball, which was about
to be tendered to him, and sent it along to the
fair practitioner as a means of introducing her to
a large circle of desirable future patients. She
came, saw, and conquered. Dozens of men in that
district are prepared to catch the first mild com-
plaint that offers, and call in the dear kind doctor,
whose bright smile haunts them still.
“ The ‘ splendid match ’ which a popular Aus-
tralian stage lady was supposed to have struck
turns out, I regret to hear, to be not a bit more
splendid than the average matrimonial experience
of the average actress who is wooed with floral
tributes. Having said this much, to enter into
details would be superfluous.
“ The latest sensation in Block identities is a
damsel of the bean-pole order, approaching six
feet, with ebon locks. On special days the lofty
stranger comes forth in a shot skirt and blouse of
orange-hued silk, with lavish trimming of bright,
grass-green velvet. To add a little more variety
the blaze of color is crowned by a large hat-load of
flowers, with a huge rose-tinted bow towering
above the structure.
“All the sweet, womanly sympathy of a Southern
suburb is just now directed towards the interest-
ing, young, High Church curate, who has been
cruelly and relentlessly jilted by his lady-love, just
h,s he was dreaming of wedding-bells. The story
goes that the lady turned from her faithful curate
to look at a lord.
“Miss Mary Callanan, artist and postu-
lant, was completing a life-size portrait of the lats
Signor Cecchi when his death put an end to tha
sittings. The painter, with a pretty air *f senti-
ment and sacrifice, has left the work unfinished
(the friends of the dead Signor can’t persuade her
to apply the finishing-touches), and a funeral
drapery hangs around the canvas in token of her
feelings on the subject. The picture will per-
haps go out of mourning at the twelvemonth.
Miss Callanan, an attractive brunette, is a fair
Italian scholar, having learnt the language from
poor old Cecchi.
“Dr. J. Harbinson, the estimable bacnelor-poet
of Brighton, has embodied the public spasm of
regret at Baronet Clarke’s death in an In
Menxoriam dirge, sent and inscribed to the widow.
It does not transpire how that sorrowing lady re-
garded the ode, but, for the present, she will keep
it, like all her belongings, in the dark. The
‘ original ’ Lady Clarke, as some people call her,
has taken her weeds and family to Franks ton to
the temporary seclusion of a picturesque villa.

brought a net profit of £217 for the charities,
thanks to the fact that principals and chorus—
about 100 in all —performed gratis. The balance-
sheet, which reveals gross takings of close upon
£lOOO, doesn’t state whether the Govt. House
crowd departed from their usual custom by cash-
ing up for their seats in this instance. Probably
they didn’t. Lord Brassey, at any rate, has

“In Lady Hopetoun’s early Melbourne days,
she once ventured down Collins-street on foot, bub
was so stared at and dogged from pillar to post
by a crowd of inquisitive women that she fled
through a shop back-door, and never again in-
spected the city without bringing along a carriage
strongly fortified by flunkeys, as a basis of retreat.
“Baron Brassey’s womankind, untroubled by
shyness, bike into the heart of the town ‘on their
own ’ whenever they want a box of hairpins, and
dismount from a dog -cart for a refresher in a tea-
room, without a misgiving. Last Saturday morn-
ing, Mrs. Freeman Thomas, in a royal-purple
coat and skirt, tripped briskly through the traffic
with hubby at her heels. After all, ‘Shanks’;
pony’ is the safest mode of transit in this demo-
cratic land, where a Governor’s lady in her landau
may be staked by the shaft of an anarchistic run-
away, before the eyes of her own flunkeys.
“On Saturday evening (August 21st), Theodore
Fink, M.L.A., threw open the doors of his bran-
new Balaklava mansion to over 60 East Melb.
harriers. When the guests had brushed up after
their run, they were turned loose in the library
and picture-gallery, where they amused them-
selves with elaborate arguments as to the pro-
bable cost of the contents until the supper-bell
rang. There was speech-making of course, and the
host, divulging his own unsuspected passion for
athletics, explained that the ‘ punching-ball ’ and
other gymnastic appliances in evidence were for
his personal enjoyment in halcyon moments of
leisure.
“ Now that the exhibition of loan pictures
nears its end, people hurry up to take a last (and
in many cases a first) peep. As revealed by the
catalogue, two or three Melbourne residents, one
of them a civil servant, have indulged in portraits
of themselves, or belongings, by Millais, who is
popularly believed never to have charged less than
1000 guineas for a work of this description. But
it’s wiser to employ an expert for a portrait or go
without. Compare, for instance, Millais’ concep-
tion of Hon. Simon Fraser with the picture of his
brother-M.L.C., J, A. Wallace, by another brush.
The former has invested his not particularly beau-
tiful subject with a saving touch of life and indi-.
viduality; the latter is merely the cold-blooded
presentment of a hard-faced person dressed in his
Sunday clothes.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

Mr. Thomas Broughton, formerly Mayor
of Sydney (1847) whose portrait recently appeared
in The Bulletin, has lived at Bradley Hall, Glen-
more Road, Paddington, for 53 years. On Satur-
day last he celebrated the 59th anniversary of his
marriage.

A very neat thing in the
way of self-advt. is done by
a man named Joseph, who
writes to a South African
paper that he helped a man
named Joel to call “Man
overboard ” when Barney
Barnato disappeared off the
s.s. Scot into the Atlantic.
The papers had mentioned
that Barnato was drowned,
and that Joel raised a yell
as he disappeared, but the
fact that Joseph helped Joel
to yell hadn’t been men-
tioned, and Joseph wrote
about it. Joseph is a fine
sample of that third party
who interferes in every
worldly concern—the third
man who helps the second
man to point out that the
first man is drowning.

The jubilee tidal-wave of snobbery in Eng-
land washed into Premier Turners hands the
intimation that he had the right to a “ baronetcy
in abeyance ” —the Jearnesy communicator of the
intelligence being, apparently, as unaware as the
recipient that baronetcies don’t “abey.”
Turner, of Victoria, was the first Premier
to gee back from the Jubilee, and he returned
worn-out, sick and all broken up. It is even said
that a serious operation may be necessary to vet
the Jubilee refreshments out of his system. The
other six jaundiced wrecks are arriving by instal-
ments.

With such painful frequency are a certain
unpopular C.J.’s decisions upset on appeal to the
Full Court, that certain sporting members of the
bar are always ready to offer a layman odds on a
reversal upon appeal.
The Australian Premiers, whilst in Eng-
land, received an invitation (from a lady) to
attend a concert of Irish music in Dublin. The
invite concluded with a polite request that the
Australians would attend in “ their native cos-
tumes.” Imagination totters at the thought of
George Reid in a native costume.

An exchange re the N.S.W. hangman :
Hunginan Howard is a great horse-breaker. His Bondi
residence is over a mile away along the beach front the
nearest (tub., and Howard puts sixpence in a hilly every
lunch-time and sends his horse off with the can in his
month for a supply of beer. Sometimes, larrikins try to
intercept the money, sometanc-s the beer. On these oc-
casions the noble animal keeps off his inferiors by moving
in a circle with his heels outwards and his f head as the axis.
Sixty-six years ago, John Bax first struck
Mudgee (N.S. W.). He was the driver of the first
mail conveyance that ever reached the town. He
has just celebrated his golden wedding, having
been married in Mudgee in ’47 by the late Arch-
deacon Gunther. At the jubilee dinner, the com-
bined ages of the Bax family present totalled 697
years. Mr. and Mrs. Bax are the oldest surviving
couple in Mudgee.
Warnford Lock, manager of the Went-
worth (N.S.W.) mines, and at present “the best-
hated man on Lucknow,” is aggressively English
in sentiment, and used to boast that he had
specially imported all accountants, clerks, and
persons in authority at the mines. Eventually—
well, the relative integrity of Britishers and
“colonials” is not now a staple conversational
topic: with Mr. Lock.

me musing on Bishop Moorhouse’s remarkable
