Charles Lilley, formerly Attorney-General,
Premier, and Chief Justice of Queensland, died
Aug. 20, aged 67. He was an Englishman, from
Newcastle-on-Tyne ; came to Australia in 1856,
when 26 years old ; went almost at once to More-
ton Bay; became lawyer, journalist (for two
years he edited the Moreton Bay— now Brisbane—
Courier), and advocate of separation from the
parent province. At separation, in 1860, he be-
came the familiar “Lilley of the Valley,” repre-
senting the Brisbane suburb of Fortitude Valley
in the first Q. Parliament. His Parliamentary
career covered 14 years—three of them m Minis-
terial office. He left politics to become a Judge,
and was Chief Justice from 1879 to 1893. Then
retirement was prac f ically forced upon him
by the ruling political gang, against some
of whose members he had given an adverse
decision in the celebrated Queensland Land, In-
vestment, and Mortgage Co.’s cases. The essence
of these cases was an allegation that certain of the
local directors of this London financial co., who
were at the same time, directors or shareholders
of the notorious Q. National Bank, had taken
advantage of their dual position to transfer a lot of
the bank’s rotten securities and others to the Lon-
don co. Judge Windeyer was specially borrowed
from N.S. Wales, and reversed Lilley’s judgment.
Lilley resigned bis judgeship, smarting bitterly,
and appealed to Caesar people by contesting the
"Tory North Brisbane electorate at a parliamentary
election two months later. He made a mistake
by bracketing himself (North Brisbane is a two-
member electorate) with Glassey, leader of the
Labor-party ; and the pair were badly defeated by
MTlwraith and Kingsbury. The shock to Lilley
was great, mentally and bodily. His constitution,
strained by a lifetime of struggle—even on the
Bench he worked very hard—commenced to break
up. He travelled in pursuit of health, and almosl
to the end retained hope of re-entering politics.
But the last four years of his life were very
melancholy. His radical utterances and associa-
tions banned him from “society,” and Lilley
wished to remain in sock ty while remaining a radi-
cal. He felt himself proscribed outcast. When
he came to Sydney a little while ago the ban fol-
lowed him. Little or no courtesy was shown him
by the men of his own rank, class, pro-
fession. Lilley had a weak strain in him
-—he accepted an English knighthood, he
dearly loved popular applause, newspaper notice —
he would unbosom himself to the greasiest little
paragrapher, and read complacently the most ful-
some report of his luncheon-conversation. Yet he
lived in a Sydney hotel not as a distinguished
stranger, only 7 as a stranger. And it was a man
sick at heart and weary of everything who, the
other day, went home to Brisbane to die.
Lilley was of the Parkes type—strong rather
than subt'e, energetic rather than brainy. A
masterful fellow—he wanted his own way in
everything, and in youth he generally got it.
When, as Q. Premier, he fought the monopolistic
A.S.N. Co. over their extortionate charges for
mail-conveyance, he was going to buy a fleet of
steamers to carry his point, and he carried it;
though a vote of Parliamentary censure followed.
And Lilley was no self-seeker, like the political
hucksters who exulted over his defeat in ’93. All
through his career he made enemies bitter
enemies—by saying and doing the thing he held
right, careless of the cost to himself. No man in
the Northern province has worked more zealously
for the best popular ideals. Lilley was truly the
father of the noble Q. system of public education—
“secular, compulsory, and free”—and well the
bigots remembered it against him. And no
political corruption, no participation in banking
swindles, no malversation of the public funds in
the interest of himself or his friends or his
creditors, can be alleged against Charles Lilley.
After a life of laborious public service, he died
poor—and horn st.
Mr. R. J Fairbairn represents the English
wholesale Co-operative Society 32 years old and
the largest in the world, being the co-operation of
many retail co operative societies, comprehending
about one-seventh of Britain's population and
turning over annually <£12,000,000. Mr. Fair-
bairn will reside in Sydney to make the best pos-
sible deals in butter, cheese, tallow, leather, &c.,
with the Australian producer for the British Co-
operative consumer. The wholesale society he
represents buys from the world, but sells only to
its member societies ; while these buy only from
the wholesale body, but sell retail to the world.
Profits are made by buying direct from the pro-
ducer, thus excising middlemen’s charges; and
these profits are distributed by the wholesale and
retail co-operators among their respective mem-

factory at Leicester employs 3000 men and girls,
and returns all its profits to the co-operators. Mr.
Fairbairn thinks that the Australian middleman
has a real fat time.

Order of the Garter.
Lord Melbourne: used to say that there was
only one order in the world worth having, and
that was the Garter, because there was no merit
attached to it at all, and that was what made it the
first order in Europe. And therein Lord Mel-
bourne showed his usual shrewdness. The dis-
tinction men and women love best, and appreciate
most, is the distinction which, instead of making
a man distinguished, merely singles him out as
already being 1 so distinguished, that no reason
needs to be given for distinguishing him further.
This is the distinction Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure en-
joys : testimony for its curative properties appears
on every hand. Mr. Albert W. Stacey, The
Elms, 31 Park Road, St. Kilda, says : “It gives
me great pleasure to testify to the wonderful cura-
tive properties of Warner’s ‘Safe’ Cure. For a
‘Cure’ it cannot be surpassed. The symptoms
were nausea, biliousness, great weakness and
trembling of the legs. After taking two bottles of
Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure I felt relief.” And that is
the kind of testimony which men and women
covet most.
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific for Liver
and Kidney Disease.
Aboriginalities.
An incident, probably unique, occurred on
Sunday, Bth Aug., in a big Grafton (N.S.W.)
church. Just as the parson began to preach, a
laughing jackass, which had secured a position
vertically opposite the clergyman on a high rafter
of the building, and which may have been ex-
pressly sent by Heaven to chasten the gentle shep-
herd, started its demoniac giggle. The bird’s mate
on the outside could not gain access but took part
in the service. The poor parson became nervous,
and sent for the police. Next on the scene came
one of the Grafton soldiers (volunteers), and after
one or two volleys the bird fell from the house-top
like the sparrow of Scripture. Now the jackass
is a “scheduled” bird whose destruction involves
a legal penalty ; then there is a penalty attached
to the carrying or usiny of fire-arms on the Sab-
bath—to say nothing of the desecration involved
in the discharge of firearms in a sacred edifice.
The sacred text says much about laughing in the
house of God and the destruction thereof. The
bird “died the death,” but the shock and the irre-
verence caused by the unregenerate warbler made
the preacher quite ill for several days.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Be Fiji cockroaches.
Years ago, in my lonely plantation shanty away
upone of the rivers of Viti Levu, the big cock-
roaches provided no end of sport. At night they
freely promenaded the whitewashed walls, and
many a time, when things were dull, and the heat
and mosquitoes too bad for reading, I have shot
those blackjacks with a small air-gun. It was
great practice, sitting in a cane chair and potting
them as they came within range. Every one slain
left a star on the wall, and by carefully shooting
at the right time I decorated" the wall with quite
a pretty pattern. Your cockroach correspondent
hardly does justice to the appetites of the insects.
They are insatiable and omnivorous. My boots
and books went first, then my razor-strop, and
lastly, the edge off each razor. Prolific is too
moderate a word when speaking of their fecundity.
A hundred tons of sugar lay in a lighter for a
week, and weighed out 105 tons—balance cock-
roaches. — Blackjack.

In May, 1892, for the first time in the his-
tory of the oldest inhabitant, large flocks of ibis
(the sacred bird of ancient Egypt) came from the
East and remained in Southern Westralia for
seven months, during which time Coolgardie was
found, and men came to it from the East at first
in small numbers and afterwards in thousands,
spreading over the land from Fremantle to Cool-
gardie, and from Esperance Bay to Gerald ton.
When Romo was founded the soothsayers foretold
by the flights of birds that it would last as a king-
dom, or empire, for twelve centuries. What is
the Coolgardie ibis portent ?

Backblocks hospitality. Two pictures.
First: Station near Nyngan, N.S.W. Intelligent
sheep-dog sees traveller approaching the home-
stead, and forthwith starts to “work” a fowl
towards the kitchen-door. Strong evidence to the
traveller of hearty welcome. In this case, the dog
knows the squatter. Second : Station near
well, on the Bogan. Little girl, true daughter
and imitator of her father, rebuked by mother for
using rather lurid expression, caught that day
fi’om “the old man.” “ Yes, I’m very sorry, but
(hopefully) I suppose I can say blanky to the
travellers ? ” In this case, the infant knows the
squatter.

A N.S.W. squatter was driving the newly-
imported local clergyman out to his station when
his horses took fright and bolted. The squatter
set his teeth and hung on to the reins, and as they
tore along like greased lightning the parson leant
towards him and said, as well as the jolting would
permit, “My dear friend, is there any ocoasion to
go quite so fast ? ”

fence. Possibly, some extra strong ‘ lepper ’
might do it in an agony of fear, and his apparent
accomplishment would then be grafted on to tl ■

A pious and prosperous Tas. sheep-run
. n ,- J- , • f 1 , . , . 1 1,111
holder is notouously eccentric, but is considerate
towards swagmen. He always makes a point of
employing the casuals for a day or two. His usual
formula when a swaggie comes along is : “ Want
work ?. Have you looked for any ?. D’ye think I
can find employment for the whole country ? No,
I don’t want anybody. Got enough ! . . . Let
me see. Can you work ? Oh ! well, go up to the
hut. ” The man goes to work. A few days later
the boss singles him out. “ Hi, you fellow, come
over to the house and I’ll pay you. You’ll have
to move on with your traps. There’s two more
men arrived, and I must find a bit for them to do.”

An Australian resident received a circular
from the Hamburg State Lottery, Germany, and,
believing it square, invested a few pounds. Some
ihonths later he received three tickets, with notice
that he had not been successful, that there was
12s. to his credit, and requesting him to send on
£4 4s. for the next issues. His reply :
Batchemalla Lanapur.
Ni uma condamba bunk burra dageo goulumbarbar qi
qi. '
It is as well lor me to address you in the above language
(Austral aboriginal), as for you to send me notices in
German. I wish you to distinctly understand that English
is the universal language out here, as we Australians have
not yet devoured enough Wiirst and sauerkraut to talk
so far down our neck as the German language demands.
I find I have not drawn a red cent, and that you want £4
4s. more. As you credit me with 125., please send me
that value in Lager bier, which is about the best thing I
know of “ made in Germany.” —Disgusted.

his old mates, being also on gold, had visions of a brighter

Arthur wooed his lady-love in, took up a position some

He (after long silence): “ What do you think of the

From a man who dislikes the cocky :
Ed. Bulletin, —One of the man-y eye-sores of the bush
is the cocky’s son. Slow’ of speech, dull of apprehension,
loutish and stupid ; to be in his company is a curse, to
live with him is an affliction. Where he shines is on
horseback, dressed in a slop-made suit of black-corded
cloth, a slouch hat, elastic-side boots, a white shirt, green
silk handkerchief and a silly grin on his vacuous face.
With these, a stockwhip and a gun, he’s equipped for life.
He’ll go three miles to destroy a young wattle-tree to
obtain a penn’orth of bark, or shoot an iguana for the
same value in oil. His parents live in a bark hut,
the worse of two on the selection, the better being
for the buggy, for which they’ve mortgaged-the crop, and
on w’hich in turn they’ll get a loan from the storekeeper
to buy a side-saddle or piano for the daughter. They
often start life with about £2OO w’orth of machinery and
implements and let them go to rust and ruin for the want
of a£2 shed to protect them from the weather. To the
cocky, elementary chemistry and botany, fruit, flower,
and vegetable growing, dairy-farming, jam-making, stock-
breeding, &c., are unknown quantities. -He will take up
a square mile of land, roughly fence it, ringbark a part,
and throw in a few handfuls of w'heat, grow' a few pump-
kins, and call it farming. When he’s done this he comes
out into the labor-market, doing fencing, shearing, road-
making, tank-sinking, &c., at sweaters’ wages. In case of
strikes, he serves the squatter as blackleg or special con-
stable, being a faithful vassal of that modern feudal
baron, who supplies the cocky with contracts for servioes
rendered. Altogether the cocky is an object to make
men weep and angels mourn.-—M.B.
Youliana.
The late Coroner Youl had a snug medical
practice among old Melbourne identities, up to
20 years ago. As his clients successively passed
away, he contented himself with the fading re-
mainder, like the heroes of the W'aterloo Banquet.
His father, chaplain John Youl, was one of Van
Diemen’s Land’s earliest parsons ; the Youl clan is
still strong in Raspberry land. Parson Youl used
to summon his Launceston congregation to wor-
ship by personally banging an empty rum-
hogs-head, suspended on an iron bar, with a
huge wooden mallet. On his first arrival in
Georgetown, too, in 1819, this unconven-
tional cleric paraded round the place in all
his robes of glory, just to let folks know
that the church had come to town. On one
occasion the old parson married 30 Tas.
couples in one day, and the same ring was
the bridal bond of the lot. He was once, so
goes the story, booked by Tahitians as the
menu for a big feast. He failed to fatten,
however, being a thin, wiry person, and his
life was spared on his shaving a lot of big
natives with his own razor without drawing
blood once. For some time before his death
the late Coroner was so feeble that he had
to be lifted from his house to a cab, and
thence into the Morgue.
The old gentleman had several pet fads
which he was wont to air at ever)’ oppor-
tunity. “ What made you call this child
Sardanapulus Aminadab Smith ? ” he would
demand of a weeping mother, “You might
have known he would die of improper feed-
ing. All babies with names like that die of
improper feeding; or, if they grow up,
they get hanged, so they come to me after
all. Next time, madam, call the baby one
name, John or -Tames or William, and he’ll
probably grow up healthy and reputable.”
Another favorite theory of the old man’s,
with possibly more than a grain of truth ill
it, was that husbands were poisoning their
wives and wives their husbands every day of
the week ; and yet another all-truth was
his trenchant denouncement of flannelette
for clothes. “You might as well dress a
child in gunpowder as flannelette,” he has
said again and again at inquests on infants
roasted from this cause. He was anything
but a teetotal advocate, and it was a standing
principle with Him that possession of
money was not necessarily precedent to
drunkenness. “I’ll guarantee to go up to
the city without a penny in my pocket
and get blazing drunk within an hour,” was
his frequent boast. The one great griev-
ance of his life concerned a pressman who, 20
years ago, borrowed from him a set of depositions
and never returned them. This rankled in the
old coroner’s mind, and no Melb. reporter lives
who has not heard the deceased relate the yarn
impressively over and over again.
The last story told by Dr. Youl was about a
young married man whose wife had strayed from
home and ended her life in the Yarra. “ I went
into the mortuary,” remarked the doctor, “and
there was the young man whistling ‘Ta-ra-ra-
boom-de-ay,’or some other mad tune of the day.
I watched him pace up and down two or three
times in front of the only corpse on the slabs, and
when I approached and inquired if he knew any
‘ body ’ there, he lightly responded ‘ No, I do
not,’ and continued his whistling. Next day I
held an inquest on the body, and the first witness
called was my whistling friend of the day before.
Now, what do you think of that for a married
man ? ”
At Dr. Youl’s very last inquest a brewer came
forward to give evidence. “What are you?”—
“A brewer.” “Ha, ha!” ejaculated the old
doctor, “ you should not say a brewer ; say a
‘poisoner.’ We have no brewers now ; all beer is
made with chemicals.” The coroner was a privi-
leged libertine of speech, but unfortunately he had
an inveterate habit of over-stating things, thu
often missing his point. The public would alwayss
discount his utterances, even when thoroughly
justified. His tongue was too smart, tart, and
ready ; but against this is to be set his love of fair
play, his determination never to let an abuse pass,
even if he stood alone (which he frequently did),
until he had secured a reform.
One saying of his which caused endless heart-
burnings was: “ Every brick in the Melbourne
Hospital is saturated with erysipelas and pyaemia.”
There never was an inquest at which Youl did not
say something angular and injudicious—one of
those truths which are in such glaring “bad
taste.”
Rations (termed free rations in the British
Army and Navy enlistment circulars) supplied to
H.M’s braves, are not nearly so good as those
given to prisoners in modern gaols. The sailor
gets for breakfast 1-pint cocoa, bread or biscuit;
for dinner, 1-pint pea-soup, pork, biscuit; for tea,
1-pint tea, bread. No milk or sugar or potatoes
included, neither is soup allowed when in harbor,
but fib. of fresh meat, inclusive of bone and
gristle, is substituted for pork. The soldier’s pro-
visions are lib. bread, and fib. meat per diem, the
remaining necessaries of life, also washing, hair-
cutting, repairs to kit, etc., being acquirable only
one of personal expenditure. Summed up, Govt,
gives its maritime and military employe six-
penn’orth of food and a shilling a day, and the
man, having to spend half his wages to keep him-
self alive, has about 6d. a day to draw at the end
of the month. Then the extravagant beast goes
and gets drunk ! He deserves to be flogged—for
joining.


Dear Bulletin, — Professor Tucker, of
Melb. University, once exhorted his young Vic-
torian audience not to go travelling around the
world with the proud parochial thought that they
“had seen no finer street than Collins-street.”
Premier Turner has returned from London and
elsewhere with the old-young cry on his lips. The
public buildings didn’t impress him, and he saw
nothing to surpass Collins-street. When he was
in Manchester he told the Mayor, or somebody,
that he preferred Melbourne, and when he visited
Brighton (if he did go there) patriotic George pro-
bably noticed that it was merely another St.
Kilda on a larger scale. This kind of criticism is
partly justifiable, and partly absurd. The most
fashionable thoroughfares of the chief European
cities are not broader and cleaner and brighter
than Collins-street, but they are a darned sight
more interesting as places for a promenade. _ And
they haven’t a noisome colony of Chinamen in the
near neighborhood. The next time Premier
Turner walks down Piccadilly, Regent-street,
Oxford-street, or even the Tottenham Court
Road, he had better remind himself that
he can’t smell a Lilly Buck-s reet within a
stone’s throw of the nose. Apart from climatic
considerations, which cut both ways, any unbiassed
person would naturally prefer Sydney to Mel-
bourne, although the principal streets of the
Harbor-city are, as everybody knows, inferior in
all respects to the leading London samples. From
a cockney point of view, the Collins-street “ blow”
seems foolish, whereas the Beautiful Harbor
“ blow ” errs only on the side of moderation.
And, from a cockney point of view, the Premier
who measures London with a Collins-street rule is
a very small-minded rooster, no matter what he
may have done in the way of democratic legisla-
tion. How The Bulletin would scoff at a
Hinglish Premier who, returning from a visit to
Paris, might say, “ I have seen nothing finer than
the Crystal Palace,” or “nothing more interesting
than our bits of old Roman wall! ” [Would it?
The Bulletin has a great deal too much on its
hands to care a straw what an English Premier
thinks of Paris, or what a Bulgarian Premier
thinks of Spitzbergen, or what other remote people
think about other remote places. —Ed. B.] By the
way, did Premier Turner turn down one of the
streets off Cheapside, and gazeat a bit of old Roman
wall? I ask again, did he? For, if he did, I
would fain force him to admit that he has “ not
seen anything so thick in Melbourne.’ —Bow
Bell.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The Hotel Australia, on erection, was
deemed by many wise-acres to be a white elephant
—so handsome and highly-appointed was the
colossal establishment for a small populated
country like N.S.W. This year, however, the
answer has come in more decided form than usual
—the management having declared a 6 per cent,
dividend for shareholders, after expending some
,£3OOO on exterior and interior decorations and
new-appointments. Probably the big hotel is the
securest place in Australia to sleep in. The water
available for extinguishing fire is tremendous.
When the Theatre Royal was burning, the Aus-
tralia manager got his men to discharge water
from his upper storeys across several roofs on to
the burning theatre. Superintendent Bear quickly
sent round a message: “Tell Moore to stop that
water or he’ll drown every man and fire-engine I
have. ”

Undertakery .
[F R THE BULLETIN.]
There is nothing peculiarly meritorious about
dying. Almost any man can die if he desires
to. Also if he does not. The act involves no
lofty intelligence. First-class fools and gilt-
edged philosophers alike pervade the tombs.
And there is nothing novel, nothing unique,
about the operation, because death is a habit of
considerable antiquity. At the same time,
there is nothing blameworthy in the pro-
cess of dissolution. Proportionately few per-
sons die from merely self-indulgent motives.
On the contrary, and generally speaking,
they neglect the most pleasing engage-
ments in favor of death. No doubt some
few have died out of vulgar spite, to deprive a
foe or relative—the terms being frequently
synonymous—of an inheritance, an annuity, or
things like that. But the vast majority die
because dying is, under the circumstances,
about the only thing they can do. If, then, a
corpse commits no breach of decorum or ethics
by becoming a corpse, why should he, as now,
be made an unholy show of and held up to the
ridicule of the reflective, simply to indulge the
yearnings of an undertaker for ghoulish
pageantry and filthy lucre ?
• * • •
These remarks are the delayed outcome of a
protracted course of loathing contemplation of
funereal methods in what are called civilised
countries, and the more immediate result of a
recent encounter with a corpse wearing a mauve
coffin, dyed barndoor-rooster plumes, and a
black-and-gilt caravan with plateglass windows
—the latter probably inserted in order that the
remains might be subject to police supervision,
so, if era route they, tired of the surrounding
gewgaws, wanted to get out, they might be
promptly run-in for “ ondacent behavior and
resistin’ the polis.” This particular and typi-
cal deceased was driven by a red - nosed
person of ginulous aspect and personally
conducted by a bilious citizen armed with a
long black stick, convenient to smite defunct
on the head in case of his premature resurrec-
tion. Judging by the modified glitter, paucity
of professional attendants, and meagre follow-
ing of buggies with wall-eyed horses, the
remains was some good fellow in moderate cir-
cumstances, who had not been given to violently
despoiling the widow and the orphan, had never
been a Legislative Councillor, and had not been
in the habit of masquerading in a mauve
tourist’s suit with lacquered handles when
alive. Then why, The Bulletin rises to ask,
should these latter things be done unto him
when dead?
• * • *
This enquiry is made not so much on behalf
of those corpses that have been disposed of,
because it would be work of supererogation to
dig them up and re-inter them with decency,
but in the interests of those persons who, there
is reason to believe, in absence of sufficient evi-
dence to the contrary, are still alive. Why
crape—the most mildew ed of all materials—
which might be feathers or sweeps’
AT THE MUSICALS.
“ Wonderful execution !”
“ Yes; almost amounts to a massacre.”
brooms, black cloaks redolent of the charnel-
house, cow-hide gloves, black-leaded horses
with all their back hair let down, and dolorous
men, like blighted notes of interrogation, with
chronic cold in the head and black suits relieved
by bed fluff, and dingy white cotton gloves
(extra tens), have come to be accepted as sym-
bolical of “ the resurrection and the life” is one
of those tangled theological problems which
could only be satisfactorily elucidated by an
old-time biblical commentator in full training,
or the unchained lunatics who haunt editorial
offices with the view of explaining the last
chapter of Revelations by aid of a blunt bread-
knife.
• • • *
As a broad rule, a corpse dies to please him-
self. As a broader, he is buried to please an
undertaker. From the moment the latter runs
his two-foot rule over the remains he is master
of the situation. And he knows it. He knows
that the demised, owing to the exigencies of
urgent private affairs elsewhere, has time
neither to wither him with profanity nor welt
him with a club. He knows the survivors are
either too grief-stricken, too careless, conven-
tional, or too solicitous for self-display to
expostulate with him with a gun. So he
garishly bedizens the non-resident residuum
with gold-paint and dyes, like an illuminated
testimonial, and sticks cocks’ tails and brass
handles and nickel nails about him, as if he
were the sign of a rum-mill or an African
fetich, until the corpse, if at all “ right-think-
ing,” squirms with shame in his varnished box
and willprobably blushattlieremembrance when,
if ever, he gets up again. And the undertaker,
having persuaded the relations of the remains
that the “ dear departed ” can properly prance
to paradise only in one of “our patent, refri-
gerating, reversible, fireproof £7 10s.
caskets,” attaches bits of black to them till
they resemble scarecrows on a gin crawl, and
shuts them into the moiety of a morgue on
wheels, and drives them, slowly and solemnly,
that the aroma of death may soak into their
bones, to the appointed humid planting-place,
where deceased is dumped into a dirty hole, and
rocks and clods thrown at him to keep him
from coming back to spend his personality,
while the mournerß catch mumps and consump-
tion bacilli.
* • * •
Death has been called King, but King of King
Death is the undertaker, and great is his power.
Nor is the reason far to seek. We are
frightened of death. We may adorn the con-
dition with poetry and aphorisms and other
verbal prettinesses ; we may decorate it with
the promises of creeds in which none have any
real belief, but the facts remain that death
terrorises and the dead scare us. We may not pro-
fess to know what harm the latter can do us, but
when unplanted remains are around weentertain
a vague fear that they may get up in the night
and bite us, or chevy us round the house when
our bedroom candle is out. So we treat them
as dust and ashes, and are so eager to have them
put into the rubbish-box and carted away by
the undertaker, who is your true dustman, that
we allow that functionary to work his own
wicked will in the matter of funeral bric-a-brac.
• • • •
And, though death is absolutely the common-
est circumstance in the universe, though we
must all die, save the inconsiderable minority
who ascend in chariots of fire, civilised man-
kind contemplate the transaction from purely
barbaric standpoints. They have permitted
mysteries, superstitions and horrors unutterable
to gather round their so-called “ God’s Acres ”
and “ Happy Valleys ” and those who so
soundly sleep therein. In earliest years iqpst
of us were fascinated and cowed by the queer
mixture of heathen and biblical mythology our
elders taught, and shuddered at ghostly tales
and grim verbal pictures of the “awful pit-
hole.” And many, in their turn as adults,
revenge the creeping consternations of their
childhood by passing them on to newer
generations. Acting on the barbaric view
of death, we have draped the whole
subjeot with a preposterous pall of gloom,
false as the mask of grief we wear when the
“ sorely-lamented ” deceased has left us a
thumping legacy. How screechingly illogical
it all is ! Regarded whether from Christian,
Spiritualistic, or Materialistic standpoint, where
do the terrors come in ? where the tears, the
gloom, the grief ? If the remains was a
believing Christian, even though he wooed
Eternity vid the gallows, his title to mansions
in the skies is surer than any right under any
Land Act writ on earth ; and, even if he be
only a common hedgerow Christian, who had
never won Paradise by paying pew - rent, he
is, since publication of the revised version and
abolition of hell, comfortably enough placed,
for Sheol must be preferable to earth to 999,990
out of every million persons. If the corpse be
a spiritualist, death should open to him endless
vistas of spookeries and their delights and the
opportunity of haunting his mother-in-law and
repaying, with interest and impunity, her con-
tumely. While, to the materialist, death must
mean sleep as eternal as dreamless, everlasting
release from the school benches whereon we
living doleful sit, annihilation of the uneasy,
calami tv-freighted ego, and nevermore the re-
curring landlord nosing for his rent.
“You’ll be in heaven in five minutes ! ” said
the skipper, his ship sinking, to the bishop.
“ God forbid ! ” said the bishop. We’re all
built that way. We like to cant of future bliss,
but we’re “ not taking any ” chance of going
there. We “ grunt and sweat under a weary
life,” yet vastly prefer the devil we do to the
devil we don’t know. Until we either entirely
believe or disbelieve our ologies and isms, there
is small prospect of bruising the head of that
old serpent the undertaker, and, consequently,
little hope of wide-spread funeral and burial
reform. There could be no new fashion in
death ; there could, however, be new fashions in
funerals. Among those capable of leading a vogue
must be some who have correctly appraised
the undertaker and his mouldy and disgusting
fripperies, some who have intelligently regarded
the burial hole and its abominations. A great
mission awaits such leaders, no matter where
they live, here or at the antipodes—the mission
A BLACK BURLESQUE .
(At Wooroon [?]oonga Aboriginal Some on Jubilee day.)
CHORUS BY THE PUPILS: “Gorsay Our Craysha Kaween.”
of funeral reform and the substitution of crema-
tion for the present vile method of interment.
Funeral reform should aim at the total suppres-
sion of the present hideous hearses, mourning
coaches, unnecessary professional mourners,
crape, hat-bands, weeds, and the various other
rubbish undertakers have used over a thousand
other dead persons, and introduce to add to the
repulsiveness of death and the items in their
bills. Decency, cleanliness, and cheapness
are the great desiderata the latter more
especially, as thousands of families spend
on funerals, the gorgeousness of which the dead
cannot appreciate, money sorely needed by the
living. Black has come to be regarded as th
fitting symbol of death and dreariness. If,
however, there is no real dismalness about
death, black garments and their attendant
expense may go by the board. Again, were
cremation substituted for burial, which is an
outrage on the dead and as revolting as threat-
ening to the living, there would be no necessity
for gaudy and costly coffins—the millionaire
Hebrew is even now* satisfied with an un-
painted egg-box. With cremation, family
vaults and graves and costly mendacious
marbles would go. The adoption of cremation
would not abolish but purify undertakery,
while the more true and logical conception of
death, which would follow banishment of the
existent, obnoxious and illogical “ trappings of
woe,” would induce people not to vainly mourn,
but to rejoice when some loved one, after life’s
storms, had found peaceful haven, rest and
sleep.
J. EVISON.
Maryborough (Q.) possesses a sensible
P.M. in Mr. Morey. The other day he told a wit-
ness : “ You needn’t kiss the Book. It is a filthy
habit. The oath is better taken by holding up the
right hand.”
Timi : “In a gorgeously-decorated Sydney
undertaking establishment I to-day saw a child of
five playing with a barrow fashioned to represent
a small coffin. Poor little baby ghoul ! It made
me shiver. But then, perhaps, if one is destined
for the corpse-trade, there is nothing like starting
in it young. ”
Maxillus : “ Would-be purists are object-
ing to the introduction of the word ‘ slump ’ into
the language of the share-market. I have even
seen it stigmatised as a * degradation to the lan-
guage.’ Lowell, however, uses it; not, indeed, as
a noun, but as a verb. ‘ Your very God would
slump into Himself, like a mockery king of snow.’
(‘ Condescension in Foreigners.’) Now, Lowell
was a purist, and never used a so-called American-
ism that was not an old English form, brought in
by the Pilgrim Fathers. His use of the word
gives it brevet rank. If it originally referred to
the thawing and melting of snow into slush, this
new use, as applied to shares, is really picturesque.”
Botanist Botting Hemsley, in Knowledge,
avers that there are now more blue-gums growing
outside Australia and Tasmania than exist in
their native forests.
Warra, from, the backblocks :
My missus is one of those who clip poetry and “ yarns "
out of The Bulletin and paste them in a book. Now, it
often happens that two splendid yarns are printed pne on
each side of the paper, so that it becomes necessary to
paste one under. The missus asks me which I like, and
then carefully spreads the paste on it. This leads to
“ strained relations.” For the sake of domestic felicity
throughout the land, could you not follow your own pre-
cept (with variations) and print yarns on one side of the
paper only ? The other side could be filled up with advts.
about “ manly vigor.”

member casu-
ally remarks
in N.S.W. As-
sembly that
the unemploy-
ed trouble is
mainly due to
“the coun-
try’s enormous
quantity o f
unskilled
labor.” I
won’t bet
about this be-
ing so in cities,
but I’ll lay
odds that it’s
altogether the
reverse in the
bush. Every
second swag-
man one meets
on the track
is a tradesman
or professional
of some sort.
It’s quite com-
mon at shear-
ing on large
stations to
find a couple
of broken-
down doctors,
a brace of law-
yers, a parson
or two, and
several bank-
accountants
in the rouse-
abouts’ hut.
M’sA. and such, of all kinds of universi-
ties, are as common as Bathurst-burrs out
back, and every trade, from boiler-making to type-
snatching, is heavily represented. No, sir, un-
skilled ” labor is distinctly in the minority in the
country ; and if all the “skilled” gentlemen could
and would go back to their trades and professions,
and the pestering “ seen-better-days,’’broken-down
swells to their—haw —wealthy friends and —haw—
relatives, things in the bush would be O.K. for
the likes of —R.H.
A Scotch Night.
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
If you chance to strike a gathering of half-a-dozen
friends
Where the drink is Highland whisky or some
chosen “ Border Blends,”
And the room is full of “ speirin,” and the
“gruppin”’ of brown “han’s,”
And the talk is all of “ tartans ” and of “ plaidies ”
and of “clans,”
You can take things “ douce” and easy, you can
judge you’re going right,
For you’ve had the luck to stumble on a “ wee
Scotch night.”
When you’re pitchforked in among them in a
sweeping sort of way
As “ anither mon an’ brither ” from the Tweed or
from the Tay,
When you’re taken by the “oxter” and you’re
“ couped ” into a chair
While someone slips a ‘ * whusky ” in your tumbler
unaware,
Then the present seems less dismal and the future
fair and “bricht,”
For you’ve struck earth’s grandest treasure in a
"guid Scots nicht.”
When you hear a short name shouted and the same
name shouted back
Till you think in the confusion that they’ve all
been christened “ Mao,”
When you see a red beard flashing in the corner
by the fire,
And a giant on the sofa who is six-foot three or
higher,
Before you’ve guessed the color and before you’ve
gauged the height
You’ll have jumped at the conclusion it’s a “ braw
Scotch night.”
When the red man in the corner puts his strong
voice to the proof
As he gives the “ Hundred Pipers,” and the chorus
lifts the roof,
When a “ chiel ” sings “ Annie Laurie ” with its
tender, sweet refrain,
Till the tears are on their eyelids and—the drinks
come round again,
When they chant the stirring war-songs that would
make the coward fight,
Then you’re fairly in the middle of a “ wee Scotch
night.”
When the plot begins to thicken and the band
begins to play,
When every tin-pot chieftain has a word or two to
say,
When they’d sell a Queensland station for a sprig
of native heath,
When there’s one “Mac” on the table and a
couple underneath,
When half of them are sleeping and the whole of
them are tight.
You will know that you’re assisting at a (hie)
Scotch night.
When the last big bottle’s empty and the dawn
creeps gray and cold,
And the last clan-tartan’s folded and the last
d d lie is told,
When they totter down the footpath in a brave,
unbroken line,
To the peril of the passers and the tune of “ Auld
Lang Syne,”
You can tell the folk at breakfast as they watch
the fearsome “sieht,”
“ They have only been assisting at a braw Scots
nicht! ”
WILL. H. OGILVIE.

POLITICAL
POINTS.
Every N.S.W. Premier has, at some period
of his Premiership, threatened to smash the Leg.
Council.
Max Hirsch announces himself for Yic.
general elections (third week in October) against
Government whip O’Neil.
Dr. Maclaurin, M.L.C., shows signs of
yielding to the same gentle pressure which urges
R. E. O’Connor from the N.S.W. Upper to the
Lower Chamber.
Two Indian “princes,” graduates of an
English University, are said to manage a con-
tingent of many hundreds of their countrymen on
N.S.W. Northern rivers.
Yic. ex-Speaker Bent intends trying to re-
enter Parliament via Toorak. “I am wasted out
of Parliament ” he declares. New precept fear
Toorak electors : “ Waste, and want not.”
J. G. Drake, M.L.A: (Q.), the other day,
challenged the right of Labor-leader (and, virtu-
ally, Opposition-leader) Glassey to speak for all
the members on the Opposition benches of which
Drake is a cross-bench man.
AT THE RELIEF OF LUCKNOW, N.S.W.
UNDERGROUND BOSS: “And did they search you,
Johnny, before you came down ? ”
JOHNNY (blackleggina) : “ Yaas—how widiculous !
As if. I had any money why, my pa is an
English duke!"
Three years ago Deakin declared, in answer
to a question at an election-meeting : “ I will not
be a member of a stop-gap Government.” That
Government lias had a run of three years, with
another likely three years on top of that.
Tasmanian Parliament actually discussing,
in all seriousness, State supervision of the apple-
export. Of course, nothing more than the ap-
pointment of an English expert is intended ; but
now terribly socialistic Tasmania is becoming !
The Melbourne Tories trod on one another
in their efforts to welcome back Premier Turner,
erstwhile called “ brigand ”by the Argus. They
were all, Argus included, anxious to complete the
ennobblement so skilfully commenced by Chamber-
lain.
Solomon, M.P. for the Northern Territory,
refused to attend the Federation Convention on
Saturdays, because that is the ancient Hebrew
Day of Rust. But his enemies allege that Solomon
collects his two-guinea fee for Saturdays and
Sundays, and all other days, just the same.
Verb. sap. : —S.M. Herald approvingly
quotes Bourinot to the effect that the Canadians,
in adopting the “Dominion” designation, “de-
sired to give to the confederation a monarchical
likeness in contradistinction to the Republican
character of the American Federal Union.”
Dr. MacLaurin, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), wanted
to label the Federal Lower House the “Com-
mons ” ; but, as there can only be commons in
contradistinction to tmeommons, the Upper
House would naturally, in that case, have come in
time to be styled the “Lords.” Which was pro-
bably the business-end of MacLaurin’s proposal.
Queensland Labor-party is unanimous in
opposing the Govt, beer-tax. Considering the
number of teetotallers in that party this is very
remarkable. Years ago leader Glassey was loud
in his advocacy of heavy taxation for every variety
of d-r-r-rink, and his opponents quote the Ancient
Thomas against the Modern Tom with great
effect.
It is commonly reported
that the late N.S.W. Railway
Commissioner Eddy left be-
hind him a report in which
he gave it as his fixed
opinion that the railways of
Australia should be put under
Federal control. The report,
if there was one, has been
suppressed, and Acting-Pre-
mier Brunker is understood
to say, in a vague sort of
fashion, that he doesn’t really
know of any report at all.
The Vice-President of the
Council, on the other hand,
admits that there was a re-
port, but keeps strictly dark
about the contents. The docu-
ment might not do much
good, but still it should throw
some light on the railway
question, and it might as
well be brought to light.
Robert Harper, coffee-
miller, bank-ambassador, pil-
lar-of-the-church, etc., etc.,
by declaring for Protection,
put the Argus into hysterics.
The first day after the de-
claration, the poor old paper
had not a word of comment
to utter, but every issue since
has been a scream. One
symptom of her hysterics was
vi attack on Benjamin Hoare.
Jen is a leader-writer on the
Age, and, years ago, he was
connected with a Geelong
paper. Something in that
Geelong paper is unearthed
to contradict something in
the Age ! What this is sup-
posed to prove nobody knows.
Fancy the dignified Argus of ten years ago stoop-
ing to enquire what—ah—persons were employed
by the—ah—paper down the street ? Max Hirsch
gets the credit in journalistic circles for a recent
series of articles directed at the Age, hence the
Age’s vague references to “ paid emissaries of
.German importers,” etc.
Turner's jaundiced remarks about Britain
and the House of Commons and John Bull
generally are the remarks of a man with much
undigested food on his stomach. In the case of a
man like Turner it doesn’t do for the “silken
bond ” to consist too exclusively of turtle fat and
cake with a rich perspiration of grease on the
surface.
The Australian ‘ ‘ Commonwealth ” locu-
tion was born of Parkes when in his finest “Take-
away-that-bauble ! ” vein. But Parkes —though
emulous in the abstract of the Cromwellian
legend—filled far too extensively his own mental
foreground ever to have successfully played primus
inter pares. Be his theory what it might, his
practice would still have panned-out Parkes Im-
perator!
The drafting department of the Melbourne
Metropolitan Board of Works is quietly being
inundated with Germans, some of whom speak no
better English than the average vegetable John.
The reason of this swampage is not apparent at
first sight,, seeing that the new-comers receive the
standard salary, but observation shows that they
start work about an hour earlier and relinquish it
about an hour later than their Australian col-
leagues.
Excess of arrivals in Queensland over de-
partures during ’96 —3257 ; half of which number
were Japanese. Apropos, in Piebald Land, Dick-
son, a very saintly Minister of the Crown, was
lately fined for unlawfully employing a Kanaka
about his house and grounds. His lawyer had the
brazen cheek to plead “ privilege of Parliament,”
but it didn’t “go”—10s. and professional and
court costs.

PROPRIETOR: “ You do ! Walk in !'
Glassey’s sudden asssumption of the posi-
tion of Q. Opposition leader, the other evening,
raised quite a storm. Firstly, Groom indignantly
repudiated him ; then Jackson (metaphorically)
spurned him while the Ministerialists grinned de-
lightedly. How well Tozer probed Glassey’s
weakness when he tickled him almost into Tory-
ism by promptly recognising him as leader of the
Labor-party !
MISFIT PRIDE.
THE NEW PARSON (explanatorily ): “ I was having a hath in a
quiet nook when some evil-minded hoys stole my clothing.
They are too familiar in this locality, and do not pay
sufficient respect to a gentleman of my position. I shall
have to put on my English reserve . ”
FARMER: “I hope it jits you better than my oats ! ”
Stephen Barker, the new Melb. Trades
Hall President, who has no doubt a constituency
m view, has been on the job in the Evening
Herald for some weeks past with stodgy column
letters on “ Land and Poverty.” One of Stephen’s
latest letters speaks of an imperfect land policy as
an “ incubus that is eating the heart out of ” the
“ people ” ! He means well, but he belongs to that
serious class of men who were sent into the world
to bore it awfully.
Trenwith, M.P., in answer to the assertion
that if the State were made one electorate for the
selection of Federal Senators no poor man would
be able to stand because of the costliness of
electioneering, told Vic. Assembly, the other
night, that his electioneering expenses as a Federal
candidate amounted to Jl3 6s. It is Fat Man
who has to pay his way into the House and who is
horrified at the prospect of having to shout beer
for a whole nation.
S.A. Oppositionists have always asserted
that the Kingston Govt, is a one-man Ministry,
and it looks as if it were true. Holder has been a
failure as a leader in his chief’s absence, and the
Opposition, although in a minority, has had
matters all its own way. The Ass policy has al-
ways been to block business, and lately it has
triumphantly succeeded. One more session like
the present one and the democratic majority would
vanish to the winds.
Chief Secretary Moore, of the Tasmanian
Federal ten, offers to resign in order that Attorney-
General Clark may attend Sydney Convention—
an enabling Bill to be run through Parliament
for the purpose. Of course, it is unconstitutional,
and all that sort of thing*, but, barring ex-Speaker
Bird, the eleventh man on the Federal poll and
the present Ishmael of the Assembly, nobody will
very violently object. Clark certainly knows more
about Federation than any of Tasmania’s ten.
The Australian “Commonwealth” has
been shuttlecocked back to Australian “Dominion”
by N.S.W. Upper House, and now there are
demands to omit both terms and in lieu employ
the simple designation “ Australia.” But “ Aus-
tralia ’ won’t work—just at present, anyway, as
territorially at least one-half of Australia will
probably stand from under the current Federal
scheme. When all Australia comes into the
Federation, “ Commonwealth ” and “Dominion”
may both go.
H. : ‘ ‘ One of Premier Turner’s interviews
in England cuts deep. He indignantly asks the
newspaper-man what is the meaning of such a
vast extent of England having relapsed from agri-
cultural occupation into sheep-walk. It was
almost more like Australia than Australia itself.
Was this their Freetrade ? destroying the yeo-
manry, and cramming a degenerate population
into cities ! Don’t let them talk about Protection
having bloated Melbourne at the expense of the
territory at large.”
Vic. general election is due about two
months hence, and already 183 candidates are
out for the 95 seats. The biggest disagreement,
so far, seems to be at South Yarra, where six men
are after one seat. At Grenville, Dandenong, and
Maloney’s constituency of West Melbourne, five
candidates are after one seat; eight are after two
billets at East Bourke ; and Labor-member ’An-
cock, in his smellful stronghold of Footscray,
where the bone-mills are, has to save his political
scalp from four hungry competitors.
The Federation Bill got into the Tasmanian
Leg. Council last week, and old Adye Douglas,
who has lately become a bitter Provincialist,
opened proceedings with about the most compre-
hensive damn-blast that has been thrown upon
the subject up to date. N.S.W., according to
Douglas, is a barren waste, and Victoria a fly-
blown land, where a man’s chop rises and walks
away while he is eating it, and Federation is a
composite beast, mentioned in Revelations, with a
mane on its head and a sting in its tail. The idea
of Tasmania federating on any terms with a
desert peopled by mental wrecks Douglas regards
as an absurdity. Then the old man broke a few
things, and sat down among a blaze of adjectives.
The row in S.A. Labor-party, which has
been simmering for months, has now reached boil-
ing-point. Charleston, M.L.C., ever since he
openly sided with the medical party in the hospi-
tal troubles, has not been on speaking terms with
his colleagues. The ill-feeling was increased by
the action of thp party in openly opposing his
candidature at the Federal Convention election.
The whole trouble, which has caused a serious
split in the Labor ranks at a time of weakness fol-
lowing upon election defeat, can be placed at
the door of the Hospital curse, which has severed
more friendships than any other dispute in S.A.
political history.
The opponents of the N.S.W. Legal Prac-
titioners’ Bill, which allows admission to anyone
of good character and having the necessary know-
ledge of law, strongly insist on the necessity of
general culture, evinced, among other things, by a
knowledge of Latin. Premier Turner (a pretty
successful and brainy Melbourne solicitor) to an
interviewer : “At Cambridge they kindly made
us all doctors of law, and I heard all my virtues
and good qualities in Latin. I have to confess,
however, that 1 did not understand a word of it,
but, as the undergraduates cheered everything, I
have no doubt it was highly complimentary.”
And this is about what the average solicitor’s
general culture amounts to. Any Latin beyond
ultra vires, locus standi, or primd facie, is Greek
to him.
With the blind partisanship of party-
Government no outrage upon humanity or justice
is allowed to weigh for an instant against the
chance of getting a little cheap mud on to the
other side. The “man named Suffh-ld,” of whose
case Sydney Daily Telegraph affects to be dimly
reminiscent, might apparently rot in gaol ten
times over, as far as that paper is concerned,
sooner than that there should be a chance of his
getting justice upon what it is pledged to con-
sider the wrong initiative. Suffield, in the judg-
ment of a vast majority of the community, is a
cruelly-wronged man ; yet, though the Telegraph
is probably,, in private, of the same opinion, it
doesn’t hesitate to help destroy his chance of
vindication by insinuating that “ the Opposition’s
sudden interest in the case ” is no more than a
paltry political dodge.
M‘Farlane, M.L.A., alleges that the
N.S.W. Land . Tax authorities deliberately and
habitually alter and falsify the land-tax valuations
—presumably for the purpose of getting more
revenue as the alterations seem to be all in the
same direction. By way of corroboration, he
planks down particulars of twenty cases in the
Illawarra district with the Government assessors’
valuations and the amounts to which, it is alleged,
these valuations were altered in the Tax Office at
Sydney. As a rule, the valuations are just
doubled, though in one case the amount was
apparently increased in an off-hand fashion from
=£7631 to <£30,525. If these statements are true—
and M'Farlane says he is prepared to verify them,
while Acting-Premier Brunker, instead of denying
them, says he has made inquiries, and is “ not in
a position ” to state the result —there should be
another robust shindy ahead.
“IT NATURALLY FOLLOWS."
The Harper-Smith row in Yic. Assembly
gave a week of joy to the Ministerialists. The
Opposition Corner was indeed a corner of opposi-
tion, when the only two men of individuality in
it were wrangling. Harper is the Pharisee of the
House (as opposed to Stiggins Gray, of Prahran),
and R. M. Smith is its dilletante. Harper has
long been vaunted as the coming Treasurer of the
Tories, and it is significant that no explicit nomi-
nation was made for any other portfolio. Harper
was indeed a Tory ideal—toney, churchy, narrow-
minded, right-thinking, and a capitalist. And
now, where are the loves of long ago ? Gone for
ever. Harper is anathema, or words to that effect.
“Just for a handful of silver he left us,” quotes
R. M. Smith, moaningly ; “we that had loved
him so, followed him, honored him, lived in his
mild and magnificent eye, learned his great lan-
guage, caught his clear (Doric) accents, made him
our pattern to live and to die ! ”
That Tasmanian political antique, Adye
Douglas, has been foaming lately in denunciation
of Federation. Tasmanians, he says, can live
happily and enjoy their liberty without being
federated, and he is particularly bitter against New
South Wales and Victoria.—
Politician, old and weird,
With the patriarchal beard,
What in Sheol maketh thee
Wag that beard so wrathfully ?
Would thou see thy little Spot
(Charming Land of Sleep a Lot)
Slowly perish of dry rot ?
Or, while other States advance,
Lie in cataleptic trance ?
Federation is the fate
All things meet with, soon or late.
Waits there not for us the dread
Federation of the Dead ?
Be at peace, then ! Cease thy roar !
Wag thy beard in wrath no more.

imagines that he is doing a clever thing in getting

to pull the beer pump in his bar. Such engage-

Big biz. £or a fortnight, and then the pub. loses the
rnaua it has taken years to attain.
Dear Bulletin, — It is not true, as stated,
that a S.A. capitalist paid off the =£looo mortgage
on the house left by George Ash, M.P. The
money devoted to that purpose was the proceeds
of the late legislator’s life-assurance policy, and
the total amount of the sum raised for the educa-
tion of his five children was less than =£2so.
Though he was the strongest fighter on the Con-
servative side of S.A. Assembly, the capitalists
soon forgot him and his services after his death. —
Yours', &c., C. K. Wilton, Adelaide, Aug. 15.
Yet another story of Melbourne’s late
gleeful Coroner, Dr. Youl. One day the doctor’s
sanctum was invaded by a lady with a hard, work-
a-day phiz, and a brogue as thick as Pentridge
hominy. “Ye’re inqueshtin’ Malone the day?”
said the visitor. “ I have one Denis Malone on
my list,” replied the Coroner. “ I’m his broken-
hearted widdy, so I am. Malone shwalleyed a
ha’f-sovereign wanst. If ye come across it fwhin
yez’re inqueshtin’ himself, sure I don’t moind
lettiri’ ye have ha’f-a-crown fer yer throuble.”
Yet another of the “ old hands ” who con-
nect the convict - days with the present, has
gone. Solomon Blay, ex-convict and ex-public
executioner, died last week at Hobart. Solomon
was transported in 1837, appointed Govt, man-
butcher in ’47, pardoned in ’57, hanged his last
man in ’B7, and now dies in ’97. Also, he had
seven letters in his Jewish-Christian name, and in
his day he made hundreds of men “ throw seven.”
He was the original of the “Absalom Day” in
Price Warung’s Bulletin tale, “ Absalom Day’s
Promotion. ”
Ex-Treasurer Ward, of M.L., who resigned
his seat and filed a very lamentable schedule
simultaneously, has been re-elected by his South-
land constituents by a large majority. Nobody
really knows for certain whether M.L. law allows
an uncertificated insolvent to sit in the Legislature
or not; anyhow, the Opposition intends to raise
Tophet over the matter when Parliament meets.
On the other hand, the Government, it is
rumored, is thinking of taking Ward back into
the Ministry, and, if it does, the row will be some-
thing memorable. The Ward row promises to be
as enduring as the Adelaide hospital scuffle.
A wail from the Far North
Editor Bulletin, —During the Diamond Jubilee festival
(such as it was) held here (Normanton, N.Q.) 1 out of
sympathy and good fellowship (perhaps bad fellow-
ship) gave a nigger one bottle of “ colonial ” beer. The
aboriginal had no sooner received it than a constable
established behind a water-tank came out and seized it,
the consequence being that I, a music-teacher, was sum-
moned and fined £3 and 6s. 6d. costs. Take two other
cases of the same in this town. One hotel-keeper was
fined £1 for serving an aboriginal (two bottles), another
fined £1 ss. for supplying a darkey with one bottle of
beer. The poor music teacher through good-naturedly
entering into the festivity of the season had to pay a fine
three times the amount imposed upon a licensed vic-
tualler ! —Mjjsicus. [Now wanted—the other side of this
yarn ?—Ed. B.]
Minister Carruthers presented a fine ex-
ample of dog-meanness when he alleged, in N.S.
Wales Assembly last week, that ex-Premier Dibbs
had “ practically told ” the unemployed to go and
eat grass. The story used to be that Dibbs actually
told the unemployed to eat grass—a yarn of the
first French Revolution which some dull perjurer
fastened on to the long and stormy K.C.M.G.
Crick promptly gave Carruthers the lie, but
had to back down because the latter didn’t
say that Dibbs said the thing in question—only
that he “practically” said it. “Practically”
made all the difference. Yet if someone had said
that a member was “practically” a thief or a
drunkard or the father of many illegitimate
children !! !
N.S.W. Leg. Council—that House of Fat
Men which Premier Reid was going to reform but
didn’t—wants to call the Commonwealth a
“ Dominion,” and to run the Federal Parliament
with unpaid politicians. Also, it proposes that the
power of levying customs and excise duties —the
only thing of any importance left to the Federal
Legislature—should be taken away from it. This
presumably means that all the diverse tariffs would
rage separately just as they do now, and under
these circumstances it wouldn’t matter much
whether the thing it is proposed to create is called
a “Commonwealth” or a “Dominion” or any-
thing else. But if it were called a Kilkenny cat
—just a plain, ordinary Kilkenny eat —it would
fill the bill as well as anything.
The Yarrabah (Q.) Mission Station blacks
have actually turned to horticulture, the station
having undertaken to grow a constant supply of
the papaw for the Cairns preserving-works for
sauce-making. Papaw-sauce is becoming a big
thing.
Refreshing to see that even the S.M.
Herald bears tribute to the sterling qualities of
the late Charles Lilley. “He was a man of the
masses rather than the classes, and, contrary to
the general rule, the Supreme Court Judgeship
did not alienate him from the liberal instincts and
sympathies of his earlier period.” Which, how-
ever true, is rather rough upon Supreme Court
Judges, and must surely have been unintentional
on the Herald’s part. Lilley and Higinbotham
are honorable exceptions to the rule that Judges
are of the classes not the masses.
“ One of the most impressive things at the
London Guildhall Jubilee ball,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “was the herald’s
announcement of the arrival of a gentleman whose
name and rank had been given to the flunkey as
1 Alderman Llewellyn Jones, of Sydney, member
for Petersham in the House of Parliament of New
South Wales.’ Stricken to his bulbous calves by
the majesty of the situation, the human phono-
Sraph ejaculated, ‘ Alderman Sydney, of New
outh Jones,’ and, held up by the Mayor’s hand
the strangely-labelled article passed on to ob
livion.”
Premier Turner appears to be quite un
aware of the painful fact that he lost his head on
Jubilee Day. Speeching at Melb. Town Hall, on
the morning of his return to graft, he said : “We
were cheered from one end of the procession to the
other,” which implies a fond belief that the mob
recognised George Turner, and was expressing a
fervent admiration for his Australian character
But Rickards explains the matter with a cine*
matographe picture. If Turner cares to drop into
the Opera House at about 10.15 p.m., any night of
the week, he may see himself and Mrs. T. inciting
the populace to bursts of enthusiasm by promis-
cuous waving of a hat and handkerchief, whilst
George Reid glares at their artless conduct through
his solitary eye-glass.
Henry Lawson’s book of prose stories
gets an excellent notice from London Academy—
a journal of very fair critical repute. “ A real
book, a book in a hundred,” the writer calls it in
quoting long extracts from those well-known Bul-
letin sketches “Steelman’s Pupil” and “The
Drover’s Wife.” Lawson’s “ feeling for style in
his own person is poor, but when he makes another
speak his language is terse, supple, and richly
idiomatic’ He can tell a yarn with the best.” As
compared with Bret Harte, “ the Californian
writer thinks of the story before anything else,
the Australian of the human document.” And so
on, winding up with the statement that the book
“can be recommended to every one who likes
full-blooded, man-to-man writing.” Lawson, by
the way, gets no direct return from the English
reputation of his books. He sold both his verse
and his prose when he was hard up for about .£l7O,
and instead of drawing a legitimate income from
the sales of the cream of his work for ten years,
he has now to turn to and sweat his brain afresh.
“ Launceston ” to The Bulletin
Go-as-you-please justice in a southern town : A man
shot his mate during a drunken brawl. Coroner’s ver-
dict, after hearing evidence clearly setting forth that
accused was drunk at the time of the affray, was one of
manslaughter. At. the Supreme Court accused was
charsred with murder, but offered to plead guilty to
manslaughter. His request was granted—three years.
The Judge evidently agreed with the coroner’s jury that
prisoner was intoxicated when he shot hismate. Nextday
the police proceeded against the licensee of an hotel for
supplying prisoner, an intoxicated person, with liquor.
The police Super, swore that, in his opinion, prisoner was
not drunk ; he also said the coroner’s jury verdict was a
farce—thereby inferring that the Supreme Court sentence
was also a farce. The licensee was acquitted. To sum
up : A Supreme Court was so satisfied that drink was
responsible that it allowed prisoner to label his own
crime. The police Super, afterwards says accused was
sober, and practically tells the Supreme Court it is an
ass of the most aggravated sort. The Super.’s declaration
certainly saved a publican a £5 fine, but that doesn’t look
much of a reason for telling a Supreme Court it doesn’t
know its business. Truly, the ways of the law are various.
Melb. “ Recent demise Attorney Clever-
don. When in partnership with Eccleston, they
pretty well mopped up all the Wesleyanio busi-
ness—no clique sticks together like the Methodist.
Since parting they have divided the spoil;
Eggleston nipped his full half. He has the pres-
tige of being the son of one of the pioneer clergy,
which did not prevent him from being a crack
cricketer of the East Melb. Club, while the present
eminent lawyer Riggall was another, along with
Dan Wilkie, a third clever ‘ limb.’ Eggleston has
since developed musical enthusiasm. Cleverdon
never was beard of in anything outside that
jealous mistress, the Law, and her profitable
acquaintance, the Church. Like demised Wes-
leyan Cutts amid the medicos, Cleverdon had the
full and worthy confidence of that Goshen cenacle,
which is supposed to be very lugubrious, but, in
reality, enjoys the fat things. Nathless, there are
no Melb. lawyer-fortunes nowadays like those of
the good old times of the Duerdins, Clarks, Klin-
genders, Moules, Seddons, Vaughan3— et hoc.”

Recovery.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS FOR PALE
PEOPLE SCORE ANOTHER VICTORY.
INTERMITTENT FEVER IN ITS WORST
STAGE CURED.
TRANSFORMATION FROM A LIVING
SKELETON TO A HEALTHY,
ROBUST MAN.
Mr. Cornelius O’Neil, of 193 Cumberland-street,
Sydney, is a well-preserved man of about p 5 years
of age. He has led a chequered career since his
arrival in the colonies some 32 years ago, and he
attributes an illness he recently had entirely to the
trying climate of Northern Queensland, where he
pursued a variety of callings all more or less of a
health-impairing nature. _
To a reporter who recently interviewed him he
stated :
“ I have been suffering off and on for the last
seven years with what in Queensland is called
intermittent fever. It is caused by damp and
exposure, and the fever is always prevalent.
The symptoms are cold chills, severe pains across
the loins and intolerable pains in the. head.
I have had doctors without end, and every
conceivable patent and other medicine, but
neither doctors nor physic afforded me
the slightest relief. A lady friend of my wife’s
who called one day to see us, seeing me lying so
prostrate, said, ‘ Why don’t you try Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills? You know how badly I suffer from
rheumatism ; well, since I have been taking Pink
Pills I feel as if I had taken a new lease of life, and
this winter is the best I have passed for many a
year. I am sure it is entirely due to the Pink
Pills of Dr. Williams’.’ Well, this lady spoke so
emphatically of their good qualities that I deter-
mined to try them, bought some boxes and com-
menced taking them in the manner recommended
an the wrapper.”
“ How did you feel after taking the first box,
Mr. O’Neil?”
“ My appetite improved, but otherwise I did
not feel much better, but after the second box I
felt A lot better, so I went steadily on with them.
I was taking at this time three pills a day, one
after each meal. It was only when I suffered
from a terrible tightness across the c hest that I
took two at a time, and then this feding would
disappear as if by magic. Added to this the
agonising cold chills across the loins have entirely
left me. It is Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills that have
done it, and nothing else, as, with the exception
of opening medicine, I have taken no other. Had
you seen me a few months ago you would not have
known me. I was reduced to a shadow, and felt
a burden to myself and to others. I now feel_ a
different man, and cannot speak too highly in
praise of this wonderful medicine, which has.
brought me from death’s door to health and
strength. I was for years a master-butcher in
Queensland and in this colony ; I have also been a
gold-digger. lam sure this account of my sick-
ness and recovery will be read with interest by
many of my old friends in Queensland and New
South Wales.”
In men, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People effect a radical cure in all cases arising
from worry, overwork, or excess of any nature.
They are not a purgative, but they restore pale
and yellow complexions to the glow of health, and
are a specific for troubles peculiar to the female.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a sure cure for
rheumatism, neuralgia, paralysis, locomotor
ataxy, St. Vitus's dance, nervous headache,
scrofula, chronic erysipelas, influenza’s after-
effects, &e. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are obtain-
able from all leading chemists, or from the Dr.
Williams Medicine Co pany, Queen’s Place,
Sydney, who will forward, post paid, on receipt of
sta 1 or post-office order, one box for 2s. 9d., or
hai. . dozen for 15s. 3d.

[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
Just a word, Toy tiinef.nl brethren, who evolve the
peuple’6 Song, ?*««' -
For your irtonbnful. Tueubrations do your readers
grievous JVrohg :
Where’s the usg of chanting dirges to the “ men
we might have been
Better draw the curtain gently o’er the disappoint-
ing scenes
It but tells of fair beginnings, and of prospects
fair and bright,
And of how the reign of Folly brought us to that
piteous plight ;
Where’s-the pathos in the waster who with For-
tune broke his lance,
To the great unmurmuring millions who have
never had a chance ?
If you note the Misanthrope, and boom disciple
of the Sad,
You will have the sorry prospect of a nation mor-
bid mad,
Crooning ghastly, maudlin dirges of the “ people
they should be ”
Had they but the moral courage e’en to wish fair
destiny,
For the wish in things politic stands paternal to
the act,
But desire must have conception, and precede
accomplished fact.
Therefore give the cue of what may be a Nation
great and free,
And the whispered wish will soon be voiced in
shouts of Liberty.
Let us put the matter calmly; let us tear away
disguise ; -
Let us show how subtle tyrants pose as Saints in
people’s eyes.
It is not the stern oppressor who grinds victims
under heel,*
But the godly,, sleek-faced humbugs, tongue of oil
and heart of steel.
Let us show the hidden dangers: let us show the
dreadful fate,
Arm to arm in swift encounter is a custom out of
date.
In our warfare specious cunning must be met by
sharper brains,
And the one who’s best enarmoured is the one who
victory gains.
When you sing,' my tuneful brethren, give your
clearer vision scope,
Pitch your songk>f ardent pleading in the ecstacy
of I lope.
Weave your words with rythmic measure so to
please the people’s ears,
Let them stir the soul and strengthen, and dispel
their idle fears.
Better sing the hopes of millions, sing in burning
words of fire,
Though the breath.of Nation’s passion light your
own funereal pyre,
Happy in the certain augury that, Phoenix-like,
will rise,
The suffusing- flame of Consciousness to light our
just emprise.
D. HEALEY.

STBANGE, BUT TBUE.
“ Matches are a nuisance any way you fix them.’’
“ How do you make that out?”
“ Well, if you take only one to light your lamp it
invariably goes out, and if you take two you always
have to put one back in your pocket.”
A CASE OF CONSCIENCE.
Junior Partner : “The salesman in our cycle de-
Eartment says he won’t perjure his soul another week
y lying about our goods, and stating they are as
good as Red Birds. It will be difficult for us to fill
his place.”
Senior Partner : “ How much is he getting ?”
Junior Partner : “Three pounds a week.”
Senior Partner : “ Offer him JES and a commission."
Some cyclists, although they may not have passed
their first season awheel, know more about cycles
and their construction (so they claim) than those who
have spent y’ears in the business. Cycle-riding and
the judging of cycles, from a conversational stand-
point, seem to be dead easy.
Tourist : “ Say, guide, what can I see in Rome?”
Guide : “ Old and historic ruins.”
Tourist: “ Ruins be darned ! What do I want to
see ruins for? I sold cheap cycles before I struck it
rich, and saw enough ruin to last me my natural life.
We all couldn’t sell Red Birds, so I tried other makes.”
Any cycle is good enough until something happens
to it. Then you will wish you had paid the small
difference for a Red Bird.
A fancy list price is no more the sign of a high-grade
wheel than a paper collar is the evidence of a shirt.
Many cheap machines are sold in Australia as high-
gfade. Intending purchasers should be careful atid
not invest in repair-shop products when they can
secure a cycle with a past.
The “ RED BIRD.” They never require repairs.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
M.L. A. Adrian Knox, supporting the Tote Bill
in N.S.W. Assembly, stated that “In Maoriland, last
Sear, close on t 900,000 went through the totalisator. In
.S. YV. certainly not less than £1,500,000 would go
through in the course of a year. The Government share,
or the charity share, would be £50,000, and the clubs
would divide £lOO,OOO among them.”
* * • *
Two winners at Caulfield G.N. meeting, Brito-
marte and Blue Metal, were quoted in the sporting re-
turns at 4 to 1 and 15 to 1. The same winners on a private
Is. tote in Collingwood (Wren’s) paid divs. of £l7s. and £5
—equal to 26 to 1 and 99 to 1.
* * • »
The A.J.C., having taken legal advice, has set
aside a fund of £2OO to resist the payment of £3OO per
annum in rates, on the ground that Randwick Race-
course, as a place of amusement, is exempt. But the
A.J.C. had better let a long-sleeping public lie. For the
privileges obtained from the community the amount of
rates is a verj' small offset.
* » » *
A rather interested party, an owner ot “ tap-
pers,” complained at the A.J.C. annual meeting that too
little money was spent on steeplechasers. But this is a
complaint that the A.J.C. can treat lightly,for the money
already spent on jumping events has given little return
to the club or public. When not cruel and downright
brutal they are verj r drearv affairs.
* * * *
Old Devon, the runner-up for both Cups in ’94,
is still on the racing job, with his iron legs as clean as ever.
Now, if Devon was to recover his best form, Melb. Cup
would be all over, bar the shouting.
* • * •
The perennial Dreamland, who retains, with his
good looks, an aroma of early celebrity, was backed for a
I‘2-furlong race at Flemington on Saturday, and ran
second. But, seeing that- Trent, the winner, will meet
him on better terms in the two Cups, there is not, thus
far, any more reason than usual for expecting the beauti-
ful son of Nightmare to do the great thing he has always
been going to do since he recovered from the strangles.
A heart-hreaking horse to follow, but they do say he is
expected to “spiel” in Melb. Cup, and is the best out-
sider to back at a long shot.
• * * •
Mr. S. R. Kennedy’s argument against jockeys
receiving their fees at scale —“ that they spend it on
’bacca, beer and billiards when they do get it ” —comes
with particularly bad grace from him. Mr. K.’s stud
usually consists of a couple of jumpers, the riders of which
risk a smashing-up every time they mount. If jockeys
were paid according to the risk they ran, jumping-fees
would be so prohibitive that even such an enthusiast as
Mr. Kennedy would be run off the game.
* * * *
Surcingle : “ Byron Moore, like his predecessor,
Indefatigable Bagot, understands neither racing nor
betting. He is a teetotal barman, or brewer’s manager,
like Martin Hood. When Bagot died, all the speculators
went coursing over turf men only for his successor.
Moore was not thought of for a moment in the tips. But
the committee resolved to have just another Bagot—a
virgin, as it were, for y«n%er ci the temple of Joe Thomp-
son Solomon.”
* * ♦ »
An up-to-date racing yarn, casually quoted by
the Argus, contains a solemn warning for Melbourne
punters. It is not more unpleasant, as a statement of
turf trickery, than the spirit in which it is published by
the “ great ” newspaper:—“ A story is going round racing
circles that but for the ‘ instructions to the jockey ’ being
given too late, one of the races at Caulfield on Saturday
might have had a different result. The rider of one horse,
about which there is always a good deal of mystery, was,
the story goes, to be given a signal after he reached the
post whether his horse was to be ‘ a trier.’ The boy was
looking for the signal for the first furlong or two in the
race, and in his anxiety to get it nearlj' fell off. At last
he received the signal, and finding that he was to win if
he could, he set sail after the leaders, but he had lost too
much ground in the early part of the race, and failed to
score. These ‘ clever ’ people very often overreach them-
selves.” The soul of the horse-reporter is not, appa-
rently, disgusted at the fact that backers of the mysterious
animal in question were in danger of not getting a fair
run for their monej' previous to the hoisting of the
signal. He doesn’t howl for an enquiry into this scandal.
Such scandals are of every-day and all-day-long occur-
rence. The representative of “ respectable ” journalism
merely observes with a snigger that racing rogues often
overreach themselves. One of these days the sickened
public will overreach themselves. •

—Prince Albert Edward’s Oakdene, 3yrs.. Bst. and mak-
ing all the running, won anj’how by six lengths. His
Reverence, carrying 9st. 71b., was second. In one of the
6-year-old’s defeats a fortnight hack she finished last, re-
ceiving 2st. from a horse that His Reverence can give 2st.
to, so the extent of this surprising form-reversal can be

onlooking P. of \V. said to the Duke of York was lost in
the dust of the winner’s heels, but it was noticed that the

The Northern Jockey Club, N.S.W., held a very
successful meeting last Friday, when that sterling mare,
Semiramis (who ran second to Amberite in the Hampden
Stakes), easily secured the N.J.C. Handicap (1 mile),
carrying 9st. 51b., and having behind her several lightly-
weighted country performers. She would probably now
be able co hold her own over a mile with the very best of
them.

That splendid high-jumper, Spondulix, has been
resuscitated, and showed some of his old brilliancy by
clearing the bar at 6ft. 6in. recently in Queensland. The
old champion now, however, needs much humoring.
• * • •
The “ stand-in ” owners of racing ponies are
becoming a public nuisance down South. Let any man of
decent appearance attend a pony meeting and ostenta-
tiously risk, say, a fiver, he will be approached by the
5.1.0. to put, say, £lO on a cert, of the 5.1.0.’s for the
next meeting. Should the mug turn up at next meeting
as desired, he is urged not to “spoil the market” by
putting the coin on himself, as the bookies “ won’t lay a
stranger a fair price,” or they “smell rats when a stranger
stuffs it on ” ; so the 5.1.0. works the commission, on con-
dition that he (the 5.1.0.) stands in for half the winnings.
If it comes off, the backer is lucky to get evens about a
6 to 1 winner, but in nine cases out of ten the 5.1.0.
doesn’t own a hair in the pony’s tail and runs the
backer’s money “on hi? own,” or he is a bookmaker’s
lumberer, who whacks the price of the dead meat with
the layer of odds to the mug.
One of these bogus owners, once a bookie’s
clerk, has a rich crop of city mugs to draw upon, lives in
first-rate style, and during his five j'ears’ career on the
illegitimate turf has never been known to risk sixpence of
his own. Verb. sap. , especially to those who think them-
selves very wise on ponies.
Woodford’s win at Randwick has jumped him
into the position of first favorite for the Metropolitan,
but as he is one of the very “uncertain ” type he is far
more likelv to profit layer than backer.
