Bilmartin has one leg shorter than the other ; so
he is able to fool wagerers who bet that they can guess
his height. It all depends which foot he chooses to stand
upon. Reminds one of the Indian officer who bet he
could guess, at sight, the height of a table, and gauged it
at 2ft. 3in., his opponent crying 2ft. 2£in. “ But I mea-
sured it this morning, and j’ou’ve lost. ” “Yes? I saw
j’ou measuring it, and I guessed j'our game ; so I sawed
half-an-inch off each leg as soon as you had gone.”
• * * •
Dr. Gresswell, of Melb. Board of Health, has
denounced chewing-gum as a deleterious dissipation, but,
from the tenor of his remarks, it would appear that the
learned bacteriologist’s opinion arise? from a belief that
the substance is swallowed, whereas every experienced
chewist knows perfectly well that the proper procedure
is to chew the gum for about four hours and then dab the
result on to the hair of your nearest enemy. The man
who would swallow chewing-gum would call his pedal a
treadle, and work it with his instep. There are such un-
wise creatures to be found here and there, of course, and
The Bulletin has heard of one adult gum-swallower, who
now complains that he suffers from dyspepsia.
* * • *
A partyof cyclists made a run, the other Sunday,
to Redcliffe (Q.). Among them was a bikist who was also a
footballer, and he amused himself bj- selecting one man
as his companion and running rings round him back and
forth, as a dog shoots away from his master and comes
racing back again. Arrived at the Redcliffe pub., dinner
was tackled, and the amateur biking footballer began to
run rings round his mate in the m6nu. Soup and fish
having been swallowed, he called for roast pork, and, just
as he was finishing this third assortment, his friend
ordered roast chicken. “ I’ll have roast chicken, too,”
said the A.8.F., in a loud tone of voice. He got and
gobbled it. Someone called for “boiled chicken and
ham.” “I’ll have boiled chicken and ham.” Going,
going, gone. A voice was heard asking for some turkey.
“Fetch me some turkey.” Then he tackled all the
“sweets” seriatim, and was well into the “fruit” by the
time his mate had finished munching his roast 'chicken.
His mate called for butter and cheese. “ Bring me
butter and cheese as well,” roared the A.B.F. This was
rather more than the waiter could stand. The latter’s
jaw dropped for a second or two, and then he managed to
stammer out: “What, after the fruit, sir?” “My
(blanky) troubles whether it’s after the fruit, or before it 1
If j’ou don’t like it, I’ll wade in at the fruit again after-
wards just to oblige you ! ” The A.B. F., being the holder
of a certain club medal, or token, was further entitled to
have his dinner for Is. 6d., instead of the regulation 2s.
And yet the N.S.W. footballers beat the Banana boys
hollow !
Which recalls that a certain game and great
professional cyclist is a terror with a knife and fork. At
a lunch at which a Brisbane cyclist (the same who was
run rings round in the last paragraph) was present, the
pro., after soup, had four “goes” of roast chicken right
off the reel, two more “ goes ” at some other kind of flesh,
two large bottles of beer, sweets, cheese and celery, and
fruit. Then he went out and won a race from scratch.
* * * *
At Gympie (Q.), many years ago, old Bill Hall,
who used to be known as “ Bristol Bill ” until he “ struck
it hot,” after which he was addressed as “Mr. Hall,” and
“ the doctor,” now a well-known Northern M.L.A., went
into a restaurant. Part of the bill-of-fare was roley-poley
currant-duff, and both men ordered some. “This’ere’s
rather a skimpy quantity for a digger with a healthy appe-
tite,” says Bristol Bill. “Oh, well, eat it up, and we ll send
forsome more,’ suggested the doctor. They did so. “Duff
repeat,” sang out the attendant, and a maiden’s slice was
presently laid before each. The doctor’s dander riz—he
was not known on that part of the field, it being away
“off his beat,” as it were—at the words “duff repeat.”
He swallowed his portion in a couple of mouthfuls, and
sent for more. “Duff repeat,” sang out the waiter. “I’ll
keep on sending for more so long as he calls out ‘ duff
repeat,’ ” vowed the doctor. This duel went on for two
or three times more, until, at last, the waiter, thinking to
shame his voracious customer, brought a large dish con-
taining a whole roley-poley pudding about 2ft. 'Bin. long
and placed it with a snort before the doctor. “ Now,
boys,” said the latter, nonchalantly, “ this is the first and
last time you’ll ever get a square meal in this restaurant;
who says‘duff’?” And he cut up the roley-poley into
good 4-inch sections and passed a portion round to those
of his mates who “ signified ” by sending up their plates.
The waiter retired behind the scenes, discomfited.

At the Towers cycling carnival recently, spills
were the order of the day. Porta marred the beauty 'of
his legs hv falling over Inch, and a Townsville wheelman
sustained’the intertown antipathy by collaring a Towers
opponent during a race and pulling him off his bike ; a
free-fight nearly resulted. Track wretched, and racing
consequently more hazardous than interesting, but
Walne’s exhibition compensated spectators.
• • • «
Peculiar that, despite the great boom in cycling
during the past three years, there has been no attempt to
promote a 24-hours’ contest in Australia. So far, only
one race of this sort has been held here—that on the Melb.
Exhibition track, in ’93, which was won by Gus. Chrisp,
with 401% miles. This looks very poor beside the 564%
miles put up by Huret last week, but it must be remem-
bered that Chrisp’s was an unpaced race, and run for 18
hours through drizzling lain.
* * * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen makes a mistake
in holding winter race-meetings They not only don’t
pay, but sicken the public of the sport and put them off
it in the spring and summer. Cycling, like every other
sport, needs a close season. Vic. League has been the
first to recognise this fact, and has showed its good sense
by prohibiting track-meetings during the winter. Oon-
sequently, when the season opens in October, the publio
will take it up again with renewed enthusiasm, not hav-
ing had their ardor damped by wet winter experiences of
slush processions round a frost-bitten track.
* • * *
The conditions of the coming Warrnambool
(Vic.) big road-race include one which looks unfair on the
face, namely, that each protest must be accompanied by
a fee of £l—to be returned should the protest be upheld.
Under which circumstances, a man with a good case
would naturally refrain from risking his E, knowing that,
unless he could prove it right up to the hilt, he would be
simply throwing good money after bad.

• • • •
After the allegations of “towing” so freely
made against about three-quarters of the competitors in
last year’s Scott-Morton road-race, the present Warrnam-
bool-to-Melb. contest will be unpaced. This, however,
does not get over the difficulty of competitors on the same
mark fixing up the pacing between themselves, to the
detriment of the man who is alone. Neither does it pre-
vent the entry of men solely to act as pace-makers for
others.

* * * •
“ All these cycling accidents,” said a scientific
orator talking at the Vic. road-race last Saturday, “ arise
from the pernicious practice of building bicycles from
hollow metal. If these bars were solid, as they should be,
and as the public are deluded into believing them to be,
by these unscrupulous dealers, there would be no
accidents."
* * » *
Of the 25 candidates elected to the Vic. League
of Wheelmen Council last Saturday, barrister Forlonge
headed the list with 627 votes of 749 polled. Dr. Adams
was close up, and all the prominent names in matters
cycling tailed after. Yet neither of the two has been
closely associated with the sport. But possibly that ac-
counted for it, as neither would have had the chance to
make enemies thereby. The old moral : If you wish to
be elected to a club, go up before you are known except
to j our proposers. In fact, go up even before they know
you.
The new premises of the league, formerly those
of the Y.M.C.A., form, after the Melb. Australian and
Athenaeum Clubs, the best club-rooms in Melb., and how
thej’ are to be run on subscriptions of 2s. 6d. a year is in-
comprehensible. Perhaps the committee means to add
glove-fights to the attractions of the place.
* * * *
The Vic. 50-miles Road Championship ended in
a ludicrous finish between Aif. and Charley Middleton,
who had paced each other splendidly throughout, and
who were six minutes ahead of the field. The brothers
slowed down and seemingly tried to put up a dead-heat,
but the judges were keen-eyed, and placed the younger,
Charley, an inch ahead. The lengthy Wedd took third
monej’. The Middletons smashed up the field early, and
were leading before the turn. They turned off the dis-
tance in 2hrs. 43min. 4sec., the previous best being Alf.
Middleton’s last year’s win in 2hrs. 54min. 58sec. How-
ever, it was lucky for the Dunolly men that Green was
in Sydney, for the Englishman would probably have
beaten them all-out as he did at the half-distance.

During the few months he was here, Lesna
lowered nearly every previous record from one mile to a
hundred—or, rather, his pace-makers lowered the records
whilst the little Frenchman hung on. As a handicap and
scratch-race rider he wasn’t nearlj’ up to Walne's best
form, not to mention that of Bilmartin and Megson.
• * * *
Capt. Wynyard, who comes out with Stoddart’s
team, is still remembered in England as the hero of a
great batting performance. In '94, Sussex closed its
innings on the last day of a match with Hampshire, leav-
ing the latter to make 240 in two hours. They astonished
all England by making the runs within five minutes of
time, Wynyard batting brilliantly for 117 not out.
* * * *
Just as well to observe, before we lose our heads
altogether, that the best English batting averages against
the last Australian Eleven were made by Abel, Ranjit-
sinjhi, Gunn, and Jackson, in that order. Of the four
only one comes with Stoddart. In last season’s matches,
taking the first half-dozen counties, Brown headed the
batting-list for Yorkshire, Abel for Surrey, MacLaren for
Lancashire, O’Brien for Middlesex, Owen for Essex, Gunn
for Notts—of whom only MacLaren conies. The first half-
dozen season’s first-class averages were owned by Ranji,
Wynyard, Gunn, Grace, Abel, Jackson —two only coming.
Peel and Hirst alone scored over 1000 runs and took over
100 wickets—neither coming. Thirteen batsmen made
200 and over—three only (Wynyard, MacLaren, and Hay-
ward) are coming.
The defeat of Geelong and Melb. on Saturday
leaves the Vie. premiership practically to the winners of
the Colli ngwood-Essendon contest this week, for there is
little hope for a defeated club. On past observations,
Collingwood should prevail again.
By the death of Dinnie M'Kay disappears one
of the oldest and best of playing Melb. footballers.
M'Kay’s association with senior football dates from ’B7,
when, at the age of 19, he came from Ballarat to take the
position of one of the five “followers” of the S. Melb:
team. With the exception of one season, when he cap-
tained Richmond, M'Kay’s black, cropped head, round
shoulders, and long arms have been always the most pro-
minent features of the South’s ruck, and in the palmy
days of Vic. football M'Kay was one of the heroes of the
hour. The daily papers paragraphed him, the weeklies
pictured him, and the small boys of South Melb. grew up
to speak of Dinnie M'Kay with bated breath. Never a
meteoric player, he was always good, occasionally brilliant,
and the surest kick and mark in the province. At the age
of 29 he dies in the Melb. Hospital, leaving nothing but a
wife and family, dependent on the probleiiiatical goodwill
of the forgetful barraekers who howled his praises in the
past. The fate of Dinnie M'Kay is a thing for every -
popular athlete to ponder over. •»

As a horrible example of Yankee duplicity, the
Argus’s special with the ill-fated Australian baseball-
team quotes one of the ’Murkan bystanders at a match :
“ M'Fetridge is the finest amateur pitcher in America.
He gets lOdol. for playing to-day. He never plays for
less than that amount.” To unprejudiced eyes, the
difference between M'Fetridge, the Yankee “amateur”
baseballer, and Grace, the English “ amateur ” cricketer,
is hard to discern.
• * * *
Mr. Dan Sheehy dropped upon the idea for the
models of his crack yachts, Xarifa and Australian, in a
small book on yacht-building picked up in a Sydney book-
stall. The models were tested in a long tank, by means
of a string passing over a sheave, with weight attached,
the resistance of the various models being sized-up by the
time it took the weight to drag them the length of the
tank. It was found that a circle-segment-shaped model
traversed the tank in the fastest time, therefore showing
the least resistance ; and on these lines Sheehy designed
his cracks : keel, topsides, and every way you took
them being the segment of a circle. Mr. “Tom”
Brown gave Sheehy a big figure for the model of a yacnt,
on which lines Mr. Brown had the old Alick constructed
at his Pyrmont ironworks. She proved a flyer until Mr.
Browm had her lengthened, after which she never did any
good. The lengthening made a flat-sided tub of her.

The latest half-yearly number of J. J. Miller’s
Australian Racing Chronicle (Jan. 31st to July 31st) was
issued about ten days later than the Australian Turf
Register, whereby hangs a tale. The Argus-Australa-
sian proprietary, in bringing out the Register with suvb
amazing promptitude, stated that it had not taken the
trouble to include “public-house and backblocks meet-
ings.” To this the industrious Miller replies with
a circular, calling further attention to the value
of the 10s. Racing Chronicle compared with the
20s. Turf Register (it used to be 30s. before the
opposition show started) by giving a list of 943
meetings not mentioned in the older arid more expensive
volume. Among them is one held at Charters Towers on
Dec. 10th, when the stakes amounted to £220. The total
number of days’ racing recorded in the Chronicle is 1453,
as against 500 given in the Register, therefore Miller
thumps a big-drum, figuratively speaking, and implores
the public not to confuse the ignorant twenty “bob”
Turf Register with the well-informed Australian
Racing Chronicle, published at half the price. Compe-
tition is the soul of horsey history. Previous to the
advent of the Chronicle the horsey history of Australia
walked in darkness, and never had a soul worth men-
tioning.

The management of the forthcoming Axemen’s
Carnival, whose advt. appears in this issue, draws the
attention of intending competitors to the necessity for
early application for entry-forms. So far the Victorian
and YVestralian axemen and sawyers are entering very
freely, but the tree-hewers of Queensland and N.S.W.
seem backward.
• * * *
The heads of the telegraph department of an
Australian province were, some time ago, much concerned
over the destruction of a certain telegraph-pole and con-
sequent interruption of line. The pole had evidently
been chawed in two. Immediately the Tantanoola tiger
was suggested, and the most searching investigations re-
vealed nothing more probable. So the tiger got it. But
the facts were these: The owners of two dogs in the
viciniby arranged to meet and have “ago ” for a £2
wager. Only one man turned up to keep the appoint-
ment, and he waited three hours. Much incensed and
with a wilful waste of obscenity—considering the limited
number of auditors—he sooled his dog on to the nearest
telegraph-post, venting his and his dog’s disgust upon it.
This is to ease the worried mind of over-worked official-
dom and exonerate the much-libelled dead tiger.

Dear Bulletin, —Enquiring for someone’s
office in the City Council’s new fish-market
(Melb,), I was thus directed ! —“Go along to the
right till you come to the third crack in the wall,
then turn to your left till you come to where the
coping-stone has fallen away, then straight on
under the archway till you come to the first over-
head crack, and there you are.” I chanced it. —
F.C.
Our Gracious Queen testifies to the consti-
tutional ''grovel” of the fair sex by her taste in
light li' ;rature. Her favorite novelists, says the
Quarterly Review, are Jane Austen, Charlotte
Bronte, Mrs. Oliphant, Mrs. Craik, George Eliot,
Edna Lyall—not a man amongst them. Also
Adelaide Proctor is bracketted with Shakespeare,
Scott, and Tennyson, -as one of the Monarch’s
“ favorite ” poets.
Vic. Education Department has, under the
whip of the Age, begun to make things hot for
the parents of children who have not been attend-
ing school regularly. A suburban truant officer,
filled with sudden zeal, asked a local bench to im-
pose on a number of summoned parents the full
legal penalties. The bench truculently complied
and fined the parents —some of whom could not
afford to buy boots or clothes, or even enough
food, for their offspring—amounts ranging to 10s.,
in default imprisonment. In many cases the
parents were punished for the non-attendance, for
the required number of days, of their children
during the quarter ended March last.
Tasmanian Parliament adjourned on Friday
last. The last subject debated was the aged un-
employed of Hobart, and it transpired that there
are in that progressive hamlet some 30 or 40
sturdy old fellows who absolutely refuse to go
into the Invalid Depot while they can gee any-
thing to do in the shape of work outside. They
agitated so successfully in the winter that the
Govt, subsidised a private relief-job on which
the wages paid were 3s. a day. The work
is finished, and the old chaps are again
knocking at Ministerial doors and carry-
ing placards round the streets bearing the
legend—“ We don’t ask charity, we want
work! ” On the adjournment debate in the
House Acting-Premier Fysh alleged that the aged
unemployed were not worth the 3s. a day, and the
Govt, couldn’t be bothered with them any more—
they would have to accept the hospitality of the
workhouse. Which means that sturdy, active old
fellows, willing and able to work, are to be sepa-
rated from their wives and whatever families they
may have and wear a pauper uniform. All this
because the Vandemoniah Govt, cannot find
work for 30 old men at 3s. a day ! Fyshy pro-
sperity, surely.
Rev. Isitt, the M.L. Cold Tea Missionary,
told a Melb. audience, the other evening, a har-
rowing tale of a father and mother who took from
the throat of their just-expired child a silver
tracheotomy pipe and pawned it to buy whisky.
Frightful, no doubt, but the writer knows a man
who takes the pipe out of his own throat and
pawns it every time his wife—who holds the purse
—refuses to give him money to buy beer. When
he is on the point of choking she rushes off and
redeems the tube. Rev. Isitt evidently doesn’t
know half the things that alcohol-fiends will do in
order to obtain what to them is literally the vital
fluid. The writer (who has had some curious ex-
periences in this way) remembers being told by the
son of a doctor, who practised ih an inland Vio-
torian town, that one day wheft he (the son) was
desperately in want of liquor, and was without
either money or credit, he unscrewed the skull
from a skeleton left his father by an old friend
and patient, and pawned it —the skull. There
was, he said, a silver-plate in the top of it which
increased its intrinsic value. The irreverent
ruffian actually seemed to think he had done a
clever and humorous thing, and laughed prodigi-
ously as he told the story. He is getting to be an
old man now, but is still —the Rev. Isitt may be
pleased to hear —in the enjoyment of excellent
health and an unabated thirst.
The Old Familiar Juice.
Curiously enough, as a consequence of much
association with teetotallers and Good Templars,
G. D. Clark, the Sydney cold-tea boss, has come
to the conclusion that the world becomes yearly
more drunken. On the other hand, New
South Wales Secretary Bevill, after much ex-
perience of many publicans, is equally convinced
that it grows steadily more sober. Each dis-
putant, in order to support his case, bolsters up
his position and bores the public with figures and
statistics which nobody ever believes in, although
it is true that if any credit is to be given to those
of Mr. Clark they seem materially to support his
opponent’s case. Says Mr. Clark: “ Any satis-
faction arising from a perusal of these figures is
largely discounted by the reflection that the de-
crease in the number of public-houses is almost
balanced by the increase in the number of wine-
licenses.” The figures spoken of are given by Mr.
Clark himself. Elsewhere this Jeremiah tells us
that “while the number of public-houses per head
of the population is steadily decreasing, there is a
continual growth in the wine-licenses, with the
result that the consumption of alcohol and the
arrests for drunkenness show no perceptible fall-
ing-off. In support of this statement he tells us
that the arrests for drunkenness in 1891 were
2026 per 100,000 of population, whilst in 1895 they
were only 1672. It is said that figures cannot lie.
The same statement is not universally true of
those who use them, or of some of their converts.
* * • *
It is always drink that is at fault. Suppose
that we were to examine some other agents or
institutions in the same way, and were to work
out statistics and figures. Imagine that a curious
person should take it into his head to investigate
the effects of evening church services upon
the morals of the people. What would he
find, do you think ? How many a repentant
or, perhaps, unrepentant Magdalen would be
ready to aver (perhaps with truth) that her
first departure from the paths of virtue dated from
evening church, or Sunday-school, or Salvation
Army meeting, or Chinese-mission Sunday class ?
Put her down as one more in the 100,000 of popu-
lation and presently you will have figures hope-
lessly condemnatory of evening church. In the
same way it may be that we shall be able to prove
the danger which lurks for the young in our pub-
lic parks, and can show that they ought all to be
turned into railway-stations—if, indeed, danger to
morals does not lurk in these also, and in
everything except a cemetery. Nay, for only
the other day a cemetery was given by a female
witness as the place of her seduction —she had
yielded to the tempter at her own mother’s grave.
Also, we are tired of hearing of the “ statements
of Judges and prison officials.” From the dawn
of history down to the days of the late sainted
Butler every man who ever made an ass or a rogue
of himself has always been anxious to say (if he
could): “ It was not I (I being by necessary impli-
cation a noble creature, and once upon a time a
dear, little, curly-headed Sunday-school scholar,
perhaps); it was the drink ! Oh, my dear and
reverend sir, drink it was that brought me to
this,” &c., &c. For this kind of confession is not
only good for the soul, but sometimes for the
body also, if all tales be true ; and, after all, when
all is said and done, the world, which has more
common-sense than the law and more charity than
the church, does accept drunkenness as an excuse
for much which it would find very difficult to for-
give upon any other consideration. This fact is so
thoroughly recognised that very sober persons have
been found to allege inebriety in themselves upon
occasions when it has been imperceptible to all
witnesses less expert than the average policeman.
But, as a matter of fact, there is no need whatever
to dispute or refute Mr. Clark’s figures. They
ought to be welcome. They show, upon Mr.
Clark’s own statement, that Australia is upon the
right track. This is a wine-growing country, and
it ought to be a wine-drinking country; and the
more that wines and the less that spirits are
drunk, the more will the so-called drink-question
tend to disappear, because the only question that
there is is not of quantity of alcohol, but of the
dilution of it.
* • * *
The world is wiser than any individual in it,
even than Mr. Clark. The world knows what is
good for it better than he. And because the world
knows that alcohol (a substance which has a
strange way of making itself out of almost any-
thing, and almost without provocation) is good for
it, the world has gone on drinking alcohol from
the dawn of history, and very likely right through
the night which lay behind that dawn. And, if
Mr. Clark wants statistics to amuse himself with,
let him get hold of the histories of the alcohol-
drinking races and compare them with those of
the nations which have abstained. In Europe,
the Turks are the abstinent race. This is given
as a hint towards this interesting enquiry ; and if
Mr. Clark will pursue it honestly he will learn
some surprising facts, and perhaps furnish us with
a fresh table to show that the dominant and pro-
gressive races have always been drinkers of alcohol,
whilst the degraded and decadent have been ab-
stainers.
* * * *
The truth of the matter is that men, like other
animals, eventually settle down to the diet that is
best for them, whether in meat or drink. And if
alcohol were not a good thing it would not be used
so universally by the dominant races of the world.
The world at large does not go on using what is
bad for it merely because it is pleasant. Opium is
pleasant, and so are cocaine, and ehlorodyne, and
arsenic, and a number of other things. But the
mass of mankind does not take to them on this
account; and nature takes care not to let those
who indulge in them increase and multiply beyond
a certain point. If he were really disobeying her
laws she would do the same’to the'alcohoi drinker
instead of keeping him always in the van of the
nations. The fact that she does so proves a good
deal more than all the statistics that ever were
got together ; and the fact that in the last resort
almost every doctor who is not a crank flies to
alcohol to tide over a deadly crisis, is a strange
commentary upon its alleged action as a deadly
poison to the healthy human frame.
* * * »
If the prohibitionists don’t care about the con-
clusions of common-sense, there is another reason
to be urged in favor of the moderate use of their
pet antipathy. The lives of the majority of
people in this world are not too happy ; and it is
to be feared, if leading temperance apostles speak
truly, that they cannot expect to have muoh op-
portunity of satisfying their thirst in the next.
Fermented liquors bring a great deal of pleasure
and very little harm to all but an inconsiderable
minority of the human race : and, even if they
produce a certain amount of injury, what is pleasant
is worth paying for, if the price be not too high.
Those who have looked upon the merry face of
the average temperance advocate may well doubt
whether it is not possible to pay too dearly for the
joys of teetotalism.

Dear Bulletin, —Can you or any of your
readers set me right with regard to the real origin,
or authorship, of the following fantasy ?
The Earth was lifeless. The Race of Man was dead.
The Resurrection and the Day of Judgment were yet to
come. The Lord Almighty and the Angel Gabriel, who
had his trumpet tucked under his wing, were taking a
final stroll over the played-out planet. They came across
a stately monument bearing the inscription
IN MEMORY
OF
MANKIND
Erected by the Last Survivors of the Race.
Hequiescat in Pace !
“ Poor devils ! ” said the Lord, “ I’ll take them at their
word. You may put up your trumpet, Gabriel 1 There
will be no Resurrection.”
I always had an idea that I had read this (or
something like it) in Dean Swift’s writings, some-
where or other. But, after an exhaustive search,
I have not been able to find it in any of his works.
Surely I could not have dreamt it?—Y.J.D.
Queensland Minister for Agriculture was
showing M‘Cord, M.L. A., a well-known squatter,
a photo, of a certain dairy-herd bull the other day.
“A regular mickey!” said M'Cord, contemptu-
ously. “If I found such a scrubber among my
herd he’d be shot immediately.” The Minister
then calmly assured the irate squatter that the
animal in question was a well-known Victorian
prize-winner. “Ah, well,” remarked M'Cord,
frankly, “it wouldn’t have done for me to have
been one of the judges ! ”
Sheehan, the recent Maoriland murderer,
was hanged as privately as possible, no press
representatives being admitted. It is now acknow-
ledged that the authorities looked for something
particularly gruesome and wanted it kept dark.
Sheehan was such a physical wreck that it was
half-expected he would fall to pieces before the
ceremony. He had heart-disease, and one possi-
bility was that he might drop down dead just as
the show was beginning. Also, he had huge
goitrous swellings on the neck which kept the rope
off his windpipe, and also made it difficult to break
his neck by a fall —the goitre acted as a sort of
spring or cushion to ease the shock. A patent
india-rubber device was contrived to press on the
wind pipe and effectually scrag the proposed corpse,
but it being impossible to actually experiment
with it in advance its success was problematical.
If it failed, then the law would be confronted by
the fact that Sheehan was practically an unhang-
able individual, and it would be necessary, as one
local paper expressed the matter, to “ finish him
some other way.” As this might possibly mean
attacking him personally with an axe the advisa-
bility of having no newspaper men on hand to
write up the sensation was sufficiently obvious.
Apparently, however, the thing got through,
despite all these difficulties, without any serious
hitch. Still, if there must be executions, the
advisability of obliterating the condemned by
closing the ventilator of his cell at night, and
turning on carbonic acid gas, seems to stick out
conspicuously. Then there would be no gallows, no
thud, no hangman, no greased rope, and nobody
present except the one man chiefly concerned —
unless some of the ghouls that haunt executions
cared to go in and smother along with him for the
sake of seeing the performance. Also, gas is
equally adapted to men of all shapes. It fits the
most knobby and gnarled and triangular indivi-
dual just as well as any other.
The Bulletin begins to have an idea that
•hanging is hereditary, or infectious, or something.
Not very long ago an ex-hangman was hanged in
New South Wales. Sheehan wasn’t an ex-hang-
man ; but a very near relative of his, it is alleged,
was once an applicant for the billet and was within
an ace of being appointed, only another strangler
cut in and took the job at non-union rates. In
that indirect fashion hanging got into the Sheehan
family, and, after suffering from suppressed execu-
tion for a number of years, it broke out openly,
and the disease came to the surface, so to speak.

Cowya station, N.S.W., has just finished
shearing 4200 mixed hoggets which have cut 100
bales of wool, bales averaging 3cwt. 2gr. 10lb. —
roughly, about Bflb. per sheep of really first-class
wool. These sheep are bred on Cowya, and art
the progeny of Yarrawin and Springfield rams.

A ticklish question wrts opened up by the
fining of a Westralian milkman, the other day.
The offence was the selling of milk not up to
standard quality. Analysis did not say the milk
was watered, but that after leaving the cow it was
“ not up to standard quality,” that is—deficient in
nutriment. At this rate, dairymen will have to
beware of poor cows.
Centralia labors under a serious grievance
in having the 24th parallel fixed as the tick quar-
antine line. As a matter of fact, the tick is con-
fined principally to the coast, and the running of
a quarantine line due west out to plains and downs
where the tick cannot live is simply an absurdity.
Southern stock-owners, however, are hugely
pleased at the idea ot shutting out Centralian
competition, and insist on the maintenance of the
line. They do not see that they are simply offer-
ing a premium to induce Centralians to help the
tick a little further Southwards !
A hint for Northern stockowners. Texas
graziers, finding their cattle deteriorating, sent
North for bulls, but the bulls, when entering
tick-infested country, suffered severely from fever,
and often 105t.60 or 70 per cent, of their number.
Whereupon the smart Texans took a few handfuls
of ticks North, placed them on the bulls in the
home pastures, waited till the animals became
fairly immune, and then travelled them South-
wards with scarcely any loss at all.

“ I never bolt my bath-door. For fear of
an accident you may meet with a worse.” — Auto-
biography of an actress.
Dear Bulletin, —Met a navvy just leav-
ing work in the sewers at South Melb. to-day.
He is in a tunnel below Hanna-st., a sort of flat
gully between Emerald Hill and St. Kilda-road.
He showed me some silver coins he had in his
pocket —they were quite black. Said the candles
they used continually burn dim and occasionally
go out. He suffers from bad eyesight since work-
ing there. Also, the face of the drive, though
shored up as fast as possible, has, two or three
times, oozed in, and they had to run for it. This
fo'r»6s. fid. per day.— Melb.
The Tongans have puzzling ways of ex-
pressing degress of relationship'. For instance, a'
woman whose married sister had a family of boys
and girls, instead of these being called nephews
and nieces they would all be called sisters, boys
and girls alike ; and, if any of them married, their
children would be her sisters too. All a man’s
mother’s relations are called mother, and his
father’s are called father. A Tongan will
frequently introduce to you a girl younger than
himself as grandfather, because she is descended
from his grandfather. It is more difficult to un-
derstand as one can be several relations at
once to another. No white has ever been able to
understand this curious custom, but it appears to
be perfectly clear to the native intellect.
The Appreciation of Cattle. — An aver-
age of .£9 13s lOd. for 83 bullocks at Homebush
on Monday was the biggest price obtained there
for this number for years. Pitt, Son, and Bad-
fery, Ltd., sold them on account of W. and T. C.
)ickson, of Yarrawin, Brewarrina.
The Law sometimes transcends itself in
asiiiinity. Chief Justice Madden felt called upon
to make the following remarks in the Melb.
Divorce Court the other day:—
Under the Amended Crimes Act, a man who has rela-
tions with a girl under 16 is liable to imprisonment for a
period not exceeding 10 years. ... I have already
seen eases in this court in which a man, who was liable to
punishment under this Act, married the girl in order to
escape the criminal proceedings, and, having married her,
started to ill-treat her from the very beginning, so that
she hiis been compelled to obtain a divorce.
In other words, the man, having incurred the
vengeance of the Law, took refuge from that venge-
ance under the wing of the Law, and then goaded
the girl into setting the Law in motion, with the
result that the Law relieved him of the responsi-
bilities he had taken upon himself in order to
escape from the Law. So, in a somewhat cir-
cuitous fashion, he. became as he was before he
committed the offence for which he would have
been imprisoned several years, if he had not known
how to cause the Law to make a double and triple
ass of itself.
The Royal Geographical Society has offered
to contribute <£sooo towards the cost of an Ant-
arctic expedition if each of the Australias will
plank down a like amount. Lord Lothian, a
magnate of the Society, said he trusted that none
but British names Would be given to lands and
islands which are really the Hinterland of Aus-
tralia and South Africa. Great Orion ! Nothing
less than the whole Antarctic circle for a British
backyard will satisfy this Scotchman! As
Britain already owns a considerable portion of the
earth, and is “ Mistress of the Seas,” there is no
reason why she shouldn’t also possess both Poles,
and control the weather, and the winds, and cur-
rents, and the Gulf Stream, and the Law of
Gravity, and little things of that sort, and charge
'other nations for the use of them. Hodge Land
would, perhaps, suit Lord Lothian as a name for
the Antarctic Continent. But while giving names
in this way, why not at once call the earth the
Planet Bull? "
A Boon to Sufferers.— A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address —J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A. #
Dear B. —Some great man said that a
mathematician does not make a good lawyer.
Napoleon said he could not be a great soldier.
Now, I am mathematical but was never thought a
legal authority (except as regards mining law)
until a half-system cockie asked my advice
as to a summons for cutting a rabbit-proof
fence. The land on the shandygaffer’s side
belonged to the squatter, the Hon. Harold
Coeur-de-lion, on the other leasehold of ditto.
“Plead mateship,” said I. Then before the
beak Mr. Cockeye pled: “As John Cock-
eye, gentleman, not guilty; as John Cock-
eye, manager for Cockeye and Coeur-de-lion,
farmers, guilty. If your worships will impose a
light fine, payable out of our crops, we shall be
grateful.” Case dismissed, because the plaintiff
was also the defendant, through his mate and
agent. And it’s law !— Scotty the Wrinkler.

Influenza and Rheumatism,
FORMIDABLE FOES MASTERED.
Among those who speak in grateful terms of the
wonderful curative powers of Dr. Williams’Pink
Pills is Mr. William Carroll, of Sydney, Manitoba,
Canada. Mr. Carroll says-:—“Following an at-
tack of influenza, my whole system was so
weakened that it was with difficulty I could do
any work. In this condition I was an easy prey
to my old enemy, rheumatism, and I suffered the
agony of its pains in almost every bone in my
body. I doctored a great deal, but without any
beneficial results, and I spent seventeen dollars for
one medicine largely advertised as a cure for rheu-
matism, without the least benefit. I naturally felt
despondent, and thought the trouble had fastened
itself upon me in permanent form. I had read
so much about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills that I
at last determined to give them atrial. I can’t
say that I felt anything in the way of beneficial
results until I had used the third box, but at that
juncture the encouragement was so great that I
determined to continue the treatment, and I used
the Pink,Pills throughout the winter, constantly
gaining in strength. When spring arrived, I
found myself better than I had been for years.
Every vestige of rheumatism had disappeared, and
although 10 months have passed since I took the
last pill, I have not had the slightest return of the
trouble. I therefore think it is but right that I
should make known the good that I have received
from the famous Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I
hope my experience maybe the means of putting
some other sufferer on the road to renewed health.
W. JNO.

is simplicity itself.”

A Rise in the River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the ’Bidgee has been low all the summer, I
when the lagoons are either brick or stinking
mud, when the men from outback are camping for
grass or water, when the squatters are swearing j
and the cookies are whining, we wait for a rise in
the river. We fishers of the rivers love the low i
waters, and we prefer a falling river; but we !
don’t care that our lines should be drawn up a j
cable of grass-green silk, the baits a mermaid’s
wig—which, according to Byron, is also green—
Where the mermaid is decking
Her green hair with shells. '
My rise, however, though actually in the river
was quicker than the snow-waters of September,
and more acceptable than the red rush caused,by
“six inches at Gundagai, still raining heavily ”
as posted outside Howlong P.O.
Those were good times, fifteen years er so ago.
We had cheques, and “did them up” carefully on
colonial wine at a shilling a bottle —or even per
billy; and we invited all swagmen to share, and
co-operated with the welcome, self-invited ladies
of the flat—the Rat, Molly Mills, Mothers Burn-
away and Warren. And on Sundays the upper
circles would look in—Mesdames Bumbleton and
W estaway, the Misses Bubble, Squeak, and
Hornet. _ i
It was better to break my tibia and fibula, to
get patched up in Wangaratta hospital, than to
die of new wine and rats on the brain on the river-
bank, as Vast Heaving did. No one knew the
name of Vast Heaving—even I did not; and oyer
his grave to this day you will see V.H. —nothing
more.
There was a nice girl called the Rat; she is nice
now, but not a girl. Her name is Idina Kap- J
lopski, although she is still called the Rat. She
alone saw Vasty die ; and until last year I never
met her since he pegged out.
I camped for a week on the old spot, and had
rolled up to go, when there was Martha in front of
me, older, but neater and jollier than ever, and
with the inevitable bottle of Howlong (O Lord !)
wine in her hand.
“ You’re not much changed, Peter.” “You’re
at the old game, Ratty.” That’s all we said
before the wine. Then Martha told me how my
mate had shifted his camp when I left, and had
lived alone until she found him dying. He left a
message : “ Tell Peter that there’s money for him
in the Red Bank, and that he’ll know the initials
and draw it.”
Now, there are two banks in Howlong ; they are
both drab-colored now, but one used to be painted
an abominable maroon color, executed cheaply by
a German wine-bibber. No money had been left
there by Mr. V. Heaving, Mr. Vasty, or any
other swagman. I gave the conundrum up.
Then Martha said : “ Let’s go and see where he
died. He carved his name in front of the tent on
a big gum tree.” There it was and is : V.H. 22
NlB W. But what were the additional letters
| and figures for ? We had another billy of strong
1 old military ditto, and Martha was musing.
“ Blessed if I know what possessed him to camp
under the red bank ! ” “ What red bank ? ” said I.
Then I learned that the precipice from the road
to the river was always called the red bank. They
say—at least, Valverde, of Albury, says—there’s
truth in wine. There’s more: there’s imagina-
tion, invention, and intellect. The visions I have
j seen in wine would fill a milky way of Royal
! Academies. And Vast Heaving had been a sailor
and a surveyor.
| Next: Were they yards or feet? It did not
take long to decide. Twenty-two feet did not
1 come opposite the bank. Twenty-two yards
brought me to where, eighteen steps off, the cliff
was red and solid, except where the strata were
separated by a layer of grey sand. The river had
never in the memory of man risen to that level,
but the debris raised a ramp to within a foot of it.
(Read Poe’s “ Gold Beetle,” and various others,
and finish my story of treasure trove.) Vasty’s
dilly-bag was there.
The rise was only six quid : but that’s a lot for
a man who has nothing but a swag and some lines,
aided by the Rat and half a pint of “genuine
Chateau Howlong Burgundy.”
That, mates and co-mates, was my last rise, and,
let me tell you, one hundred and twenty bottles
of Murray Valley red wine cause elevation.
They make a man King of Anywhere, and half of
them would make Mademoiselle Rattonski Queen
of the South, chucking in the other three Cardinal
Points.
Thus I, thankful for memory and forgetfulness,
wait for a Rise in the River and drink to
“Vast Heaving ! ” Belay !
SCOTTY THE WBINKLER.
“ Prison doctors have no knowledge of
mental disease of any kind. They are as a class
ignorant of men. The pathology of the mind is
unknown to them. When a man grows insane
they treat him as shamming. They have him
punished again and again. Naturally the man
becomes worse. When ordinary punishments are
exhausted the doctor reports the case to the
j ustices. The result is flogging. ” — (Oscar W ilde,
writing from, personal observation in gaol.)

A grandmother at 26. Philadelphia
Polyclinic reports the case of a girl who had an
illegitimate daughter a month before her 13th
birthday. This casual offspring got married at
12 years old, and in 11 months had a son. She is
in excellent health, does not look in any way pre-
maturely aged, and grew 4in. after the birth of
that son.
Dear Bulletin, —There should be splendid
openings on N.S.W. country papers for unlucky
shearers who can’t get work at the sheds. Shears
are much quicker than scissors, and a “gun”
shearer would much facilitate the weary, sore-
wristed country-editor’s work. In the hands of a
ringer,’’ a pair of shears could clip a whole
column in two snips. I know a country print
where the comps, (three or four “rats,” i,e., wage-
apprentices) set MS. only about once a year,
and that s when some wandering mulga poet visits
that particular town, and occasionally hands in a
little thimr of his own.” The rest is all reprint,
ri X i f*'®* I n my mind’s eye, I see a couple
ot burly shearer-men ride up to the office of some
Ltldya Skiteb, and scare the wits out of editor
and staff by asking, in a voice of thunder, whether
there s a chance of ‘ a cut.’ ”

A Bulletin correspondent whites from a
Maoriland town :
The local kirk has been without a “ meenister” for the
last .year. From time to time, in accordance with ancient
custom, the powers that be have sent along various
samples of divinity to show their paces and battle for the
bawbees. The other week, a new aspirant ruined his
chance in one act. .lie was a toll, rawboned Iliuhlander,
and started his discourse with : “Mv freens, a great
mony.things have been runnin’ thro’ tiiy heid a’day”—
at the same time passing a leg-of-mutton fist through his
stubby hair. After that, they refused to take him
seriously, and, although he pounded the cushion and
yelled in the orthodox manner, he finished amidst sup-
pressed, merriment.
“The above,” continues The Bulletin’s corres-
pondent, “actually happened.” This paper
.always suspects yarns that “ actually happened,”
and believes that the present story is a chestnut
originally made up by some malignant Irishman
or humorous Jew. Anyhow, no ordinary congre-
gation would have seen anything humorous in a
freckled shepherd, with ginger hair, earnestly
talking aboot the things that were runnin’through
his heid. • "

A great part of Victoria is divided up into
tuppenny-ha’penny, milk-walk-size shires, each
with its own secretary and engineer and coun-
cillors and loan and overdraft —especially over-
draft. The law forbids the expenditure by muni-
cipalities of loan-money on maintenance and
repairs, and one shire which has lately floated a
new loan has hit on an original way of circum-
venting the Act. The usual way to repair a road
is to plant blue-metal by the roadside, break it up
and spread where required. But this Council has
carefully dumped huge boulders on the main road
in casual patches, blocked the scene of operations
by felling trees across the road, then started men
to break the metal and spread it over the area
blocked. This is not maintenance or repairs—it
is the new formation of a road that has been in
use for years.

The “ Tuyfelshoot. ”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was at Tan Boon’s, in Sourabaya, and
Che lemon - tinted countenance of Tan himself
was as the rising sun for satisfaction that his
nimble satellites were none too nimble for the
occasion. The air was thick with tobacco-smoke,
liquor-fumes, and talk ; which talk—being ehiefly
nautical —couldn’t fail, sooner or later, to turn
upon the “ Tuyfeisboot.” Everybody there had
heard of her ; nobody had seen her.
“ Ver’ good reason vy ! ” said the barrelly little
padrone of the Stella di Genova. “ Noboddoe
leeve ver’ long aftair see ’er.”
Old Sandy Macallan snorted cigar - smoke
through his long nose, and cocked his single eye
upon the Italian skipper. “The Laitin nations,”
he said, “ are laimentably supersteetious. ” He
took a solemn drink, and looked round with the
importance proper to the commander of four
masts and 3000 tons. “Though I must admeet
that this rideeclous notion came first from a Teu-
tonic peopled This Deevil’s-Boat, in fac’, is neither
tnair nor less than the auld Eleein’ Dutchman —
sairved up wi’ a wee bit oriaintal imaigination ! ”
“ Divilish little imagination about a Scotch-
man, anyhow,” said the Irish mate of a Bristol
“ tramp.”
“Ye’re very right, mon!” Macallan faced
round upon the red-headed blasphemer. “ We
haven’t imaigination enough, at all events, to
keep skirlin’ to a’ the world aboot the noble
speerit o’ a country that’s been no better, for sax
centuries, than a football for the mutton-headed
Englishman ! ”
Ireland instantly hove its tumbler at Scotland—
whereupon the Thistle landed the Shamrock in the
eye—then more Hibernia joined in, and more
Caledonia —and for ten minutes there was Arma-
geddon !
“ And I maintain,’' resumed the pertina-
cious Macallan, while the prompt Tan Boon was
collecting from the combatants the price of his
broken glasses —“I assairt, with regaird to this
Java Sea appareetion, that no indiveedual here
has ever as much as spoken to onybody that would
say he had seen onything o’ the sort ! ”
“Haul your wind a bit, there, Sandy!” A
short, man came across the room—square and
grizzled, and teak-faced, but most unnautically
frock-coated and tall-hatted. “ I’m a man of my
word, I suppose ? ”
“ Ye are, Tom ! ” graciously assented the Mac-
allan—“ Ye are that! Caiptain Thomas Waig-
staff, gentlemen ! —to those who don’t know him
already. The maister o’ the Bamborough Castle,
an’ as good a sailor as ever looked through a taile-
seope!”
The short skipper lighted a short pipe, drained
a long drink, and observed that his sailoring was
neither here nor there.
“ But, about this Tuyfeisboot, now !—what’s the
usual yarn about her ? ”
“ Only some few of a ship’s crew are able to see
her ! ” said one.
“And they don’t live .till the end of the voyage! ”
added another.
“ Just so,” said Wagstaff. “ And the ship her-
self will have the worst of luck ! —isn’t that it ?
Well, now—here’s a plain story from a plain man
- —a yarn I couldn’t invent, as Malcolm there will
tell you, to save my life ! You remember
Cassandra salvage case, Sandy ? ”
“ Ay, mon ! You were first-mate o’ her, I recol-
lec’! ”
“ I was. We brought salt and railway-iron out
to Calcutta, that trip. Splendid voyage ! —never
started a rope-yarn all the way ! Ship always had
been a rare lucky one, in spite of her name, which
I’m told wasn’t the best in the world to sail under.
Well, the Calcutta agents ordered us on here for
sugar, and again we had a first-class run and speedy
despatch.
* * * *
“ First day out, on the run back, we had a smart
easterly breeze up to the first dog-watch, when it
fell away, and a bank got up over the land that
made the skipper think we were going to have a
stiff blow from the wrong quarter Glass kept
steady, though, and the old man had gone below
for tea, without ordering anything to be taken off
her, when the bo’s’n comes up to me, and says he,
‘What d’ye make of them chaps, sir?’ ‘What
chaps ? ’ says I. ‘ There—in that prahu—wavin’
their spears at us,’and the man pointed away over
the port bow. ‘How far away?’ I asked, for I
could see nothing ; in fact, there wasn’t a sail in
sight all round. Hoskins stared at me, and pointed
again. ‘ Why , there, sir !—not two cables’-lengths
away!’ ‘What’s she like; in Heaven’s name?’
I said, beginning to think he was off his head, and
I suppose he thought ditto of me, for he kept
giving me a side-glance every now and then as he
told me that she was a big black prahu, with sheer-
leg masts, and a kind of hurricane-deck, and
things like feathers streaming from her stem and
stern-posts. ‘ And the men ? ’ I asked, for this
was a description of the fighting prahu of the old
pirate days. ‘Full of ’em, sir—below ; but there’s
only one of them on the platform affair, and he—
but what’s the use of talkin’, sir ! ’ he broke off,
‘ when you must see her as plain as I do ! ’ I said
nothing for a minute, for all this agreed exactly
with the account I had many a time heard of the
‘ Tuyfeisboot.’ ‘ Ever make the Sourabaya voyage
before, Hoskins ? ’ I said then. The man glowered
at me—sure, now, that I had gone a bit cranky.
‘No, sir ! it’s my first trip any where’s out of Cal-
cutta.’ ‘Well, look here, my man; I can see
nothing of all this you talk about, so it’s plain
something has carried away aloft with one or other
of us ! I don’t think it’s with me, Hoskins ; so I
think you’d best get away forrard again, and say
nothing about it.’
♦ * * *
He turned away without another word, but as
I looked after him I saw a lot of the hands
gathered about the fore-riggin’, staring away over
the water, and one or two shading their eyes, as if
to see better. One fellow —half-a-dozen rattlins
up—nearly fell overboard when I shouted at him
to come out of that! ‘ What were they gaping at,
like a parcel of old women ? ’ They looked foolish
for a bit, and then a big old Irishman came aft to
me. ‘ It’s the Tuyfeisboot, sir ! ’ says he in a half-
whisper. ‘The Dago says he can see her as plain
as the fingers on his hand, but divil a one else of
us can make out a plank of anythin’ that floats !
Ye don’t see her yourself, do ye, sir ? ’ ‘ See her ?
—of course not! There’s nothing of the sort to
see. I’m surprised at you, Muldoon—a man of
your age taking any notice of such tommy-rot!
Here—Spirito ! —I want you!’ Muldoon slunk
away forrard as the Dago—a Greek he was —came
aft. A skinny little rascal —yellower than that
scoundrel, Tan Boon there, and fairly stinking of
the Eastern seas. If he’d spoken first of this
cursed thing and Hoskins had followed in his
wake, I’d hardly have given the matter a second
thought ; but, you see, the bo’s’n had described it
first, and he’d never heard of the Tuyfeisboot.
“ Well, to cut it short, the Greek was just as
positive as t’other chap, with the difference that
he was badly frightened over the business. Ho
wouldn’t see the end of the voyage, he said—and
neither would the bo’s’n ! And the ship would
never have any more luck!. ‘Look here, my
friend!’ I-told him, ‘if you want to spend the
rest of the trip in the lazarette, with a pair of
clinkers on your shanks, just you go and put that
notion about among the men ! It’s all cursed
rubbish !—so get away forrard and keep your
mouth shut! ’ I suppose I ought to have laughed
at the whole business, but there’s things you can’t
get over, and the old man didn’t like it a bit
more’n I did, because, he said, it was the sort of
thing that sometimes worked its own fulfilment.
‘ The sailor-man,’ he says, ‘ is a curious mixture
of courage and cowardice. If this thing gets to be
talked-over in the fo’c’s’le the men’ll lose heart, and
to lose heart means the doubling of every danger ! ’
“In which, gentlemen,” commented Captain
Wagstaff, between gulps of another whistle-
wetter, “ every sailor here will agree with one of
the wisest men that ever handled a sextant! ”
* * * *
“Of course, the thing did get turned over and
over among the crew, and from that out there was
a cloud over the old Cassandra. Splendid weather
we had after we got through the Straits —wind
mostly on the quarter, and t’gallant-stu’nsails
right up into the Bay of Bengal. But the men
went about their work like fellows waiting for
something. And one day—when we had made
our latitude about 13 —they got it! About the
middle of the forenoon watch it freshened up so
that we had to take the kites off her, and she
dipped the chap that was stowin’ theflyin’-jib into
an extra big sea—and left him there. Happened
many a time, you’ll say. It has ; but ” —Wagstaff
put down his pipe and circled the company with
his eyes—“ that man was Spirito the Dago ! And
he never came up; or, if he did, we didn’t see
him.”
“ Didna ye heave her to ? ” Macallan asked.
“We did. Risky job for our foremast, with
what we were carrying. And we got a boat out.
But, as I say, we never saw a hair of the poor
devil.”
Gloom fell upon the hearers. Nearly every
man there had witnessed, in his time, that hope-
less search amongst the hills and valleys of the
water-wilderness. Tan Boon’s mellifluous English
broke the spell. It was “ welly neal time shuttee
up ! ”
“Shut-up, yourself, you yellow rascal!” said
Wagstaff—“ and let’s have drinks all round. That
was the beginning,” he went on. “ One little
mishap after another, till we got to the Sandheads
with a falling glass and every sign of heavy
weather. Brought-up off the Eastern Channel
lightship, and lay at two anchors for three days—
blowing till we rolled the inizzen-topmast out of
us and broke the upper maintopsail-yard in the
slings. Then one cable parted, but, while we were
waiting for the other to go, a big ocean-steamer
came fighting in through the smother, and we
signalled her for help. Turned out to be a P.
and O. —the Thibet—and she got a hawser to us
just as the other cable parted. But before she’d
towed us ten minutes the hawser went, too, and
she signalled that she’d take us off, if we liked.
And —what with cargo shifted, pumps and boats
smashed,’ and wreckage pounding our sides—we
had to come to it.
“ And I’ll never run down Lascars again, for it
was a Lascar crew that took us off—three trips in
such a sea as I never expect to see again. Couldn’t,
come alongside, of course, so we had to jump for
it, and be picked up.”
* • * *
, “ Ay, ay, mon ! ” said Macallan, lumbering to
his legs and buttoning his pea-jacket. “ But
you’re wanderin’ a wee. What’s all this to do wi'
the Tuyfeisboot ? ”
“ This ! ” and Wagstaff got his anchor up in
turn. “ How many men did we lose in getting
away from the ship ? ” . ,
“ Let’s see ! Only one, as weel’s I remember.”
“Right, one man only. Now’’—Wagstaff
looked slowly round him at the listeners—“ why,
when the steamer’s boat could be kept clear of the
side while everybody else vvas picked up—why, I
say, should a sudden drive of the sea send her in
to crush that particular man like an egg-shell ? ”
He walked to the door, then turned to answer his
own question.
“ Because, gentlemen, that man was Hoskins,
the bo’s’n. He had seen the Tuyfeisboot ! ”
ALEX, MONTGOMERY.


GOULD’S TINY TONIC LIVER PILLS
CURE
A Musician’s Cure in Netvcastle.
AFTER DOCTOR FAILED.
ANOTHER TRIUMPH FOR GOULD’S.
A remarkable case (says a Newcastle paper) was
brought under our notice a few days ago of a lead-
ing musician in Newcastle, who, through constant
strain of teaching and conducting the Victoria
Theatre orchestra, was allowing his system to get
so low that he would eventually have had to give
up his profession. The gentleman in question —
Mr. W. D. Barry—is conductor of Victoria Theatre
orchestra, and general leader and conductor of
most of the first musical organisations of New-
castle, and resides at 4 Noster-place, off Wolfe-
street. Mr. Barry is 33 years of age, and a highly-
respected resident; born in Newcastle. Inter-
viewed by a reporter a few days ago, he said he
had been a great sufferer from indigestion,
stomach and liver troubles for a long time, and
had been under some of the best doctors without
receiving any benefit,
“How long since did you first commence to
suffer badly, Mr. Barry? ” inquired the reporter..
“ Oh, it was about 12 months ago, and at that
time I moved from late residence on the hill —
which was healthily situated—to where lam now,
and, being so closely confined to teaching, &c., I
got very little exercise, and hence the results. My
digestion got very bad, and my liver and stomach
got out of order. I would have an aching pain in
my back and right side—a kind of stinging sensa-
tion—and at night my legs would get numb. The
circulation in my fingers got very bad, and
I suffered with a dry cough. I would have it that
it was lung trouble, until the doctor told me it
was my liver. I could not sleep. I would go to
bed, and after a few hours I would get restless,
and would have to get up and walk about; so you
can judge that I did not feel very refreshed in the
morning. Again, a cold, clammy chill would
come over me. My digestion continued to get
worse and worse, and after my meals I would feel
as if my clothes were too tight across my chest,
and I felt as if a board was pressing down on it.
At times I would get a shooting pain in the left
shoulder, and a pain up my left side, which would
extend to my ear, and a beating sensation would
take place. Well, I continued this \yay for
nearly twelve months, and was under doctors,
but got no permanent relief until I took Gould’s
Tiny Tonic Liver Pills, which I saw advertised,
and readingof the benefits other people had derived
from their use I decided to give them a trial; so I
bought a box from Hart and Firkin, the chemists
in Hunter-street, and taking the pills I found they
did me a great deal of good. Continuing with
them, the pains in my back and side left me, I
slept well, my appetite returned, and I am not
subject to those distressing pains after my meals.
In fact, I feel better in every way. ’
“ Has it been long since you first commenced
taking the pills, Mr. Barry?”
“ I commenced about three months ago, and I
followed out the directions very closely, and took
as much exercise as possible, which facilitated the
working of the pills. ”
“ You know, Mr. Barry, it is our intention to
publish this interview.”
“ Well, sir, you are at liberty to publish my
statement. Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills have
done me good, and I can thorougly recommend
them to anyone suffering as I did.”

Constipation More Terrible than the
Black Blague.
Centuries ago people used to fear what they
called the pestilence. “ Black Death ” was the
most terrible thing in the world to them. They
feared it as people now fear the Cholera and
Yellow Fever. And yet there is a more terrible
thing than any of these. There is a thing that
causes more misery and more deaths than any of
these. It is the commonest trouble that human
beings suffer from. It is so common that nine-
tenths of all the sickness in the world is traceable
to it. It is merely that simple, common thing—
constipation. There is no telling what it may
lead to, but it is sure to lead to something bad.
Its immediate effects are unpleasant in the ex-
treme. It makes people listless, gives them head-
aches, makes the digestion poor, causes dizziness,
loss of appetite, loss of sleep, foul breath, and dis-
tress after eating. All this merely because Nature
has been imposed upon, and has been refused the
little help she needed.
This little help is furnished by Gould’s Tiny
Tonic Liver Pills. You take them until you get
yourself into perfect working order, and then you
stop. You don’t have to take any more Pills
unless after a while you do something that de-
ranges your digestive system again. We are not
guessing about these facts, and we don’t ask you
to take our unsupported word for it. Doctors
have found that they are true, and have testified
to them. Let the druggist who says that some-
thing else is “ just as good ” sell that something
else to somebody else. Don’t let him trifle with
your health for the sake of a little more profit to
himself.

A Tale of Twenty Men.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sailor from the salty South —and such as sailors
be —
A sailor from the briny North, they met upon the
quay.
“Ho! messmate there, what cheer? What ho !
here be two comrades met,
From ramping wind and roaring sea, both God-
delivered yet.”
“ But where’s the news, and how’s the news, and
what’s the last from hell ?
W ith beer between and pipe between, what tales
be there to tell ?
From here and there ; from everywhere that ships
and sailors go,
What things of Love, and Drink, and Death, and
fellowship dost know ?
“ How look the girls of Liverpool, the belles of New
Orleans ?
How tastes the taste of British junk or Boston
pork-and-beans ?
Is fever rife at Rio yet ? What boats be outward-
bound ?
Has any beggar got a berth? Is any beggar
drowned ? ”
A lassie waits by Plymouth Pier ; another at Kin-
sale,
And I must leave my missus here, God’s glory !
when I sail;
But pork and beans is beans and pork, and junk
is junk alway,
And our old tub she turned keel up, last year off
Table Bay !
She’d tumbled round the north and south, she’d
wallowed east and west;
She’d been a clipper in her day and paced it with
the best;
Her staunch oak ribs have stood the brunt of
North Atlantic seas,
When oft and oft the Devil sat aloft on her cross-
trees.
We fetched her out across the world and brought
her home to berth;
We took her in, we took her out, to ev’ry port on
earth ;
We dragged her round the frozen Horn, we slung
her through the Strait;
We slewed her at the Hobs of Hell and never
scratched a plate.
We drove her down to Beelzebub, as drunk as
devil’s swine ;
Then brought her up below the Pit and hitched
her with a twine,
A tow-line’s length from Kingdom Come, amidst
the hiss and roar,
We turned about, all sober men, and coaxed her
off the shore.
We’ve seen her flaunt her figure-head at zenith in
her climb,
Then drop her bowsprit in the trough and point it
at the slime
One hundred fathoms underneath, where should
have been her keel,
With two tied sailor-men, by God ! half-fainting
at her wheel.
She’s wallowed through the inky night, with never
star to guide,
She’s seen the sea-fires glow and burn and heard
the surges glide;
She’s seen the black and lonely waste; she’s seen
the waste sun lit;
And dipped to greet the ruddy Day—and rose to
welcome it.
We’ve watched the day-god drop athwart the
distant sea-line dim,
And witli the moonlight in our wake, due West
we’ve followed him.
We’ve heard and seen, and seen and heard! Strange
tales have we to tell,
Who’ve looked five oceans in the face and learned
to know them well.
But as she lay by Plymouth town the rats went
down her side
And when he saw, with what he knew, her skipper
cursed and cried,
But when her owners heard his tale they laid in-
surance by
And filled her up from hold to hatch and sent her
out to die.
With twenty men steeped to their lips in all the
sins of men;
With twenty men cooped up like cats not know-
ing where nor when ;
With twenty men to leave their bones, their loves,
their hopes, and fears,
Their rights and wrongs and all the rest, betwixt
two hemispheres!
They kissed their sins ashore good-bye, and drank
their sins farewell,
Then towed her down from Plymouth town and
turned her out towards hell,
And when she kicked her heels again on hell’s own
broad highway
They lurched aloft to watch the world and give
their sins “Good-day.”
They dragged her down along the track, a God-
forsaken crew,
And ev’ry knot she made they laughed, but longed
to count it two !
With sweeping seas from stem to stern, with rotten
ropes and gear,
With mouldy tack for sustenance and brine
instead of beer;
With twenty sinners keeping watch ; at graft by
night and day,
With ten to man the pumps and curse, with ten
maybe to pray;
With two of twenty swept astern and sent to fight
■ their death,
To meet the Horror in the dark that choked them
breath by breath !
They fell about her slimy deck; they clung to what
they could ;
Amidst the crash of falling spars, the wrack of
riven wood.
They drowned like rats —that would not drown
for one-and-six a day ;
They died to swell a bank-account that night off
Table Bay.
Aye, twenty things of bloated shape that sought a
resting place,
And three ship-owners shaking hands by God s
own holy grace;
Aye, twenty things of clammy kind that, very
shortly, stank,
And three well-scented Englishmen with money
in the bank.
A sailor from the salty South —and such as sailors
be—
A seaman from the briny North come out across
the sea,
With beer between and pipe between they met,
the tale to tell
Of twenty men, all sailor-men, twice damned and
sent to hell.
E. J. BRADY.
The Courting of Te Rahui.
(AS TOLD BY PIRIMONA.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Te Rahui lived at Orakei Korako, on the
Waikato River —if the place had a shorter name I
would tell you pakehas of it—there she lived.”
Pirimona paused, and looked into the missionary-
tohunga's face, as if asking whether he should
go on.
The pakeha wished to hear.
“ Rehu came from Matata, Tarata from Whan-
ganui. Both had heard of the great beauty of Te
Rahui. This thing happened before there were
any pakehas in this country of Aotea.
“ One day Rahui went to bathe in the Mirror
Cave, where the hot-spring bubbles up under the
dome of colored clays. The roof is reflected in
the water below —you may see this as you stand at
the mouth of the cave. There Te Rahui bathed,
and when she wished to rest she climbed on to the
ledge of rock on the further side, and lay down
clothed only by the rising steam.
“ Rehu had watched the rangatira girl cross the
river in her canoe. He followed her, swimming,
though the rapids were a hundred yards below the
landing-place on the further side. We all think
Rehu was a brave man to swim the Waikato at
that point.
“ He followed Rahui through the manuka scrub
which fringes the puias by the river-side. These
he passed by, keeping the girl in sight, and came
to another belt of manuka. Here Rehu hides, and
watches Rahui cross the boiling, hissing mud-holes
beyond. Rehu sees her reach firm ground and
enter the side of the hill. Rahui enters the Cave
of Reflecting Waters.
“Then Rehu crosses by the path that Rahui
has chosen. A false step and he will be boiled
alive ; but he walks in the footsteps of Rahui, and
is safe.
“From the opening of the cave Rehu looks
down on the girl as she bathes. He sees the
colors of the roof reflected in the clear water —
blue, green, red, and purple ; he watches the
rangatira maiden till she has finished bathing and
swims two strokes and grasps the ledge of rock,
and clambers up, and there lies resting.
“ Then Rehu walks quietly down the slope to
the water’s edge. He sits and speaks to Rahui.
But Rahui turns her face away ; she vvill not look
at him ; she will not answer his questions.
“ Then Rehu steps in the water, and with two
strokes he swims across the bath. His hand is on
the ledge where Rahui lies—but she looses his
hold. Rehu falls back into the water: he sinks.
When he rises again, he grasps the ledge once
more, to clamber up beside Rahui. Again he is
pushed back into the water. This happens fully
twelve times. Each time that Rehu rises he tries
to grasp the ledge of rock, or else Rahui herself.
But his breath comes in short gasps j he goes
under for the last time; he does not rise to the
“ Then Rahui remembers —the waters of the
Mirror Bath overcome those who are not used to
them. She cries out, frightened, and plunges
after Rehu ; she drags him out. Upon the white,
encrusted ground she lays him down, and rests his
head in her lap. His eyes are half-closed ;he does
not move. Rahui weeps over him—she thinks his
life will not come back—she believes his soul has
gone to the spirit-world.
“ Tenderly she places him on her korowai cloak,
and drags him over the rough ground to the outer
air. There she renews her efforts to restore Rehu
to life. He opens his eyes. Rahui gives a cry of
joy. Rehu takes both her hands in his ; then
presently he clasps his arms around her. Rahui
is won.
• * * *
“They hear a shout above them. There
stands Tarata. He has come to the cave by
scaling the hill from behind. In his hand he
flourishes his taiaha.
“ Rahui runs and hides herself in the cave. As
Tarata clambers down the hill-side, he shouts
defiance at Rehu. Rehu stands silent. He has
no hatred of Tarata in his heart; he thinks only
of Rahui ; the smile with which he had last looked
at her is still on his face. Tarata is mad
with love for Rahui. He rushes upon Rehu.
Rehu is unarmed —he turns and runs. Across
the boiling mud-holes, between the hissing
puias Rehu rushes. Tarata follows. Rehu is a
slim-built man, a swift runner ; Tarata is heavy,
slow. Rehu outruns Tarata. He reaches the
manuka on the further side whilst Tarata is but
half-way across the mud-holes. Rehu turns and
taunts Tarata. Tarata stops to answer Rehu ; his
foot pauses upon the treacherous earth ; he loses
his balance; he falls; he sinks down; he. is en-
gulphed by the smoking under-world. He is seen
no more.
“ Then Rehu returns by the way that Rahui
had shown him ; he leads her out of the cave ; he
shows her the spot where Tarata fell. Where
Tarata fell bubbles rise up long after Rehu and
Rahui have passed by, on their way back to
Orakei Korako. ”
The pakeha said he wished he had not heard.
Nelson, M.L.
